Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Definition Requires Representation, Not Unique Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the intellect conceives unknown things through representation and analogy, not unique attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of definition by unique attributes and explain why he argues for representation and analogy as the true method of concept formation.

Core Idea
The unique attribute (differentia) is circular unless you already know the thing it is supposed to define.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the Aristotelian/logician's claim that a thing can be defined by a unique combination of genus and differentia. He argues that if you do not already know the thing, the differentia is meaningless because it presupposes the thing. Instead, the mind forms concepts through representation (tamthīl) and analogy (tashbīh) based on shared common aspects. This passage directly critiques the core of the logicians' definitional theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two epistemic cases
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between one who already has a conception of the defined thing and one who does not. For the first, definition serves only as a reminder; for the second, it is impossible to give a single unique attribute.
2
Trace the circularity argument
If you try to define an unknown thing by a unique attribute (e.g., differentia or property), that attribute itself requires knowledge of the thing. You cannot understand 'neighing' without knowing the horse, so defining horse as 'neighing animal' is circular.
The attribute specific to the defined has no independent existence.
3
Examine the animal sound examples
Ibn Taymiyya lists horses, donkeys, camels, oxen, sheep, and gazelles, each with a distinctive sound. Yet these sounds do not define the animals for someone unfamiliar with them because the sound is only meaningful if the animal is already known.
Trying to define 'neighing' as 'the sound of a donkey' leads to a cycle where both terms are undefined.
4
Extract the alternative method
For those lacking prior conception, definition must rely on representation (tamthīl) and analogy (tashbīh). The mind abstracts common aspects (qadr mushtarak) from known instances and combines them, e.g., 'intoxicating drink' shares the common aspect of intoxication with other inebriants.
The common aspect is not a differentia or property but a shared feature that can be combined to specify.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that definition of a thing is only by defining its concrete instance (ta'rīf al-'ayn) or by mentioning something similar (dhikr mā yushbihuhu).
This directly opposes the logicians' claim that definition by genus and differentia is sufficient for all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : العقل يتصور بـ «التمثيل» و«التشبيه». والمقصود أنه مَنْ تصوّر المحدود بنفسه فلا بد أن يتصور ما يختصه ويميّزه عن غيره. وهذا لا يحتاج في تصوره إلى حد ولكن يترجم له الاسم الدال عليه ويميّز له المسمى عن غيره لكن الحد يكون منبهاً له على الحقيقة كما ينبهه الاسم إذا كان عارفاً بمسما . وأما من لم يكن متصوّراً له فلا يمكن أن يُذكر له صفة واحدة تختص بالمحدود، فلا يمكن تعريفه إياه لا بـ «فصل» ولا بـ «خاصة سواء ذكر «الجنس» و «العرض العام» أو لم يذكر ، لأن الصفة التي تختص المحدود لا تتصور بدون تصور المحدود، إذ لا وجود لها بدونه. والعقل إنما يجرد «الكليات» إذا تصوَّرَ بعض «جزئياتها». فمن لم يتصور الشيء الموجود كيف يتصور جنسه ونوعه؟ ولكن يتصور بـ «التمثيل» و «التشبيه». ويتصور القدر المشترك بين تلك الصفة الخاصة وبين نظيرها من الصفات و القدر المشترك ليس هو «القدرُ المشترك» ليس هو «فصلا» ولا «خاصة» ولكن قد ينتظم من قدر مشترك و قدر مشترك ما يخص المحدود، كما في قولك شراب مسکر. وعلى هذا، فإذا قيل في «الفَرَس»: إنه حيوان صاهل»، وفي «الحمار»: إنه حيوان ناهق»، وفي البعير) : إنه حيوان راغ»، وفي «الثور»: إنه حيوان خائر»، وفي المحدود «الشاة»: إنها حيوان ثاغ»، وفي الظبي»: إنه حيوان باغم وأمثال ذلك، فهذه الخواص الأصوات مختصة بهذه الأنواع، لكن لا تفيد تعريف هذه الحيوانات لمن لم يكن عارفاً بها. فمن لم يعرف الفرس لا يعرف الصهيل»، ومن لم يعرف «الحمار» لا يعرف «النهيق»، أعني لا يعرف معناه وإن سمع اللفظ، فإذا أريد تعريف «النهيق» قيل له : هو صوت الحمار»، فيلزم الدَّوْرَ ، إذ يكون قد عرف الحمار» بـ «النهيق»، و «النهيق» بـ «الحمار». وهكذا سائر الخواص المميّزة للمحدود - لا يعرف بها المحدود لمن لم يكن عرفه . فتبين أن تعريف الشيء إنما هو بتعريف عينه، أو بذكر ما يشبهه. فمن عرف عين الشيء لا يفتقر في معرفته إلى حد ومن لم يعرفه فإنما يعرف به إذا عرف ما يشبهه.
Second station - fourth aspect: The intellect conceives through 'representation' and 'analogy'. The point is that whoever conceives the defined thing by itself must conceive what is specific to it and distinguishes it from others. This person does not need a definition for his conception, but rather a translation of the name indicating it and a distinction of the named from others, but the definition serves as a reminder of the reality just as the name reminds him if he knows its referent. As for one who does not have a conception of it, it is impossible to mention a single attribute specific to the defined, so it is impossible to define it to him by a 'differentia' or a 'property', whether 'genus' and 'common accident' are mentioned or not, because the attribute specific to the defined cannot be conceived without conception of the defined, since it has no existence without it. The intellect only abstracts 'universals' when it has conceived some of their 'particulars'. So if one has not conceived the existing thing, how can he conceive its genus and species? Rather, he conceives through 'representation' and 'analogy'. He conceives the common aspect between that specific attribute and its counterpart from attributes, and the common aspect is not a 'differentia' nor a 'property', but from a common aspect and another common aspect something that specifies the defined may be composed, as in your saying 'an intoxicating drink'. Based on this, if it is said of a 'horse': it is a neighing animal, and of a 'donkey': it is a braying animal, and of a 'camel': it is a grunting animal, and of an 'ox': it is a lowing animal, and of a 'sheep': it is a bleating animal, and of a 'gazelle': it is a belling animal, and similar examples, these specific attributes of sounds are specific to these species, but they do not benefit the definition of these animals for one who is not acquainted with them. For one who does not know the horse does not know 'neighing', and one who does not know the donkey does not know 'braying'—I mean he does not know its meaning even if he hears the word. So if one wanted to define 'neighing', it would be said to him: it is the sound of a donkey, leading to circularity, since he would have known the donkey through 'braying' and 'braying' through the donkey. And similarly for all the distinguishing properties of the defined—they do not define the defined for one who does not know it. So it becomes clear that the definition of a thing is only by defining its concrete instance, or by mentioning what resembles it. Thus, whoever knows the concrete instance of a thing does not need a definition for his knowledge, and whoever does not know it only knows it if he knows what resembles it.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about how the mind forms concepts (tasawwur). Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two cases: one who already has a conception of the defined thing and one who does not. For the first, a definition serves only as a reminder or a translation of the name, not as a source of new knowledge. For the second, it is impossible to give a single specific attribute (whether differentia or property) because that attribute itself requires prior conception of the thing. The mind abstracts universals only from particulars; thus, one must first conceive a particular before understanding the universal. Those who lack conception must rely on representation (tamthīl) and analogy (tashbīh). They conceive a 'common aspect' (qadr mushtarak) shared between the specific attribute and another similar attribute. By combining common aspects, a specific characterization can be achieved, as in defining wine as 'an intoxicating drink'—the common aspect of 'intoxicating' is shared by various substances. He then illustrates with animal sounds: defining a horse as 'a neighing animal' is circular if the hearer does not already know the horse, because 'neighing' cannot be understood without knowing the horse. Hence, definition either points to the actual thing (bi ta'rīf 'aynihi) or mentions something similar (bi dhikr mā yushbihuhu). The conclusion: the logical theory of definition fails for those unfamiliar with the subject.

Key Terms
التمثيل
التشبيه
القدر المشترك
الدور

A) They will understand the essence of horse.
B) They will know it is an animal that neighs, but may confuse it with a donkey.
C) They cannot know the meaning of 'neighing' because it depends on knowing the horse, causing circularity.
D) They will learn the definition perfectly.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that definition by unique attributes is circular; true concept formation relies on representation and analogy with known instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take Ibn Taymiyya's example of 'horse' and 'neighing'. Write a brief dialogue explaining why defining 'horse' as 'neighing animal' fails for someone who has never seen or heard of a horse.
?
Give an Islamic legal example: suppose you want to define 'salah' (prayer) for someone who has never seen it. Explain how you would use tamthīl (representation) or tashbīh (analogy) rather than a technical definition.

Why Definition Varies with the Questioner
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' assumption that definition gives a unique answer to 'what is it?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different possible demands of a questioner asking 'What is it?' and explain why a single definitional formula is insufficient in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The answer to 'What is it?' depends on the questioner's knowledge and goal, not on a fixed essence.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that the question 'What is it?' ('ما هو؟') has a one-size-fits-all answer in the form of a definition (hadd). He shows that the answer must adapt to what the questioner already knows and what he or she truly seeks. This insight has profound implications for how we use definitions in Islamic sciences such as fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two cases
Identify the two types of questioner: one who does not know the named object (المسمى) at all, and one who already knows the named object and the name's indication. The first needs simple naming; the second needs something more.
2
List the possible extra demands
From the passage, enumerate what the knowledgeable questioner might seek: hidden specific properties, efficient cause (علة فاعلية), final cause (علة غائية), composition, or the true essence (الحقيقة) even if unknown or inexpressible.
Ibn Taymiyya gives the example of the soul (النفس) as something whose reality may be unknown or inexpressible.
3
Understand why a unique definition fails
Realize that the logicians' assumption—that the answer to 'What is it?' is always a definition composed of genus and differentia—cannot cover these varied demands. The answer may require only common attributes, only specific ones, or something else entirely.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider a student in usul al-fiqh asking 'What is qiyas?' The answer differs depending on whether the student knows the term 'qiyas' already or wants its underlying causes ('illah) or its components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كله إذا كان السائل القائل ما هو؟ غير عالم بـ«المسمى». وأما إن كان عالماً بـ«المسمى» ودلالة الاسم عليه فلا يحتاج إلى التمييز بين السائل العالم المسمى وغيره، ولا إلى تعريفه دلالة الاسم عليه، فيكون مطلوبه قدراً زائداً على التمييز بينه وبين غيره، وهذا هو الذي يجعلونه مطلوباً للسائل عن المحدود، وجوابه هو عندهم المقول في جواب ما هو؟ ومعلوم أن مطلوب هذا قد يكون ذكر خصائص له باطنة لم يطلع عليها، وقد يكون مطلوبه بيان علته الفاعلية أو الغائية، وقد يكون مطلوبه معرفة ما يتركب منه شيء آخر، وقد يكون مطلوبه معرفة حقيقته التي لا يعلمها المسئول، أو علمها ولا عبارة تدل السائل عليها كالسائل عن حقيقة النفس، وأمثال ذلك. وجواب مثل هذا لا يجب أن يحصل بذكر صفة مشتركة مع الصفات المختصة، بل قد لا يحصل إلا بذكر جميع المشتركات، وقد يحصل بذكر بعض المختصات، وقد يحصل بغير ذلك، بحسب غرض السائل ومقصوده.
All this is when the questioner saying 'What is it?' is unaware of the 'named'. But if he is aware of the 'named' and the indication of the name upon it, then he does not need distinction between the questioner who knows the named and other, nor does he need definition of the indication of the name upon it. So his demand is an extra amount beyond the distinction between it and other, and this is what they make as the demand of the questioner about the defined thing, and its answer for them is what is said in answer to 'What is it?'. And it is known that the demand of this [questioner] may be a mention of hidden specific properties he has not been informed of, or his demand may be an explanation of its efficient or final cause, or his demand may be knowledge of what a different thing is composed of, or his demand may be knowledge of its reality which the respondent does not know, or knows it but no expression indicates it to the questioner, like the questioner about the reality of the soul, and similar examples. And the answer to such a one does not have to be obtained by mentioning a common attribute along with specific ones, rather it may not be obtained except by mentioning all common attributes, and it may be obtained by mentioning some specific ones, and it may be obtained by other than that, according to the purpose and intention of the questioner.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' theory of definition (hadd). He argues that the logicians assume a universal answer to the question 'What is it?' regardless of the questioner's state, but in reality the answer varies. He distinguishes two cases: first, when the questioner does not even know what the name refers to, the definition must first clarify the named object. Second, when the questioner already knows the named object and the name's indication, then the definition needs to provide something beyond mere distinction. That extra demand could be: hidden essential properties, efficient or final causes, composition, or even the true essence which the respondent may not fully know. Ibn Taymiyya thus shows that the logicians' rigid framework fails to capture the diverse aims of real inquiry. He concludes that the answer may involve only common attributes, specific ones, or something completely different, depending on the questioner's purpose. This argument undermines the claim that definition (hadd) yields certain knowledge of simple essences.

Key Terms
المسمى
مطلوب
علة فاعلية
علة غائية

Key Takeaway
The answer to 'What is it?' must be adapted to the questioner's prior knowledge and specific informational need—no single definition fits all.

Use Ibn Taymiyya's categories (hidden properties, efficient cause, final cause, composition, essence) to list three possible demands and propose a suitable answer for each.

Ibn Taymiyya's Disjunctive Argument: Simple Concepts Cannot Be Sought
Ibn Taymiyya argues that simple concepts cannot be sought, so they cannot be obtained by definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's disjunctive argument against seeking simple concepts and apply it to evaluate whether a given concept can be obtained by definition.

Core Idea
You cannot seek a simple concept because you either already have it or you don't — and neither allows seeking.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers his fifth critique of the logicians' definition theory. He uses a disjunctive argument to show that simple concepts (tasawwur mufrad) cannot be the object of definition. This undermines the very purpose of definition in the Aristotelian tradition. We'll walk through his reasoning and then apply it to real examples from Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The logicians claim that definition (hadd) yields simple concepts (tasawwurat mufrada). Ibn Taymiyya rejects that such concepts can be sought at all.
2
Extract the disjunctive premise
Ibn Taymiyya presents a dichotomy: the mind is either aware (sha'ir) of the simple concept or not aware. There is no third option.
3
Analyze the first horn – awareness
If the mind is already aware of the concept, seeking it is impossible because it would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (tahsīl al-ḥāṣil). One can only seek continuity, repetition, or strength of awareness, not the concept itself.
4
Analyze the second horn – unawareness
If the mind has no awareness, seeking is impossible because all seeking and intention (talab wa qaṣd) are preceded by awareness (shu'ūr). You cannot seek what you do not know.
5
Respond to the objection
To the objection that people seek to conceive of angels, jinn, etc., Ibn Taymiyya replies that such seeking is not for a simple concept but for a complex: the thing plus its name. The seeker hears the name and wants to know the named, which involves a judgment (tasdiq) that the named is called by that name. This is a linguistic and propositional matter, not a simple conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن التصورات المفردة يمتنع أن تكون مطلوبة، فيمتنع أن تُطلب بالحد . وذلك لأن الذهن إما أن يكون شاعراً بها ، وإما أن لا يكون شاعراً بها . فإن كان شاعراً بها امتنع طلب المشعور وحصوله، لأن تحصيل الحاصل ممتنع ، وإنما قد يُطلب دوام الشعور وتكرره أو قوّتُه. وإن لم يكن شاعراً بها امتنع من النفس طلب ما لا تشعر به، فإن الطلب والقصد مسبوق بالشعور . فإن قيل : فالإنسان يطلب تصور الملك، والجنّ، والرُّوح، وأشياء كثيرة، وهو لا يشعر بها؟ قيل : هو قد سمع هذه الأسماء، فهو يطلب تصور مسماها، كما يطلب مَنْ يفهم معانيها تصور معانيها، سواء كانت المعاني متصورة له قبل ذلك أو لم تكن. وهو إذا تصوّر مسمى هذه الأسماء فلا بد أن يعلم أنها مسماة بهذا الاسم، إذ لو تصور حقيقة ولم يكن ذلك الاسم فيها لم يكن تَصَوَّرَ مطلوبه . فهنا المتصور ذات وأنها مسماة بكذا، من جنس ما يرى الثلج مَنْ لم يكن يعرفه ، فيراه ويعلم أن اسمه «الثلج» . وهذا ليس تصوراً للمعنى فقط، بل للمعنى ولاسمه وهذا لا ريب أنه يكون مطلوباً، ولكن هذا لا يوجب أن يكون المعنى المفرد مطلوباً، فإن المطلوب هنا تصديق، وفيه أمر لغوي . وأيضاً، فإن المطلوب هنا لا يحصل بمجرد الحد بل لا بد من تعريف المحدود بالإشارة إليه، أو غير ذلك مما لا يكتفي فيه بمجرد اللفظ . فإن قيل : جعلتم امتناع الطلب لازماً للنقيضين، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، وإذا عُدم اللازم عُدم الملزوم ؛ فإذا كان امتناع الطلب - وهو اللازم - معدوماً امتناع الطلد
Fifth: Simple concepts are impossible to be sought, so it is impossible that they be sought by definition. That is because the mind either is aware of them or is not aware of them. If it is aware of them, then seeking the sensed and obtaining it is impossible, because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible; rather what may be sought is the continuity of awareness, its repetition, or its strength. And if it is not aware of them, then it is impossible for the soul to seek what it is not aware of, because seeking and intending are preceded by awareness. If it is said: A person seeks to conceive of the angel, the jinn, the spirit, and many things, while he is not aware of them? It is said: He has heard these names, so he seeks to conceive their named objects, just as one who understands their meanings seeks to conceive their meanings, whether the meanings were conceived by him before or not. And when he conceives the named of these names, he must know that they are named by this name, because if he conceived a reality and that name was not in it, he would not have conceived his sought object. So here what is conceived is a self and that it is named so-and-so, of the type of one who sees snow and does not know it, then he sees it and knows its name is 'snow'. And this is not a conception of the meaning only, but of the meaning and its name, and this undoubtedly is sought, but this does not necessitate that the simple meaning is sought, for what is sought here is an assent, and it involves a linguistic matter. Also, what is sought here is not obtained by mere definition, rather it is necessary to define the defined by pointing to it, or other than that which is not satisfied with mere utterance. If it is said: You made the impossibility of seeking a necessary concomitant of the two contradictories, and the two contradictories neither combine nor disappear, and if the necessary is absent, the necessary-of is absent; so if the impossibility of seeking—which is the necessary—is absent, then the impossibility of seeking...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument against the logicians' theory of definition: simple concepts cannot be sought, so they cannot be the object of definition (hadd). He employs a disjunctive argument: either the mind is already aware of the simple concept or not. If aware, seeking it is redundant (cannot obtain what is already obtained). If not aware, seeking is impossible because one cannot seek what one has no awareness of. To the objection that people seek to conceive of angels, jinn, etc., he responds that such seeking is not for a simple concept (tasawwur mufrad) but for a composite: the thing and its name. The seeker hears the name and seeks to know the named object, which involves a judgment (tasdiq) that the named is called by that name. He likens it to someone who sees snow for the first time and learns its name. Thus, the sought object includes both the meaning and the linguistic label, making it a complex idea. Consequently, definition alone cannot provide this; it often requires ostensive definition or other means. The passage ends with a second objection about the impossibility being a necessary concomitant of both contradictories, a point cut off in the source.

Key Terms
تصور مفرد
مطلوب
شعور
تصديق

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is wrong with the claim that a person seeks to conceive of an angel?
A) The person must have seen the angel, so it's not simple.
B) The person seeks a complex involving a judgment and name, not a simple concept.
C) The person cannot seek what he is unaware of, so it's impossible.
D) The person is actually seeking a definition, not a concept.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument shows that simple concepts cannot be the object of definition because seeking them is logically impossible – either redundant or impossible – and real seeking involves complex ideas and judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'al-ḥudūth' (temporal origination). A logician claims that by defining it you can conceive it. Using Ibn Taymiyya's disjunctive argument, explain why this claim is problematic. Write a brief analysis (3-5 sentences).
?
Find an example from usul al-fiqh where a simple concept (e.g., 'illa) is presumed to be definable but actually involves a tasdiq (judgment). Describe how Ibn Taymiyya would analyze it.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Definition for Singular Concepts
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of definition's role in attaining singular concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma against seeking singular concepts by definition and evaluate his conclusion that definition serves only as a name.

Core Idea
Definitions cannot produce new concepts; they merely name what is already known.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a powerful critique against the logicians' claim that definitions (hadd) can generate new singular concepts (tasawwurat mufrada). He presents a dilemma: if the mind already possesses the concept, seeking it is redundant; if it does not, it cannot desire what it is unaware of. This lesson unpacks his argument, addresses objections, and reveals his radical conclusion that definition is no more useful than a name.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The logicians hold that definition (hadd) is the means to attain singular concepts (tasawwurat). Ibn Taymiyya challenges this by arguing such concepts cannot even be desired.
2
Follow the dilemma
Note the two exclusive possibilities: the mind is either aware of the concept or not. In both cases, desiring it is impossible—if aware, it's already attained; if unaware, desire requires prior awareness.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Consider the counterexample: people seek to conceptualize 'king', 'jinn', 'spirit' though unaware. Ibn Taymiyya responds that they are seeking the named entity (musamma), not the pure meaning, involving a linguistic element and assent (tasdiq).
This shifts the target from pure concept to name-and-concept.
4
Examine the logical rejoinder
Ibn Taymiyya addresses an opponent who argues that impossibility of desire cannot be a necessary condition for both opposites. He explains it is a necessary condition for each opposite individually, not jointly.
This is a subtle logical point affirming his conclusion.
5
Evaluate the final conclusion
Conclude: singular concepts are not desired; if present, definition is redundant; if absent, definition cannot produce them. Thus definition functions like a name—it does not create new conceptual knowledge.
This equalizes hadd and ism, undermining the logicians' epistemic hierarchy.

الخامس: إن التصورات المفردة يمتنع أن تكون مطلوبة، فيمتنع أن تُطلب بالحد. وذلك لأن الذهن إما أن يكون شاعراً بها، وإما أن لا يكون شاعراً بها. فإن كان شاعراً بها امتنع طلب المشعور وحصوله، لأن تحصيل الحاصل ممتنع، وإنما قد يُطلب دوام الشعور وتكرره أو قوّتُه. وإن لم يكن شاعراً بها امتنع من النفس طلب ما لا تشعر به، فإن الطلب والقصد مسبوق بالشعور. فإن قيل: فالإنسان يطلب تصور الملك، والجنّ، والرُّوح، وأشياء كثيرة، وهو لا يشعر بها؟ قيل: هو قد سمع هذه الأسماء، فهو يطلب تصور مسماها، كما يطلب مَنْ يفهم معانيها تصور معانيها، سواء كانت المعاني متصورة له قبل ذلك أو لم تكن. وهو إذا تصوّر مسمى هذه الأسماء فلا بد أن يعلم أنها مسماة بهذا الاسم، إذ لو تصور حقيقة ولم يكن ذلك الاسم فيها لم يكن تَصَوَّرَ مطلوبه. فهنا المتصور ذات وأنها مسماة بكذا، من جنس ما يرى الثلج مَنْ لم يكن يعرفه، فيراه ويعلم أن اسمه «الثلج». وهذا ليس تصوراً للمعنى فقط، بل للمعنى ولاسمه وهذا لا ريب أنه يكون مطلوباً، ولكن هذا لا يوجب أن يكون المعنى المفرد مطلوباً، فإن المطلوب هنا تصديق، وفيه أمر لغوي. وأيضاً، فإن المطلوب هنا لا يحصل بمجرد الحد بل لا بد من تعريف المحدود بالإشارة إليه، أو غير ذلك مما لا يكتفي فيه بمجرد اللفظ. فإن قيل: جعلتم امتناع الطلب لازماً للنقيضين، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، وإذا عُدم اللازم عُدم الملزوم؛ فإذا كان امتناع الطلب - وهو اللازم - معدوماً لزم عدم النقيضين، وعدم النقيضين محال. قيل: هذا الامتناع لازم على تقدير هذا النقيض وتقدير هذا النقيض، فأيهما كان ثابتاً في نفس الأمر كان الامتناع لازماً له يلزم به في نفس الأمر. وليس في هذا جمع بين النقيضين ولا رفع للنقيضين. وهذا يقوى المقصود. فإن هذا الامتناع إذا لَزِم انتفائه المُحالُ لم يكن منتفياً، بل يكون هذا الامتناع ثابتاً، وهو المطلوب. وإذا كان عدمُ هذا الامتناع مستلزماً للنقيضين كان مُحالاً، فيكون نقيضه ـ وهو ثبوت هذا الامتناع — حقاً. فيكون امتناع الطلب للتصوّرات المفردة ثابتاً، وهو المراد. وإذا لم تكن التصورات المفردة مطلوبة، فإما أن تكون حاصلة للإنسان، فلا فائدة في الحد كما أن فائدة الاسم تحصل بالحد، فلا يفيد الحدُّ التصوير؛ وإما أن لا تكون حاصلة، فمجرد الحد لا يوجب تصور المسميات لمن لا يعرفها. ومتى كان له شعور بها لم يحتج إلى الحد في ذلك الشعور، إلا من جنس ما يحتاج إلى الاسم. والمقصود هو التسوية بين فائدة الحد وفائدة الاسم. لكن الحد إذا تعددت فيه الألفاظ كان كتعدد الأسماء، سواء كانت مشتقة أو غير مشتقة.
The fifth: Singular concepts cannot be desired, so they cannot be sought by definition. That is because the mind is either aware of them or not aware of them. If it is aware of them, the desire and attainment of the perceived is impossible, because attaining what is already attained is impossible; rather, one may desire the continuation, repetition, or strength of the perception. If it is not aware of them, it is impossible for the soul to desire what it is not aware of, for desire and intention are preceded by awareness. If it is said: Man desires to conceptualize 'king', 'jinn', 'spirit', and many things, while he is not aware of them? It is said: He has heard these names, so he desires to conceptualize their named entities, just as someone who understands their meanings desires to conceptualize those meanings, whether the meanings were previously conceptualized for him or not. And when he conceptualizes the named entity of these names, he must know that they are named by this name, for if he conceptualized the reality and that name was not in it, he would not have conceptualized his desired object. So here the conceptualized is an entity and that it is named such, similar to seeing snow by someone who did not know it, so he sees it and knows its name is 'snow'. This is not conceptualization of the meaning alone, but of the meaning and its name, and there is no doubt that this is desired, but this does not necessitate that the singular meaning is desired, for here what is desired is a tasdiq, and it involves a linguistic matter. Also, what is desired here is not obtained by mere definition; rather, the definiendum must be defined by pointing to it, or other means that are not satisfied with mere utterance. If it is said: You have made the impossibility of desire a necessary condition for the two opposites, and opposites neither meet nor cease, and if the necessary condition is absent, the necessitated is absent; so if the impossibility of desire – which is the necessary condition – is absent, the absence of the two opposites follows, and the absence of the two opposites is impossible. It is said: This impossibility is a necessary condition on the assumption of this opposite and that opposite, so whichever is actually existent, the impossibility is a necessary condition for it in actuality. This does not involve combining the two opposites nor removing both. This strengthens the intended point. For if the non-existence of this impossibility necessitated the impossible, then it (the impossibility) would not be non-existent; rather, this impossibility is existent, which is what is intended. And if the non-existence of this impossibility entails the two opposites, it is impossible, so its opposite – namely the existence of this impossibility – is true. So the impossibility of desire for singular concepts is established, which is intended. And since singular concepts are not desired, either they are already present in man, in which case there is no benefit in definition, just as the benefit of the name is obtained by definition, so the definition does not provide conceptualization; or they are not present, so mere definition does not necessitate conceptualization of the named for someone who does not know them. And if he has awareness of them, he does not need the definition for that awareness, except in the same way he needs the name. The point is the equalization between the benefit of definition and the benefit of the name. But if the definition contains multiple words, it is like multiple names, whether derived or not.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that definitions (hadd) can produce new singular concepts (tasawwurat mufrada). He presents a dilemma: if the mind already has the concept, seeking it is impossible because that would be attaining what is already attained (tahsil al-hasil); if it doesn't have it, it cannot seek it because desire and intention (talab wa qasd) are preceded by awareness (shu'ur). The author then addresses an objection: humans seek concepts like 'king', 'jinn', and 'spirit' without having awareness of them. He responds that they have heard these names and seek the named entities (musamma), not the pure meanings. This involves a linguistic element (amr lughawi) and is a tasdiq (assent) rather than a pure tasawwur. Another objection concerns the logical impossibility of both opposites being absent; Ibn Taymiyya explains that the impossibility of desire is a necessary condition (lazim) for each opposite, and its existence does not entail combining or removing both. He concludes that singular concepts are not desired, so definitions are useless for obtaining them; if they are already present, definition is redundant; if absent, definition alone cannot produce them—it only serves like a name (ism). Thus, Ibn Taymiyya equates the benefit of definition with that of a name.

Key Terms
تصور مفرد (tasawwur mufrad): A singular concept; here it refers to an individual concept that is either simple or complex, but Ibn Taymiyya argues it cannot be the object of desire because desire requires prior awareness. [Note: In standard logic, tasawwur mufrad is a basic unit of meaning; Ibn Taymiyya critiques the possibility of seeking it through definition.]
حد (hadd): A definition composed of genus and differentia; Ibn Taymiyya argues it cannot yield new singular concepts, only serve as a name. [Note: In earlier logical works, hadd is the primary method for attaining tasawwur; Ibn Taymiyya challenges this epistemic role.]
طلب (talab): Desire or intention; here it is said to be impossible without prior awareness of the object. [Note: Stable term across the tradition, but Ibn Taymiyya uses it in a psychological argument against definition.]
شعور (shu'ur): Awareness or perception; necessary for desire to occur. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why can singular concepts not be sought?
A) Because they are too complex for the mind to grasp.
B) Because either the mind already has them or cannot desire what it is unaware of.
C) Because definition is only useful for genera, not species.
D) Because singular concepts are accidental properties.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique reduces definition to a mere naming function, denying it the power to produce new conceptual knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument using a contemporary example: can a definition of 'jihad' produce a new concept for someone who has never heard of it? Walk through the two possibilities and explain why his conclusion holds.
?
In a qiyas argument, the 'illa (effective cause) is defined. Using Ibn Taymiyya's framework, argue whether such a definition can truly create new understanding or is merely clarifying an existing relation.

Critique of the Distinction Between Essential and Accidental
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the logicians' failed distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three distinctions logicians used to separate essential from accidental attributes and explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues they fail.

Core Idea
The logicians' own scholars admit their three distinctions between essential and accident do not hold.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents the cornerstone of the logicians' theory of definition: the distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes. He lists the three criteria they propose—dependence of reality, causal relation, and priority in mind and reality—then reveals that even their own critical scholars (muhaqqiqun) have refuted each. This undermines the entire foundation of definition by genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three distinctions
Read the passage and list the three ways logicians try to differentiate essential from accidental: (1) essential is that on which reality depends, (2) essential is an effect of the essence, (3) essential precedes the essence in mind and reality.
These are the logicians' own claims, which Ibn Taymiyya will critique.
2
Understand the subdivision of accidents
Note that logicians divide accidents into necessary (lazim) and contingent ('arid). Necessary accidents are further split into those necessary for existence (e.g., shadow for horse) and those necessary for the essence (e.g., evenness for four).
This subdivision is important because the criteria are meant to separate essential from even the necessary accidents.
3
Recognize the internal critique
Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians' own critical scholars (muhaqqiqun) have refuted all three distinctions. These scholars showed that none of the criteria successfully distinguishes essential from accidental.
Ibn Taymiyya does not repeat the refutations here but relies on their authority and his other works.
4
Assess the implications for definition
If the distinction between essential and accidental fails, then the theory of definition (hadd) that depends on it—namely, definition composed of genus (essential shared) and differentia (essential distinguishing)—is also undermined. This challenges the whole edifice of Aristotelian logic as used in Islamic sciences.
This leads to alternative approaches to definition and conceptualization in the Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس أن يقال : المفيد لتصور الحقيقة عندهم هو «الحد التام»، وهو «الحقيقي»، وهو المؤلف من الجنس» و «الفصل» من «الذاتيات المشتركة والمميزة، دون العَرَضيات» التي هي العَرَض العام» و «الخاصة». والمثال المشهور عندهم الذاتي المميز لـ «الإنسان» الذي هو الفصل هو «الناطق»، والخاصة هي «الضاحك». فنقول : مبنى هذا الكلام على الفرق بين الذاتي والعرضي. وهم يقولون : المحمول الذاتي هو داخل في حقيقة الموضوع، أي: الوصف الذاتي داخل في حقيقة الموصوف بخلاف المحمول العَرَضي، فإنه خارج عن حقيقته. ويقولون: «الذاتي» هو الذي تتوقف الحقيقة عليه، بخلاف العَرَضي». ويقسمون العرضي إلى (لازم)) و عارض، واللازم» إلى «لازم لوجود الماهية دون حقيقتها»، كالظل للفرس، والحدوث للحيوان، وإلى «لازم للماهية»، كالزوجية والفردية للأربعة والثلاثة . والفرق بين الازم الماهية و لازم وجودها أن لازم وجودها يمكن أن تُعقل الماهية موجودة دونه، بخلاف لازم الماهية لا يمكن أن يُعقل موجوداً دونه. وجعلوا له خاصة ثانية، وهو أن «الذاتي» ما كان معلولاً للماهية، بخلاف اللازم». ثم قالوا : من اللوازم ما يكون معلولاً للماهية بغير وسط، وقد يقولون : ما كان ثابتاً لها بواسطة . وقالوا أيضاً: «الذاتي» ما يكون سابقاً للماهية في الذهن والخارج، بخلاف اللازم»، فإنه ما يكون تابعاً . فذكروا هذه الفروق الثلاثة، وطعن محققوهم في كل واحد من هذه الفروق الثلاثة، وبينوا أنه لا يحصل به الفرق بين الذاتي وغيره، كما قد بسطت كلامهم في غير هذا الموضع .
The sixth aspect: It is said: The beneficial [thing] for conceptualizing the reality according to them is the 'complete definition', which is the 'real definition', composed of 'genus' and 'differentia' from the 'essential shared and distinguishing attributes', not the 'accidental attributes' which are 'general accident' and 'property'. Their famous example is the distinguishing essential [attribute] for 'human' which is the differentia 'rational', and the property is 'laughing'. So we say: The basis of this statement is the distinction between essential and accidental. And they say: The essential predicate is included in the reality of the subject, i.e., the essential description is included in the reality of the described, contrary to the accidental predicate, for it is outside its reality. And they say: The 'essential' is that upon which the reality depends, contrary to the 'accidental'. And they divide the accidental into 'necessary' and 'contingent', and the 'necessary' into 'necessary for the existence of the essence without its reality', like shadow for the horse, and temporal origination for the animal, and 'necessary for the essence', like evenness and oddness for four and three. The difference between the necessary for the essence and the necessary for its existence is that the necessary for its existence can be understood while the essence exists without it, contrary to the necessary for the essence which cannot be understood as existing without it. And they made for it a second property, that the 'essential' is that which is an effect of the essence, contrary to the 'necessary'. Then they said: Among the necessary [attributes] are those which are effects of the essence without an intermediary, and they sometimes say: that which is established for it through an intermediary. And they also said: The 'essential' is that which precedes the essence in the mind and in reality, contrary to the 'necessary', for it is that which follows. So they mentioned these three distinctions, and their critical scholars refuted each of these three distinctions, and showed that a distinction between essential and other [than essential] is not obtained by them, as I have elaborated their statements elsewhere.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes. Ibn Taymiyya first restates the logicians' own definitions: the essential predicate is included in the subject's reality, while the accidental is external; the essential is that on which reality depends; they further divide accidents into necessary and contingent, and necessary into that necessary for existence and that necessary for the essence. Then they propose two additional criteria: essential is an effect of the essence (directly or via intermediary) and essential precedes the essence in mind and reality while necessary follows. Ibn Taymiyya then notes that even their own critical scholars (muhaqqiqun) have refuted all three distinctions, showing they fail to differentiate essential from non-essential. He does not repeat the full refutation here but refers to his other works. Thus, the foundation for the theory of definition (composed of genus and differentia as essential attributes) is undermined.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
فصل
خاصة

Checkpoint
What is the logicians' candidate difference between essential and necessary accidental attributes according to Ibn Taymiyya?
A) Essential precedes the essence in the mind, while necessary follows.
B) Essential is accidental, while necessary is essential.
C) Essential depends on existence, while necessary depends on reality.
D) Essential is a property, while necessary is a differentia.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' three criteria for distinguishing essential from accidental are self-refuted by their own scholars, undermining the theory of definition by genus and differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the three distinctions logicians proposed between essential and accidental. For each, explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers it refuted by the muhaqqiqun.
?
Consider the definition of 'salah' as 'acts of worship consisting of specific actions and recitations'. Identify which attributes might be considered essential (e.g., intention, takbir, ruku) and which accidental (e.g., facing qibla, timeliness). Does the logicians' first criterion (dependence of reality) successfully separate them? Why or why not?

Ibn Taymiyya's First Objection: Essential Attributes Are Arbitrary
Author argues that the logicians' priority of essential attributes is arbitrary and contradicts external reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the priority of essential attributes is a conventional mental ordering, not a reflection of external reality, and evaluate its implications for definitions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The essential attribute's priority is a mental convention, not a reality.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a direct attack on the foundational distinction between essential and accidental attributes. The logicians assert that some attributes are 'essential' (dhati) and their concepts must precede the concept of the described in the mind, even in external reality. Ibn Taymiyya counters that this priority is nothing but an arbitrary convention (tahakkum) imposed by a particular school, not grounded in innate human reasoning or external facts. In this lesson, you will unpack his two-part objection and see how he reduces the entire edifice of quiddity and its parts to mere mental and linguistic constructs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Find in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya states what the logicians assert: that the 'essential' attribute's concept is prior to the described, and that it is a constitutive part (juz' muqawwim) of the quiddity, both in mind and in external reality.
The claim is that certain attributes are 'inside' the essence (dakhil) and others 'outside' (kharij).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's first objection: pure arbitrariness
Notice his argument that this priority is just a report of their own convention (wad'). He says they arbitrarily advance one attribute over another, and anyone who follows them is merely imitating. He points out that external realities are independent of our mental ordering.
He gives examples: blackness is both color and accident; man is both animal and rational. Each described has multiple attributes that are equally 'one' in external reality.
3
Analyze his second objection: no innate basis
He argues that if this priority were innate (fitri), all humans would perceive it without imitation. Yet most people do not spontaneously prioritize one attribute over another. He concludes that all attributes are merely concomitants (lawazim) and that knowledge of the described increases when any attribute is known, without any being 'inside' the essence.
He uses the phrase 'Sā'ir banī Ādam' (the rest of Adam's children) to emphasize universality.
4
Examine his reduction to linguistic signification
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the logicians' talk of quiddity, internal parts, and external concomitants actually refers to mental meanings indicated by conformity (muṭābaqa), implication (taḍammun), and association (iltizām). The 'parts' are only parts in mental conception and verbal expression, not in external reality.
He notes a contradiction: sometimes they say the essential precedes the quiddity, sometimes that it does not postpone it, showing conceptual confusion.

Source Text

بطلان تقديم تصور الذاتي

ومعلوم أن جميع الصفات اللازمة منها ما هو خاص بالموصوف يصلح أن يكون فصلاً، ومنها ما هو مشترك بينه وبين غيره وكل منهما في الخارج واحد. فكما أن السوادَ هو «اللون» وهو «العرض» وهو «القائم بغيره»، و «الثلاثة» هي «العدد» وهي «الفرد»، فَـ الإنسان هو الحيوان وهو «الناطق»، و «الفرس» هو «الحيوان» وهو «الصاهل». وهكذا سائر المحدودات. وما ذكروه من أن ما جعلوه هو «الذاتي» يتقدم تصوره في الذهن لتصور الموصوف، دون الآخر، فباطل من وجهين. أحدهما: إن هذا خبر عن وَضْعِهم، إذ هم يقدّمون هذا في أذهانهم ويؤخرون هذا. وهذا تحكُّم محض. فكل من قَدَّم هذا دون هذا فإنما قلدهم في ذلك. ومعلوم أن الحقائق الخارجية المستغنية عنا لا تكون تابعة لتصوراتنا، بل تصوراتنا تابعة لها. فليس إذا فَرَضْنا هذا مقدَّماً وهذا مؤخراً يكون هذا في الخارج كذلك. وسائر بني آدم الذين لم يقلدوهم في هذا الوضع لا يستحضرون هذا التقديم والتأخير. ولو كان هذا فطرياً لكانت الفطرة تدركه بدون التقليد المغيّر لها، كما تدرك سائر الأمور الفطرية. والذي في الفطرة أن هذه اللوازم كلها لوازم للموصوف، وقد تخطر بالبال وقد لا تخطر، وكلما خطرت كان الإنسان أعلم بالموصوف، وإذا لم تخطر كان علمه بصفاته أقل. أما أن يكون هذا خارجاً عن الذات وهذا داخلاً في الذات، فهذا تحكُّم ليس له شاهد لا في الخارج ولا في الفطرة. لا سيما وهم يقولون: «الذاتي» يتقدم على الماهية في الذهن وفي الخارج، ويسمونه «الجزء المقوّم لها». ويقولون: أجزاء الماهية متقدمة عليها في الذهن وفي الخارج، لأن الماهية مركبة منها، وكل مركب فإنه مسبوق بمفرداته. ومعلوم أن صفات الموصوف قائمة به يمتنع أن تكون مقدمة عليه في الخارج. وأما تسمية الصفة جزءاً فسبب ذلك أنها أجزاء في التصور الذهني وفي اللفظ، فإنك إذا قلت: «جسم حساس، نام، متحرك بالإرادة، ناطق» كان هذا المجموع - لفظه ومعناه - مركباً من هذه الألفاظ ومعانيها، وتلك هي أجزاء هذا المركب. ولكن هذا يحقق ما قلناه أن ما من سموه «الماهية» و «جزئها الداخل فيها» و «لازمها الخارج عنها» يعود إلى المعاني المتصوّرة في الذهن التي يدل عليها اللفظ بـ «المطابقة»، وجزؤها ما دل عليه بـ «التضمن»، وخارجها اللازم ما دل عليه بـ «الالتزام». وهم تارة يقولون: «الذاتي يسبق الماهية»، وتارة يقولون: لا يتأخر عنها، من هذين فرقاً. وهما متداخلان؛ فإن ما يتقدم عنها يمتنع أن يتأخر عنها.
Invalidity of presenting the concept of the essential.

It is known that all necessary attributes: among them are those specific to the described, suitable to be a differentia, and among them are those shared between it and others, each of them in external reality is one. Just as blackness is color, and it is accident, and it is that which subsists in another, and three is number, and it is odd, so man is animal, and he is rational, and the horse is animal, and it is neighing. Thus the rest of defined things. And what they mentioned that what they made to be the 'essential' has its concept prior in the mind to the concept of the described, to the exclusion of the other, is false in two ways. One of them: This is a report about their own convention, for they advance this in their minds and delay this. And this is pure arbitrariness. So everyone who advances this over that merely imitates them in that. And it is known that external realities, independent of us, are not subject to our concepts, rather our concepts are subject to them. So if we suppose this as prior and this as posterior, it is not the case that it is so in external reality. And the rest of Adam's children who did not imitate them in this convention do not recall this priority and posteriority. And if this were innate, then innate disposition would perceive it without imitation that alters it, just as it perceives other innate matters. And what is in innate disposition is that all these concomitants are concomitants of the described, and they may occur to the mind or not, and whenever they occur, man is more knowledgeable of the described, and if they do not occur, his knowledge of its attributes is less. As for one being outside the essence and another inside the essence, this is arbitrariness with no witness either in external reality or in innate disposition. Especially since they say: The 'essential' is prior to the quiddity in the mind and in external reality, and they call it the 'constitutive part' of it. And they say: The parts of the quiddity are prior to it in the mind and in external reality, because the quiddity is composed of them, and every composite is preceded by its simple parts. And it is known that attributes of the described subsist in it, and it is impossible for them to be prior to it in external reality. As for naming the attribute a part, the reason is that they are parts in mental conception and in expression, for if you say: 'a body that is sensitive, growing, moving by will, rational,' this composite—its expression and meaning—is composed of these words and their meanings, and those are parts of this composite. But this confirms what we said: that what they call 'quiddity', and its 'internal part', and its 'external concomitant' returns to meanings conceived in the mind, which the expression indicates by 'conformity', and its part is what it indicates by 'implication', and its external concomitant is what it indicates by 'association'. And sometimes they say: 'The essential precedes the quiddity,' and sometimes they say: it does not postpone it, making a distinction between them. And they are interwoven; for what precedes it cannot postpone it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the concept of an essential attribute (al-dhati) necessarily precedes the concept of the described (al-mawsoof) in the mind, and that this priority is not merely conventional but reflects an external reality. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that necessary attributes are of two kinds: those specific to the described (suitable as differentia) and those shared with others (common). He gives examples: blackness is color, accident, and that which subsists in another; three is number and odd; man is animal and rational; horse is animal and neighing. Each described thing has multiple attributes that are all equally 'one' in external reality. The logicians arbitrarily select one attribute as 'essential' and claim its concept precedes. Ibn Taymiyya calls this pure arbitrariness (tahakkum), as it is merely a convention they impose. He argues that external realities are independent of our mental ordering; our concepts follow reality, not vice versa. The fact that most people do not instinctively prioritize one attribute over another shows that this priority is not innate (fitri). If it were innate, it would be universally recognized without imitation. In reality, all attributes are concomitants (lawazim) of the described; one may occur to the mind or not, and when it does, knowledge of the described increases, but there is no inherent 'inside' or 'outside' the essence. Ibn Taymiyya then addresses a parenthetical claim: the logicians say the essential 'precedes the quiddity' and call it a 'constitutive part'. Yet attributes subsist in the described and cannot be prior to it in external reality. The 'part' terminology only applies to mental concepts and verbal expressions, as in the definition 'body that is sensitive, growing, moving by will, rational' which is a composite of words and meanings. Thus, the entire apparatus of quiddity, internal part, and external concomitant reduces to mental meanings indicated by conformity (mutabaqa), implication (tadammun), and association (iltizam). The logicians contradict themselves by sometimes saying the essential precedes the quiddity and sometimes that it does not postpone it, showing the confusion.

Ibn Taymiyya's second objection: essential vs. accidental must be based on external reality
Author argues the essential/accidental distinction must have external basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection that the essential/accidental distinction must be grounded in external reality and evaluate its implications for logical definitions.

Core Idea
The essential/accidental distinction is meaningless if it only reflects mental ordering, not real attributes.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of the logical distinction between essential and accidental attributes. In this second objection, he shifts the focus from the internal coherence of the definition to the ontological grounding of the distinction itself. If the difference between an essential and an accidental attribute is merely a matter of mental priority—what the mind conceives first or last—then the entire edifice of logical definitions becomes a subjective construction. Ibn Taymiyya argues that a real distinction must reflect a real difference in the thing itself, independent of how we happen to conceive it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' position being attacked
Read the passage and note what Ibn Taymiyya says the logicians are doing: they distinguish essential from accidental based on priority and posteriority in the mind. Underline the phrase 'التقدم والتأخر في الذهن'.
This is the target of the objection.
2
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's alternative grounding
Find where Ibn Taymiyya insists that the distinction must go back to external reality (الحقيقة الخارجية). He says 'فلا بد أن يكون الفرق بينهما أمراً يعود إلى حقيقتهما الخارجة الثابتة بدون الذهن'. Highlight this sentence.
He presents this as a necessary condition.
3
Assess the consequence of denying external grounding
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if the distinction is only mental, then reality and quiddity become mental estimates (تقديرات في الأذهان). He calls these 'تخيلات ووهميات باطلة'. Identify this consequence and consider why he considers it a reductio ad absurdum.
This shows the high stakes: the entire logical system becomes fantasy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : إن كون الوصف ذاتياً للموصوف هو أمر تابع لحقيقته التي بها، سواء تصوّرَتْه أذهاننا أو لم تتصوره. فلا بد إذا كان أحد الوصفين «ذاتياً» دون الآخَرِ أن يكون الفرقُ بينهما أمراً يعود إلى حقيقتهما الخارجة الثابتة بدون الذهن، وأما أن يكون الفرق بين الحقائق الخارجة لا حقيقة له إلا بمجرد التقدم والتأخر في الذهن، فهذا لا يكون حقاً إلا أن تكون الحقيقة والماهية ما تقدر في الذهن لا ما يوجد في الخارج، وذلك أمر يتبع تقدير صاحب الذهن. وحينئذ، فيعود حاصل هذا الكلام إلى أمور مقدَّرة في الأذهان لا حقيقةً لها في الخارج وهي التخيلات والوهميات الباطلة. وهذا كثير في أصولهم، كما بيناه في غير موضع في بيان ما ذكروه في الهيولى» و «العقول» و «الماهيات» وغير ذلك.
The second way: That an attribute being essential to the described is a matter dependent on its reality by which it is, whether our minds conceive it or not. So if one of the two attributes is 'essential' without the other, the difference between them must be something that goes back to their external reality established without the mind. As for the difference between external realities having no reality except merely by priority and posteriority in the mind, this is not true unless reality and quiddity are what is estimated in the mind, not what exists in external reality, and that is a matter that follows the estimator's estimation. Then, the upshot of this speech returns to matters estimated in minds with no reality in external reality, and they are false imaginings and phantasms. And this is frequent in their principles, as we have shown elsewhere in explaining what they mentioned about 'hyle', 'intellects', 'quiddities', etc.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's second objection: the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental attributes must be based on a real difference in external reality, not merely on mental priority or posteriority. He argues that if two attributes are both real properties of a thing, and one is deemed essential and the other accidental, the criterion cannot be a mental ordering—otherwise the whole distinction is merely subjective. If the difference reduces to what the mind estimates, then the quiddity itself becomes a mental construct, not an external reality. This leads to the absurd consequence that all logical discussions about hyle, intellects, and quiddities are mere fantasies (takhayyulat) and false illusions (wahmiyat). Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the logicians for making their principles dependent on arbitrary mental estimations rather than external reality.

Key Terms

الحقيقة الخارجية
التخيلات والوهميات

Ibn Taymiyya's Third Objection: Attributes Cannot Be Prior to the Described
Author argues that attributes cannot be prior parts of the essence in external reality because they subsist in it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's third objection against the claim that essential attributes are external parts of the quiddity.

Core Idea
Attributes subsist in the described; they cannot be prior parts in external reality.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of the logicians' theory of definition. In this third objection, he targets the claim that the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) are parts that exist prior to the quiddity both in the mind and in external reality. He argues that this is absurd because attributes depend on the described for their existence. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) are parts (ajza') of the quiddity that are prior to it both in the mind and in external reality.
This is the position Ibn Taymiyya is attacking.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's premise
He implicitly relies on the premise that an attribute subsists in (is dependent on) the described substance; it cannot exist separately or prior to it.
This is clear to reason.
3
Follow the reductio
If attributes are prior parts in external reality, then the attribute would be prior to the described. But this contradicts the fact that attributes subsist in the described.
Hence the logicians' claim leads to an absurdity.
4
Evaluate the objection
The objection is a valid reductio ad absurdum. It shows that the logicians' position is self-evidently false to sound reason.
This critique undermines the idea that essential attributes are external constitutive parts of the quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا وجه ثالث يظهر به فساد ما ذكروه. وهو أنهم قالوا: «الذاتيات» هي «أجزاء متقدّمة عليها في الذهن وفي الخارج»، و «الأجزاء» هذه الصفات الماهية هي فجعلوا صفة الموصوف متقدمة عليه في الخارج. وهذا مما يُعلم بصريح العقل بطلانه.
And here is a third way by which the corruption of what they mentioned appears. And it is that they said: 'The essentials are parts prior to it in the mind and in external reality,' and 'the parts' are these attributes; they are the quiddity. So they made the attribute of the described prior to it in external reality. And this is something whose invalidity is known by clear reason.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This brief passage presents the third objection: the logicians claim that the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) are parts (ajza') that are prior to the quiddity (mahiyya) both in the mind and in external reality. Ibn Taymiyya points out the absurdity: if these attributes are parts of the quiddity and are prior to it in external reality, then the attribute of the described (i.e., the quality) would be prior to the described (the substance) in external existence. But an attribute subsists in the described; it cannot be separate or prior. This is self-evidently false to sound reason. Therefore, the whole notion that essential attributes are constitutive parts that exist prior to the quiddity in external reality is invalid.

C) Because the quiddity is prior to its parts.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's third objection shows that essential attributes cannot be external prior parts of the quiddity because attributes subsist in the described.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Genus Requirement
Author argues that requiring both genus and differentia in definition is arbitrary convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument against the necessity of including genus in a complete definition and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
The requirement of genus in definition is a convention, not a logical necessity.

The logicians insist that a complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) must be composed of a genus (shared essential attribute) and a differentia. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this as a mere arbitrary convention. He presents a dilemma: either you require all shared essential attributes to be explicitly stated, which is impractical, or you do not, in which case the differentia alone suffices by implication. Thus, the insistence on the proximate genus is unjustified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' doctrine under attack
Find the exact claim Ibn Taymiyya is critiquing: that a complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) must include both the distinctive essential (al-dhati al-mumayyiz, i.e., the differentia) and the shared essential (al-dhati al-mushtarak, i.e., the genus).
The differentia is what distinguishes the species; the genus is the wider category shared with other species.
2
Understand the dilemma he poses
Ibn Taymiyya asks: do you require that all shared essential attributes be explicitly conceptualized in the definition? If yes, then saying 'animal' is insufficient because it does not detail attributes like 'sentient, moving by will'. If no, then the differentia alone (e.g., 'rational') is enough because it implies the genus.
This is a classic constructive dilemma: both horns lead to a problem for the logicians' position.
3
Analyze the implicit indication argument
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the differentia indicates the genus by 'tadammun' (concomitance) or 'iltizam' (implication). For example, 'rational' implies 'animal' because rationality cannot exist without animality. So explicitly stating the genus is redundant if the differentia already implies it.
This draws on the semantic theory of indication (dalala) used in Arabic logic.
4
Draw the conclusion: arbitrary convention
Since the logicians do not require explicit enumeration of all shared attributes (they accept the differentia's implication), their insistence on including the proximate genus in the definition is a mere arbitrary ruling (tahakkum mahd). The requirement is a convention (wad'), not a rational necessity.
Ibn Taymiyya is not rejecting definition altogether, only the rigid formal structure imposed by Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه السابع : اشتراط الصفات الذاتية المشتركة أمر وضعي
الوجه السابع : أن يقال : قولهم إن الحد التام يفيد تصوير الحقيقة»، واشتراطهم أن يكون مؤلفاً من «الذاتي المميز» و «الذاتي المشترك» ـ وهو «الجنس». يقال لهم: هل تشترطون فيه أن تُتصوّر جميع صفاته الذاتية المشتركة بينه وبين غيره أم لا؟ فإن اشترطتم ذلك لزم أن تقولوا مثلاً جسم نام، حساس، متحرك بالإرادة»، فأما لفظ الحيوان فلا يدل على هذه الصفات بـ «المطابقة» ولا يُفصلها . وإن لم تشترطوا ذلك فاكتفوا بمجرد المميّز كَـ «الناطق» مثلاً ، فإن «الناطق» يدل على «الحيوان» كما يدلّ «الحيوانُ» على «النامي»، إذ النامي» جنس قريب لـ «الحيوان» يشترك فيه «الحيوان» و «النباتُ». فإذا أردت حدَّ «الحيوان» على وضعهم قلت: الجسم النامي الحساس المتحرك بالإرادة كما تقول في «الإنسان»: «حيوان ناطق. والمقصود أنهم إن اكتفوا في الدلالة على الصفات المشتركة بما يدلّ بـ «التضمن" أو بـ «الإلتزام» فَـ «الفصل» يدلّ على ذلك وإن أردوا تفصيلها بدلالة المطابقة فلم يفعلوا ذلك . وهذا يبين أن إيجابهم في الحد التام الجنس القريب» دون غيره تحكم محض .
Second Station - Seventh Aspect: The requirement of shared essential attributes is a purely conventional matter. The seventh aspect: It is said: Their statement that the complete definition conveys the reality, and their requirement that it be composed of the distinctive essential (differentia) and the shared essential (genus). Say to them: Do you require that all its shared essential attributes between it and others be conceptualized or not? If you require that, then you must say for example: a body that grows, is sentient, moves by will. But the word 'animal' does not indicate these attributes by 'direct denotation' nor does it detail them. And if you do not require that, and suffice with just the distinctive such as 'rational' for example, then 'rational' indicates 'animal' just as 'animal' indicates 'growing', for 'growing' is a proximate genus of 'animal' shared by 'animal' and 'plant'. So if you wanted the definition of 'animal' according to their convention you would say: the body that grows, is sentient, moves by will, just as you say for 'human': rational animal. The point is that if they suffice in indicating the shared attributes by what indicates by 'concomitance' or 'implication', then the differentia indicates that; but if they wanted their detail by direct denotation, they did not do that. This shows that their requirement in the complete definition of the proximate genus and nothing else is pure arbitrary ruling.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the logicians' requirement that a complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) must consist of a genus (jins) and a differentia (fasl). Ibn Taymiyya attacks this as a mere convention (wad') rather than a necessary truth. He poses a dilemma: either the logicians require that all shared essential attributes be explicitly conceptualized in the definition, or they do not. If they require it, then saying 'animal' is insufficient because 'animal' does not explicitly detail all the shared attributes like being sentient, moving by will, etc. One would have to list them all. If they do not require explicit detail, then sufficing with the differentia alone (e.g., 'rational') is enough because the differentia implicitly indicates the genus via concomitant or implied meaning, just as 'rational' implies 'animal'. Since the logicians do not insist on explicit detail, their insistence on including the proximate genus is arbitrary. This arbitrariness is the core of Ibn Taymiyya's criticism: the requirement is a convention, not a rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the requirement of both genus and differentia in a complete definition arbitrary?
A) Because genus and differentia are not essential attributes.
B) Because the differentia alone can indicate the genus by implication, making explicit genus unnecessary.
C) Because logicians require too many attributes in the definition.
D) Because the definition cannot convey reality at all.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique demonstrates that the requirement of including genus in a definition is a convention, not a logical necessity; the differentia's implication suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument in your own words, using the definition of 'salah' (prayer) as 'specific statements and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Does the differentia 'beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' imply the genus 'specific statements and actions'? What would Ibn Taymiyya say about the necessity of including the genus?
?
Identify a definition from a fiqh manual (e.g., 'zakat is a specific wealth given to specific recipients') and apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique: is the genus ('wealth given') necessary if the differentia already specifies it? Write a brief analysis.

The Impossibility of Encompassing All Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya explains the impossibility of knowing all attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that encompassing all attributes is impossible for humans and identify its implications for the nature of definition.

Core Idea
Knowing all attributes would require infinite regress; only Allah has comprehensive knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the Aristotelian ideal of definition by genus and differentia by showing that no human can know all the attributes (lawazim) of a thing. He argues that the chain of implications would lead to knowing everything, which is impossible. This sets the stage for his alternative: the distinguishing description.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim
Find where he states that it is impossible to encompass all attributes (al-ihata bi jami' al-sifat). This is the foundation of his critique.
2
Trace the argument from lawazim
Notice his reasoning: every created thing entails the Creator, and the Creator's knowledge encompasses everything. To know all implications of a thing would require knowing everything, which is impossible for humans.
The argument relies on the chain of implications leading to infinite regress.
3
Contrast with Allah's knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya states that only Allah knows things as they are, without possibility of increase. Humans always have hidden aspects.
4
Identify the implication for definition
Because exhaustive knowledge is impossible, definitions must be limited to distinguishing features, not all attributes.
This justifies the Muslim theorists' practice of using wasf mumayyiz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه السابع : لا يمكن الإحاطة بجميع الصفات
ولهذا كان الذي عليه عامة الناس من نظار المسلمين وغيرهم الاقتصار في الحدود على الوصف المميّز الفاصل بين المحدود وغيره، إذ التمييز يحصل بهذا. وذلك هو الوصف المطابق للمحدود في العموم والخصوص بحيث يدخل فيه جميع أفراد المحدود وأجزائه، ويخرج منه ما ليس منه. فهذا هو «الحد» الذي عليه نظار المسلمين، كما بسطنا قولهم في غير هذا الموضع.
وأما سائر الصفات المشتركة فقد لا يمكن الإحاطة بها، ولا ريب أنه كلما كان الإنسان بها أعلم كان بالموصوف أعلم، وأنه ما من تصور إلا وفوقه تصور أكمل منه.
ونحن لا سبيل إلى أن نعلم شيئاً من كل وجه، ولا نعلم لوازم كل مربوب ولوازم لوازمه إلى آخرها، فإنه ما من مخلوق إلا وهو مستلزم للخالق، والخالق مستلزم لصفاته التي منها علمه، وعلمه محيط بكل شيء. فلو علمنا لوازم لوازم الشيء إلى آخرها لزم أن نعلم كل شيء، وهذا ممتنع في البشر. فإن الله سبحانه وتعالى هو الذي يعلم الأشياء كما هي عليه من غير احتمال زيادة. وأما نحن فما من شيء نعلمه إلا ويخفي علينا من أموره ولوازمه ما لا نعلمه.
يوضح ذلك أنهم يقولون ما ذكره أبو حامد في معيار العلم: إن دلالة «التضمّن» كدلالة لفظ «البيت» على «الحائط»، ودلالة لفظ «الإنسان» على «الحيوان»، وكذلك دلالة كل وصف أخص على الوصف الأعم الجوهري. ودلالة «الالتزام» و«الاستتباع» كدلالة لفظ «السقف» على «الحائط»، فإنه مستتبع له استتباع الرفيق اللازم الخارج عن ذاته، ودلالة لفظ «الإنسان» على «قابل صنعة الخياطة ومعلّمها».
The second station - the seventh aspect: It is impossible to encompass all attributes.

For this reason, the common practice of the people among the Muslim theorists and others is to limit definitions to the distinguishing description that separates the defined from others, as distinction is achieved by this. And that is the description that corresponds to the defined in generality and specificity such that it includes all individuals of the defined and its parts, and excludes what is not from it. This is the 'definition' according to the Muslim theorists, as we have expounded their statement elsewhere.

As for the other common attributes, it may be impossible to encompass them. And there is no doubt that the more knowledgeable a person is of them, the more knowledgeable he is of the described. And there is no conception except that there is above it a more complete conception.

And we have no way to know a thing from every aspect, nor do we know the implications of every thing and the implications of its implications to the end. For there is no created thing except that it entails the Creator, and the Creator entails His attributes, among which is His knowledge, and His knowledge encompasses everything. So if we knew the implications of the implications of a thing to the end, it would necessitate that we know everything, and this is impossible for humans. For Allah, glorified and exalted, is the One who knows things as they are, without possibility of increase. As for us, there is no thing we know except that there is hidden from us of its matters and implications what we do not know.

Clarifying this: they mention what Abu Hamid said in Mi'yar al-'Ilm: The indication of inclusion is like the indication of the word 'house' to 'wall', and the indication of the word 'human' to 'animal', and similarly the indication of every more specific description to the more general essential description. And the indication of implication and entailment is like the indication of the word 'ceiling' to 'wall', for it entails it as an inseparable companion necessitated by its essence, and the indication of the word 'human' to 'capable of the craft of tailoring and teaching it'.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and definitional argument by Ibn Taymiyya. He begins by stating the seventh aspect of the second station: it is impossible to encompass all attributes (al-ihata bi jami' al-sifat). He then explains that the common practice of Muslim theorists (nuzzar) is to limit definitions to the distinguishing description (al-wasf al-mumayyiz al-fasil) that separates the defined from others. This is because distinction (tamyiz) is achieved through that description. He defines the proper definition as one that matches the defined in generality and specificity, including all its individuals and parts, and excluding what is not from it. This is the definition according to Muslim theorists.

He then turns to the other common attributes (sifat mushtaraka) that may be impossible to encompass. He argues that the more knowledge one has of these attributes, the more one knows the described thing. But there is always a more complete conception above any conception. He then gives an epistemological argument: we cannot know a thing from every aspect, and we cannot know all the implications (lawazim) of each thing, let alone the implications of those implications. Because every created thing entails the Creator, and the Creator entails His attributes, and His knowledge encompasses everything. If we were to know all the implications of implications, we would have to know everything, which is impossible for humans. Only Allah knows things as they are, without potential for increase. For us, there is always something hidden about a thing.

He then clarifies by quoting al-Ghazali's Mi'yar al-'Ilm on the two types of indication: inclusive indication (dalalat al-tadamun) like 'house' indicating 'wall', and implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam) like 'ceiling' indicating 'wall'. He gives examples: 'human' indicates 'animal' by inclusion, and 'human' indicates 'capable of tailoring and teaching' by implication. This clarifies that the logicians' own theory of meaning shows that a definition need not include all attributes, as some are implied.

Key Terms
إحاطة
لوازم
حدّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is it impossible for humans to know all the attributes (lawazim) of a thing?
A) Because attributes are infinite
B) Because knowing all implications would require knowing everything, including the Creator, which is impossible
C) Because the logicians forbid it
D) Because definitions are limited to two terms

Key Takeaway
Humans cannot know all attributes of any thing; thus definitions must be limited to distinguishing features.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a fiqh ruling that requires a definition, e.g., 'salah'. Try to list all its attributes; then see which ones are truly distinguishing. Explain why Ibn Taymiyya would say you cannot list them all.

The Proper Definition as the Distinguishing Description
Ibn Taymiyya defines the proper definition as the distinguishing description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of Ibn Taymiyya's 'hadd' and compare it to the logicians' genus-differentia definition.

Core Idea
A proper definition is the wasf mumayyiz that matches the defined in generality and specificity.

Here Ibn Taymiyya presents the definition accepted by Muslim theorists: a description that distinguishes the defined from everything else. He emphasizes that this definition must correspond to the defined in scope, including all its individuals and parts and excluding all else. This is a practical alternative to the logicians' complex requirement of genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's definition of 'hadd'
Find the sentence where he says 'فهذا هو «الحد» الذي عليه نظار المسلمين'. This is the core definition.
2
Identify the three conditions
The definition is: (1) a distinguishing description (wasf mumayyiz), (2) matches the defined in generality and specificity (al-mutabiq li al-mahdud fi al-umum wa al-khusus), (3) includes all individuals and parts of the defined and excludes what is not from it.
3
Compare to logicians' definition
The logicians require genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) which are specific types of descriptions. Ibn Taymiyya's definition is broader: any distinguishing description that meets the scope condition qualifies as a hadd.
This relaxes the requirement for a strict genus-differentia structure.
4
Notice the source of authority
Ibn Taymiyya attributes this definition to 'nuzzar al-Muslimin' (Muslim theorists), indicating it is the established practice, not a novelty.
