Critique of Logicians' Condition on Dalaalat in Definitions
Author critiques logicians' claim that only mutabaqa and tadammun are valid in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the inconsistency in the logicians' exclusion of dalaalat al-iltizam from definitions using Ibn Taymiyya's argument.

Core Idea
The logicians' own definitional chains expose the arbitrariness of excluding implication.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' rule that only correspondence (mutabaqa) and inclusion (tadammun) are valid in definitions. He argues that their own reasoning leads to a contradiction: if inclusion is valid, then a single term like 'rational' should suffice to define 'human', but they insist on 'animal' and 'rational'. Let's see how he exposes this flaw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' condition
Find the passage where the author states that logicians consider only dalaalat al-mutabaqa and al-tadammun valid in definitions, excluding dalaalat al-iltizam. Note the reason they give: the infinite regress of implications.
Look at the first sentence of the Arabic.
2
Understand the exclusion of dalaalat al-iltizam
The logicians argue that dalaalat al-iltizam leads to an infinite chain of meanings, making a definition impossible. Recognize that this is their justification.
They fear the word would indicate too much.
3
Examine the chain of tadammun
Ibn Taymiyya points out that their own example of tadammun creates a similar chain: 'human' indicates 'animal', 'animal' indicates 'sentient, moving by will', 'sentient' indicates 'growing', 'growing' indicates 'body'. This chain is also potentially infinite.
The same problem they attribute to iltizam arises in tadammun.
4
Spot the inconsistency
If tadammun is valid, then 'rational' should indicate 'animal' by tadammun (just as 'growing' indicates 'body'). So they must either accept 'rational' alone as a sufficient definition (omitting 'animal') or require all intermediate terms—neither of which aligns with their condition.
This creates a dilemma: omission or excessive detail.
5
Summarize the critique's logic
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the logicians' condition is self-undermining: the very chain they accept in tadammun mirrors the infinite regress they reject in iltizam. Thus their exclusion of iltizam is inconsistent with their own practice.
The critique is not against definitions per se, but against the arbitrary boundary they set.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والمعتبر في التعريفات دلالة «المطابقة» و «التضمّن». فأما دلالة «الالتزام» فلا، لأن المدلول عليه فيها غير محدود ولا محصور، إذ لوازم الأشياء ولوازم لوازمها لا تنضبط ولا تنحصر فيؤدّي إلى أن يكون اللفظ الواحد دليلاً على ما لا يتناهى من المعاني، وهو محال . فقد جعل دلالة الخاص على الاسم دلالة تضمن»، كدلالة «الإنسان» على «الحيوان»، وذكر أنها معتبرة في التعريفات ومعلوم أن دلالة «الحيوان» على «الحساس، المتحرك بالإرادة ودلالة «الحساس» على النامي» ودلالة «النامي» على «الجسم» هو كذلك عندهم ومعلوم أن دلالة الناطق» على الحيوان» كدلالة «النامي» على الجسم». فكان الواجب حينئذ أن يكتفى بـ «الناطق» أو لا يكتفي الحيوان» . فإنهم إن اكتفوا بدلالة التضمّن لزم الحذفُ ، وإن لم يكتفوا لزم التفصيلُ .
He said: 'What is considered valid in definitions is the indication of 'mutabaqa' and 'tadammun'. As for the indication of 'iltizam', it is not, because what is indicated by it is not limited nor confined, since the necessary concomitants of things and the concomitants of their concomitants cannot be restricted nor enumerated, leading to a single word indicating an infinite number of meanings, which is impossible.' He thus made the indication of the specific term for the name a 'tadammun' indication, like the indication of 'human' for 'animal', and mentioned that it is considered valid in definitions. It is known that the indication of 'animal' for 'sentient, moving by will' and the indication of 'sentient' for 'growing' and the indication of 'growing' for 'body' is likewise according to them. It is known that the indication of 'rational' for 'animal' is like the indication of 'growing' for 'body'. So then it was obligatory either to suffice with 'rational' or not to suffice with 'animal'. For if they suffice with 'tadammun' indication, omission follows, and if they do not suffice, detail follows.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage critiques the logicians' condition that only dalaalat al-mutabaqa (correspondence) and dalaalat al-tadammun (inclusion) are valid in definitions, excluding dalaalat al-iltizam (implication) because it leads to infinite regress. The author then shows an inconsistency in their own example: they claim 'human' indicates 'animal' by tadammun, and 'animal' in turn indicates 'sentient, moving by will', etc. By the same logic, 'rational' should indicate 'animal' by tadammun, just as 'growing' indicates 'body'. Thus they would have to accept that 'rational' alone suffices for a definition (since it includes by tadammun what 'animal' does), or they would need to specify all the intermediate terms—neither of which satisfies their condition consistently. This demonstrates a flaw in their theory.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best illustrates the problem Ibn Taymiyya points out with dalaalat al-tadammun in definitions?
A) Defining 'wudu' by mentioning both parts (washing face, hands, etc.) and intention (niyya) leads to infinite regress.
B) Defining 'salat' as 'bowings and prostrations' (tadammun) might skip the condition of facing qibla, requiring either omission or excessive detail.
C) Defining 'zakat' as 'tax' (iltizam) is invalid because tax is not a part of zakat.
D) Defining 'iman' as 'belief' (mutabaqa) is too narrow and excludes actions.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the logicians' exclusion of dalaalat al-iltizam from definitions is inconsistent with their own acceptance of dalaalat al-tadammun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'wudu' in fiqh. It is often defined by its parts (washing face, hands, etc.) and conditions (intention). Using Ibn Taymiyya's critique, explain whether 'washing the face' alone (by tadammun) could replace the full definition.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the definition of 'amr' (command) often includes 'demand' (talab) and 'superiority' (isti'la'). Identify which of these are parts (tadammun) and which are implications (iltizam). Then decide if the logicians' condition would accept this definition.

The Impossibility of Distinguishing Dhati from 'Aradi Lazim
Author argues that logicians' requirement of differentiating fusul as dhati from 'aradi lazim is unattainable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that distinguishing essential attributes from necessary accidents in definitions is impossible.

Core Idea
No property can be objectively classified as essential or accidental — the distinction is arbitrary.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a powerful epistemological challenge: logicians require definitions to use essential differentiae (fusul dhatiyya) while distinguishing them from necessary accidents ('aradi lazim). But how can we know which properties are truly essential? In this lesson, we will analyse his argument that for any distinguishing property, one person can call it essential and another accidental, with no objective criterion to decide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' requirement
Logicians insist that definitions must include 'fusul' which are essential distinguishing attributes (dhatiyyat), and they must be differentiated from necessary accidents ('aradi lazim) that are not part of the essence.
2
State Ibn Taymiyya's impossibility claim
He asserts that this differentiation is impossible (ghayr mumkin).
3
Examine his reason
For any property that is coextensive with the defined term (corresponding in generality and specificity), one person can claim it is essential and another that it is a necessary accident.
The property is a 'mumayyiz' (distinguisher) that uniquely applies to the defined term.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take 'rational' for humans: one could argue it is essential (defining) or a necessary accident. No objective ground exists to settle the dispute.
Similarly, 'laughter' is a property of humans; is it essential or accidental?
5
Draw the conclusion
The logicians' definitional project is epistemologically ungrounded — the distinction between dhati and 'aradi lazim is subjective and conventional.

الوجه الثامن : هو أن اشتراطهم مثلاً ذكر «الفصول» التي هي «الذاتيات المميّزة مع تفريقهم بين الذاتي» و «العرضي اللازم للماهية غير ممكن. إذ ما من مميز هو من خواص المحدود المطابقة له في العموم والخصوص : إلا ويمكن شخصا أن يجعله ذاتياً مميزاً ويمكن الآخر أن يجعله عرضيّاً لازماً للماهية.
The eighth aspect: That their condition, for example, of mentioning the 'differentiae' which are the 'essential distinguishing attributes' along with their distinguishing between the 'essential' and the 'necessary accident' for the essence, is impossible. For there is no distinguisher that is among the properties of the defined term, corresponding to it in generality and specificity, except that one person can make it an essential distinguisher and another can make it a necessary accident for the essence.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection against logicians' theory of definition. The author argues that their requirement to mention 'fusul' (differentiae) as 'dhatiyyat' (essential attributes) that distinguish the species, while also distinguishing between the 'dhati' (essential) and the 'aradi lazim' (necessary accident) for the essence, is impossible in practice. The reason: for any property that is unique to the defined term and coextensive with it, one person can claim it is an essential distinguishing attribute (dhati), while another can claim it is a necessary accident (aradi lazim). There is no objective criterion to settle the dispute. For example, 'laughter' is a property of humans; one could argue it is essential (as a species-specific accident) or a necessary concomitant. This undermines the logicians' claim that definitions must be based on truly essential differentiae.

The Vicious Circle in Definition: Dawr Qabli
Ibn Taymiyya exposes circularity in the logicians' definition theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya identifies a vicious circle in the logicians' theory of definition and apply this critique to a real definition from Usul al-Fiqh.

Core Idea
You cannot know the essence without its parts, nor know the parts without the essence.

Ibn Taymiyya targets the very foundation of the logicians' definition theory. They claim that to define something (the mahdud), you must state its essential attributes (dhatiyyat), and to identify an attribute as essential, you must already know the essence (mahiyya). This sets up a logical circle. In this lesson, we will unpack this circularity and see why it undermines the logicians' method.

Guided Path
1
Step 1: Understand the logicians' two claims
The logicians assert: (1) The defined (mahdud) cannot be conceived without mentioning its essential attributes. (2) An attribute is 'essential' (dhati) if the essence cannot be conceived without it. These two claims are the building blocks of the circle.
2
Step 2: Identify the dependency cycle
From claims (1) and (2) we get a circle: To know the essence, you need the essential attributes. But to know that an attribute is essential (and thus part of the essence), you need to know the essence itself. Each depends on the other.
3
Step 3: Distinguish conceiving vs. knowing as part of
Ibn Taymiyya concedes that one can conceive an attribute (e.g., 'rational') without conceiving the whole (e.g., 'human'). But to know that attribute is part of the essence (i.e., dhati), you must already have conceived the essence. So the circle is about knowing the classification of attributes, not merely conceiving them.
4
Step 4: Recognize the type of circle: dawr qabli
This is a 'prior circle' (dawr qabli) – knowledge of A requires prior knowledge of B, and knowledge of B requires prior knowledge of A. Both are prerequisites for each other, making the acquisition of either impossible from scratch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : أن يقال : هذا التعليم دَوْري قَبْلي، فلا يصح . وذلك أنهم يقولون : إن المحدود لا يتصوّر إلا بذكر صفاته الذاتية. ثم يقولون: «الذاتي» هو ما لا يمكن تصور الماهية بدونه. فيفرقون بين «الذاتي» و «غير الذاتي» أن «الذاتي» ما يَتَوقَّف عليه تصور «الماهية»، فلا بد أن يتصوّر قبلها. فلا يمكن عندهم أن يتأخر تصوره عن تصور «الماهية»، وبذلك يعرف أنه وصف «ذاتي». فحقيقة قولهم أنه لا يعلم الذاتي من غير الذاتي حتى تعلم الماهية، ولا تعلم الماهية حتى تعلم الصفات الذاتية التي منها تؤلف الماهية. وهذا دَور. فإذا كان المتعلم لا يتصور المحدود حتى يتصور صفاته الذاتية، ولا يعرف أن الصفة ذاتية حتى يعلم أنه لا يتصور الموصوف حتى تتصور. فلا يعلم أنها ذاتية حتى يتصور الموصوف، ولا يتصور الموصوف حتى يتصور الصفات الذاتية ويميز بينها وبين غيرها. فإن تصور كون الشيء جزءاً لغيره بدون تصور ذلك الغير ممتنع. ونحن لم نقل أنه لا يتصور الوصف الذاتي حتى يتصور الموصوف، بل الإنسان يتصور أشياء كثيرة ولا يتصور أنها صفات لغيرها ولا أجزاء لها. وإنما قلنا : لا يعلم أنها جزء من الماهية وأنها ذاتية للماهية داخلة في حقيقة الماهية إن لم تتصور الماهية.
The second station - the ninth aspect: That it should be said: This teaching is a priori circular, so it is invalid. That is because they say: The defined term cannot be conceived except by mentioning its essential attributes. Then they say: The 'essential' is that without which the essence cannot be conceived. They distinguish between 'essential' and 'non-essential' by saying that the 'essential' is that upon which the conception of the 'essence' depends, so it must be conceived before it. According to them, its conception cannot be after the conception of the 'essence', and by that it is known that it is an 'essential' description. So the reality of their statement is that one does not know the essential from the non-essential until one knows the essence, and one does not know the essence until one knows the essential attributes from which the essence is composed. This is a circle. So if the learner does not conceive the defined term until he conceives its essential attributes, and does not know that an attribute is essential until he knows that he does not conceive the described until it is conceived, then he does not know that it is essential until he conceives the described, and he does not conceive the described until he conceives the essential attributes and distinguishes them from others. For conceiving that something is a part of something else without conceiving that something else is impossible. And we do not say that one cannot conceive the essential attribute until one conceives the described; rather, man conceives many things without conceiving that they are attributes of others or parts of them. We only say: He does not know that it is a part of the essence and that it is essential to the essence, included in the reality of the essence, unless he conceives the essence.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection: the logicians' theory of definition involves a vicious circle (dawr qabli). The author explains their position: they require that to know the defined (mahdud), one must know its essential attributes (dhatiyyat); and they define 'essential' as that which the essence cannot be conceived without. Thus, to know what is essential, one must already know the essence. This creates a circular dependency: you need the essence to know the essential attributes, and you need the essential attributes to know the essence. The author clarifies that he does not deny conceptualization of attributes without the whole; rather, the problem is that one cannot know that an attribute is part of the essence without prior knowledge of the essence. For example, one can conceive 'rationality' without knowing it is part of the human essence, but to classify it as essential, one must already understand 'human'—hence the circle. This critique shows that the logicians' method cannot be used to arrive at definitions without prior knowledge.

B) To define 'khamr' as 'any intoxicating drink', one needs to know 'intoxicating', which relies on knowing 'khamr'.
C) To define 'iman' as 'belief in heart, speech on tongue, action of limbs', one must first define 'belief'.
D) To define 'zakat' as 'forbidden usury', one needs to know 'usury'.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the logicians' method for defining essences is circular: it requires prior knowledge of the very essence it seeks to define, which makes it impossible as a first step in learning.

The Circularity in the Logicians' Theory of Definition
Ibn Taymiyya exposes circularity in the logicians' account of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity in the logicians' account of definition and explain why it makes real definition impossible, using Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Core Idea
The logicians' definition of 'essential' creates a vicious circle: you need the essence to know the essentials, but you need the essentials to know the essence.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the very foundation of the logicians' theory of definition. He argues that their method for distinguishing essential attributes from accidental ones leads to an unavoidable circularity. Understanding this critique will sharpen your ability to detect flawed definitions in Islamic sciences and beyond.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify the logicians' position: that a thing can only be properly defined by listing its essential attributes (al-dhatiyyat). This is the starting point of the circle.
2
Define 'essential' (al-dhati) as they do
According to the logicians, an attribute is essential if the essence (mahiyya) cannot be conceived without it. This means the attribute must be known before or with the essence.
3
Trace the dependency circle
Notice that to know which attributes are essential, you must already know the essence. But to know the essence, you must know its essential attributes. This mutual dependence is the circularity (dawr).
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that this is not about mere conception, but about knowing that an attribute is part of the essence.
4
Apply the circle to an Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal.' To know that 'rationality' and 'animality' are essential, you must already know the essence of 'human.' But that essence is defined by those very attributes. This shows the circle in practice.
5
Conclude why definition becomes impossible
Since the circle prevents any real knowledge of either the essence or the essentials, the logicians' method cannot yield a valid definition. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that their definitions are arbitrary and not based on reality.

الوجه التاسع: أن يقال: هذا التعليم دَوْري قَبْلي، فلا يصح. وذلك أنهم يقولون: إن المحدود لا يتصور أو لا يُحد حداً حقيقياً إلا بذكر صفاته الذاتية. ثم يقولون: «الذاتي» هو ما لا يمكن تصور الماهية بدون تصوره. فيفرقون بين «الذاتي» و «غير الذاتي» أن «الذاتي» ما يَتَوقَّف عليه تصور «الماهية»، فلا بد أن يتصور قبلها. ويقولون تارة: لا بد أن يتصور معها. فلا يمكن عندهم أن يتأخر تصوره عن تصور «الماهية»، وبذلك يعرف أنه وصف «ذاتي». فحقيقة قولهم أنه لا يعلم «الذاتي» من غير الذاتي حتى تعلم «الماهية»، ولا تعلم «الماهية» حتى تعلم الصفات «الذاتية» - التي منها تؤلف «الماهية». وهذا دور. فإذا كان المتعلم لا يتصور المحدود حتى يتصور صفاته «الذاتية»، ولا يعرف أن الصفة «ذاتية» حتى يعلم أنه لا يتصور الموصوف ـ الذي هو المحدود ـ حتى تتصور . فلا يعلم أنها ذاتية حتى يتصور الموصوف، ولا يتصور الموصوف حتى يتصور الصفات «الذاتية» ويميز بينها وبين غيرها . . . داخل فيها وما هو جزء لها، فإن تصور كون الشيء جزءاً لغيره بدون تصور ذلك الغير ممتنع. ونحن لم نقل أنه لا يتصور الوصف «الذاتي» حتى يتصور الموصوف، بل الإنسان يتصور أشياء كثيرة ولا يتصور أنها صفات لغيرها ولا أجزاء لها، فضلاً عن كونها لازمة ذاتية أو غير لازمة. وإنما قلنا: لا يعلم أنها جزء من «الماهية»، وأنها «ذاتية» لـ «الماهية» ، داخلة في حقيقة «الماهية» ـ إن لم تتصور «الماهية». وإن قيل: إن مجرد تصور الصفات الذاتيات كافٍ في تصور «الماهية» وإن لم يعلم أن تلك الصفات ذاتية، قيل: مِنْ أين يعلم الإنسان أن هذه الصفات هي «الذاتيات» دون غيرها إن لم يعرف الماهية التي هذه الصفات «ذاتية» لها، داخلة فيها؟ ومن أين يعلم إذا تصوّرَ بعض الصفات اللازمة أن هذه «الذاتية» التي تتركب منها الذات دون غيرها، إن لم يعلم «الذات»؟ فيتوقف معرفة الذات - التي هي الماهية - على معرفة «الذاتيات»، وتتوقف معرفة «الذاتيات» ـ أي معرفة كونها هي «الذاتيات» لهذه الماهية دون غيرها من اللوازم - على معرفة «الذات». فيتوقف معرفتها على معرفتها. فلا يُعرف هو ولا يُعرف «الذاتيات». وهذا كلام متين يجتاح أصل كلامهم ويُبيّن أنهم متحكمون فيما وضعوه ـ لم يبنوه على أصل علمي تابع للحقائق ولكن قالوا: هذا ذاتي وهذا غير ذاتي بمجرد التحكّم، ولم يعتمدوا على أمر يمكن الفَرْقُ به بين «الذاتي» وغيره، إذ هذا غير ممكن فيما وضعوه. ويبين أن ما ذكروه مما زعموه أنه صفات «ذاتية» لا يعرف حقيقة الموصوف أصلاً، بل ما ذكروه يستلزم أنه لا يمكن حَدُّه. فإذا لم يعرف المحدود إلا بالحد، والحد غير ممكن، لم يُعرَف. وذلك باطل. مثال ذلك: إذا قدر أنه لا تتصور حقيقة «الإنسان» حتى تتصور صفاته «الذاتية» - التي هي عندهم «الحيوانية» و «الناطقية». وهذه «الحيوانية» و «الناطقية» لا يُعرف أنها صفاته الذاتية دون غيرها حتى يُعرف أن ذاته لا تتصوّر إلا بها، وأن «ذاته» تتصور بها - دون غيرها، ولا يعلم أن ذاته لا تتصور إلا بها حتى تعرف «ذاته». فإن قيل: مجرد تصور «الحيوانية» و «الناطقية» يوجب تصور «الإنسان»، قيل: مجرد تصوره لذلك لا يوجب أن يعلم أن هذا هو «الإنسان» حتى يعلم أن «الإنسان» مركب من ذلك أو هو ذلك.
The ninth aspect: It is said: This instruction is circular and thus invalid. That is because they say: The defined (al-mahdud) is not conceived or defined by a real definition except by mentioning its essential attributes (al-dhatiyyat). Then they say: 'The essential (al-dhati) is that without the conception of which the conception of the essence (al-mahiyya) is impossible.' So they distinguish between the essential and the non-essential by saying that the essential is that upon which the conception of the essence depends, so it must be conceived before it. And they sometimes say: It must be conceived with it. Thus, in their view, its conception cannot come after the conception of the essence, and by that it is known to be an essential attribute. So the reality of their statement is that the essential is not known from the non-essential until the essence is known, and the essence is not known until the essential attributes—from which the essence is composed—are known. This is circularity. For if the learner does not conceive the defined until he conceives its essential attributes, and does not know that an attribute is essential until he knows that the described—which is the defined—is not conceived without it, then he does not know it is essential until he conceives the described, and he does not conceive the described until he conceives the essential attributes and distinguishes between them and others that are included in it and what is a part of it. For conceiving something as a part of another without conceiving that other is impossible. And we did not say that the essential attribute cannot be conceived until the described is conceived; rather, a person conceives many things without conceiving them as attributes of others or parts of them, let alone as essential or non-essential concomitants. We only said: He does not know that it is a part of the essence and that it is essential to the essence, entering into the reality of the essence—if he does not conceive the essence. And if it is said: Mere conception of the essential attributes is sufficient for conceiving the essence, even if he does not know that those attributes are essential, it is said: How does one know that these attributes are the essential ones and not others if he does not know the essence for which these attributes are essential and included in it? And how does he know, if he conceives some necessary attributes, that these are the essential ones from which the essence is composed, without others, if he does not know the essence? Thus, knowledge of the essence—which is the quiddity—depends on knowledge of the essential attributes, and knowledge of the essential attributes—i.e., knowing that they are the essential ones for this essence rather than other concomitants—depends on knowledge of the essence. So knowledge of it depends on knowledge of it. Thus neither it nor the essential attributes are known. This is solid speech that demolishes the foundation of their statement and shows that they are arbitrary in what they set up—they did not build it on a scientific basis following realities, but said 'this is essential' and 'this is non-essential' by mere arbitrariness, and did not rely on something by which one can distinguish between the essential and other, for that is impossible according to what they set up. And it shows that what they mentioned, claiming it to be essential attributes, does not actually reveal the reality of the described; rather, what they mentioned necessitates that it cannot be defined. So if the defined is known only through definition, and definition is impossible, then it is not known. And that is false. An example: If it is supposed that the reality of 'human' is not conceived until one conceives its essential attributes—which according to them are 'animality' and 'rationality.' And these 'animality' and 'rationality' are not known to be its essential attributes rather than others until it is known that its essence is not conceived except through them, and that its essence is conceived by them—to the exclusion of others. And it is not known that its essence is not conceived except through them until its essence is known. So if it is said: Mere conception of 'animality' and 'rationality' necessitates the conception of 'human,' it is said: Mere conception of that does not necessitate that one knows that this is 'human' until one knows that 'human' is composed of that or is that.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) can be given through essential attributes (dhatiyyat). Ibn Taymiyya argues that their account leads to a vicious circularity (dawr). He begins by stating that from the logicians' own premises, the instruction in definitions is circular and therefore invalid. He then explains their position: they hold that a thing cannot be conceived or properly defined except by mentioning its essential attributes. The essential attribute is defined as that without which the conception of the essence (mahiyya) is impossible; it must be conceived either before or along with the essence. From this, Ibn Taymiyya extracts the following circle: one cannot know which attributes are essential without knowing the essence, and one cannot know the essence without knowing its essential attributes. This is a clear circularity (dawr). He then refines the argument by distinguishing between mere conception of an attribute and knowing it to be a part of the essence. A person can conceive of an attribute (e.g., 'rationality') without knowing that it is an essential part of 'human.' The circle is not about conception but about knowledge of essentiality. He anticipates an objection: maybe mere conception of the essential attributes is enough to conceive the essence, even without knowing they are essential. He responds: if one does not know the essence, how can one determine which attributes are essential and which are merely accidental? The determination requires prior knowledge of the essence. He provides a concrete example from the Islamic sciences: the definition of 'human' (al-insan) as 'rational animal' (hayawaniyya wa natiqiyya). To know that 'animality' and 'rationality' are the essential attributes of 'human,' one must already know the essence of 'human.' But that essence is defined by those very attributes, creating a circle. Thus, according to Ibn Taymiyya, the logicians' system for defining essences is arbitrary and leads to impossibility of real definition. He concludes that their definitions are not based on reality but on mere assertion.

Key Terms

الماهية (al-mahiyya)
الحد (al-hadd)
الدور (al-dawr)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the logicians' method for defining essences through essential attributes is inherently circular and therefore cannot yield a valid definition.

5 min
Ibn Taymiyya's circularity objection
Author critiques logicians' requirement to distinguish essential from accidental attributes for definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's circularity argument against the logicians' definition requirement and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
To define by essential attributes, you must already know the essence—a vicious circle.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational claim of logicians: that a real definition (hadd haqiqi) must be composed of essential attributes (dhatiyyat). He exposes a circularity: distinguishing essential from accidental requires prior knowledge of the essence, but the definition is supposed to reveal the essence. In this passage, he contrasts this with a different distinction that is actually accessible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find in the passage the claim that a real definition requires mentioning essential attributes, and that essential attributes are those without which the essence is not conceived.
2
Locate the circularity
Notice Ibn Taymiyya's argument: to know which attributes are essential, you must already know the essence. But the definition is supposed to reveal the essence, creating a circle.
3
Understand his alternative
He contrasts the essential/accidental distinction with the necessary/accidental distinction, which is grounded in reality (fi nafs al-amr) and does not require prior knowledge of the essence.
4
Assess the critique's validity
Consider whether the critique holds: does the necessary/accidental distinction avoid circularity? Why is it 'real'?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا بين عند تأمّل مقصودهم فإنهم يقولون: لا يحدّ الشيء حداً حقيقياً إلا بذكر صفاته الذاتية. فلا بد من الفرق بين صفاته الذاتية والعرضية، والفرق بينهما أن الذاتي ما لا تُتَصوَّرُ الحقيقة إلا به. فإذا كنا لم نعرف الحقيقة لم نعرف الصفات الذاتية من العرضية. وهو المطلوب. وهذا بخلاف الفرق بين الصفات اللازمة والعارضة، فإنه فرق حقيقي ثابت في نفس الأمر.
And this is clear when contemplating their intent, for they say: 'A thing is not defined by a real definition except by mentioning its essential attributes.' So it is necessary to distinguish between its essential attributes and accidental attributes. The difference between them is that the essential is that without which the essence is not conceived. So if we do not know the essence, we do not know the essential attributes from the accidental. And that is the requested. This is unlike the distinction between the necessary attributes and the accidental attributes, for it is a real distinction established in reality.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' claim that a real definition (hadd haqiqi) must be composed of essential attributes (dhatiyyat). Ibn Taymiyya points out a vicious circularity (dawr): to distinguish essential from accidental attributes, one must already know the essence (haqiqah) being defined. But the logicians claim the essence is known through the definition, which requires essential attributes. Thus the distinction presupposes what it seeks to establish. He then contrasts this with the distinction between necessary (lazima) and accidental (arida) attributes, which he says is a real distinction in reality (fi nafs al-amr) and not problematic. The critique is that the logicians have placed a condition (shart) that cannot be fulfilled without circularity.

ذاتي
عرضي
حد حقيقي
دور

A) They need to know the essence to define it, but define it by essential attributes.
B) They confuse essential attributes with accidental ones.
C) They define the thing by its genus and differentia.
D) They use synonyms in definitions.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the logicians' requirement for real definition by essential attributes leads to a vicious circularity, undermining the possibility of hadd haqiqi as they conceived it.

Identifying Circularity (Dawr) in Definitions
Author presents circularity objections against definitions of ʿilm and khabar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the fallacy of circularity (dawr) in definitions, and apply this critique to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Circular definitions argue in a circle—they presuppose what they try to explain.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents well-known objections to definitions of key terms like 'ilm, khabar, and amr. One of the most powerful objections is that many definitions involve circularity (dawr). For example, if you define 'ilm as 'cognizance of the known', the term 'known' depends on 'knowledge', so the definition uses the defined to explain itself. This lesson will teach you how to detect this logical flaw and why it invalidates a definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Locate the term being defined (definiendum) and the words used to define it (definiens) in the passage. For example, in 'al-ʿilm huwa maʿrifat al-maʿlūm', the definiendum is ʿilm and the definiens is maʿrifat al-maʿlūm.
2
Check if any term in the definiens derives from the definiendum
Look for words that share a root with the definiendum or are linguistically or conceptually dependent on it. In our example, al-maʿlūm (the known) is derived from ʿilm (knowledge).
This is a sign of possible circularity.
3
Assess the direction of dependence
Determine whether understanding the definiens requires prior understanding of the definiendum. If yes, the definition is circular because it presupposes what it defines.
Circular definitions provide no independent explanation.
4
Confirm that circularity renders the definition invalid
A valid definition must be informative without relying on the defined term. If circularity exists, the definition fails to elucidate and must be rejected or revised.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this objection to challenge the logicians' definitions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبحاث في حد «العلم» و «الخبر». مثال ذلك: إنهم يتنازعون في حد العلم والأمر والخبر ونحو ذلك الأمور التي شاع الكلام عليها في العلوم المشهورة. فمن النظار من يحدها كما يقولون في حدّ العلم: معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به؛ أو يقولون: اعتقاد المعلوم - على رأي المعتزلة الذين لا يقولون إن الله علماً؛ أو يقولون: العلم ما أوجب لمن قام به أن يكون عالماً؛ ونحو ذلك. ويقال في حدّ الخبر: هو ما احتمل الصدق والكذب؛ أو ما ساغ أن يقال لصاحبه في اللغة صدقت أو كذبت؛ ونحو ذلك. ويعترض على هذه الحدود بالاعتراضات المشهورة. مثل أن يقال: المعلوم مشتق من العلم، ومعرفة المشتق متوقفة على معرفة العلم والخبر المشتق منه. فلو عُرّف المشتق بالمشتق منه لزم الدور. أو يقال: العلم هو المعرفة؛ وهذا حد لفظي. أو يقال: قولك على ما هو به زيادة. ويقال: إدخال الصدق والكذب أو التصديق والتكذيب في حد الخبر لا يصلح، لأنهما نوعا الخبر وتعريفهما إنما يمكن بالخبر. فلو عُرّف الخبر بهما لزم الدور. وأمثال ذلك من الاعتراضات المشهورة على الحدود المشهورة. ومنهم من يقول: هذه الأشياء لا يمكن تحديدها، أو لا يحتاج إلى تحديدها، بل غَنِيّة عن الحد. كما يقال في العلم: إن غير العلم لا يعرف إلا بالعلم. فلو عُرف العلم بغيره لزم الدور. أو يقال: تصور العلم لا يحصل إلا بعلم، وذلك العلم المعين موقوف على العلم. فلو توقف تصور العلم على غيره لزم الدور. أو يقال في تعريف العلم والخبر والأمر: إن كل أحد يعلم جوعه وشبعه، ويعلم أنه عالم بذاك، والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بالمفرد. فإذن كل أحد يعلم العلم، فيكون تصوره بديهياً. ويقال في الأمر والخبر: كل أحد يحس أن يأمر وأن يخبر، ويعلم أن هذا أمر وهذا خبر، والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بالمفرد. فيقال له: العلم بالخبر المعين والعلم المعين والأمر المعين لا يستلزم العلم بالمطلوب بالحد لأن المطلوب بالحد تعريف الحقيقة العامة الجامعة المانعة، وتصور المعين إنما يستلزم تصور الحقيقة مطلقاً، لا بشرط العموم والمطابقة. فإن الحقيقة لا توجد عامة في الأعيان إذ الكليات - بشرط كونها كليات - إنما توجد في الذهن، والعلم بالمعين لا يستلزم العلم بالكلي - بشرط كونه كليا. فإذن ما علمه بالمعين ليس هو المحدود.
Investigations concerning the definition of knowledge, assertion, and command. For example: they dispute over the definition of knowledge, command, assertion, and similar matters that have been widely discussed in the well-known sciences. Some theoreticians define them as they say in defining knowledge: 'Knowledge is the cognizance of the known as it is'; or they say: 'Belief in the known' — according to the view of the Mu'tazila who do not affirm that God has knowledge; or they say: 'Knowledge is that which makes the one who possesses it knowledgeable'; and so on. And regarding the definition of assertion, it is said: 'It is what admits truth and falsehood'; or 'That of which it is permissible to say in language, "You spoke truth" or "You lied"'; and so on. And these definitions are objected to with well-known objections. For example: the known is derived from knowledge, and knowledge of the derived term depends on knowledge of the source term from which it is derived. So if the derived is defined by its source, circularity follows. Or it is said: 'Knowledge is cognizance'; this is a verbal definition. Or it is said: Your statement 'as it is' is redundant. And it is said: Including truth and falsehood, or verification and falsification, in the definition of assertion is not valid, because they are types of assertion and their definition is only possible by assertion. So if assertion is defined by them, circularity follows. And similar well-known objections to the well-known definitions. And some of them say: These things cannot be defined, or do not need definition, rather they are independent of definition. As is said about knowledge: Only knowledge knows what is not knowledge. So if knowledge were defined by something else, circularity would follow. Or it is said: The conception of knowledge is not obtained except by knowledge, and that particular knowledge depends on knowledge. So if the conception of knowledge depended on something else, circularity would follow. Or it is said regarding the definition of knowledge, assertion, and command: Everyone knows their hunger and fullness, and knows that they are knowledgeable about that, and knowledge of the compound is preceded by knowledge of the simple. Therefore everyone knows knowledge, so its conception is self-evident. And it is said about command and assertion: Everyone senses that they command and assert, and knows that this is a command and this is an assertion, and knowledge of the compound is preceded by knowledge of the simple. Then it is said to him: Knowledge of a particular assertion, particular knowledge, and particular command does not entail knowledge of what is sought by definition, because what is sought by definition is the definition of the universal essence that includes and excludes, and the conception of the particular only entails the conception of the essence absolutely, not under the condition of universality and correspondence. For the essence does not exist universally in external things, since universals — under the condition of being universals — only exist in the mind, and knowledge of the particular does not entail knowledge of the universal under the condition of its being universal. So what is known through the particular is not that which is defined.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of definitions (hudud) of key logical and linguistic terms: knowledge ('ilm), assertion (khabar), and command (amr). Ibn Taymiyya presents several proposed definitions from different schools (e.g., Mu'tazila) and then lists standard objections (i'tiradat) against them. The objections highlight circularity (dawr) and verbal definition (hadd lafzi). For example, defining 'ilm as ma'rifah is a verbal synonym, not a real definition. Defining khabar by sidq/kidhb introduces the types of khabar, leading to circularity. Then he presents a stronger argument: these concepts are self-evident (badihi) because people directly know their own knowledge, hunger, etc., and knowledge of compound concepts presupposes knowledge of simple ones. The final part refutes a potential counterargument: knowing a particular instance does not entail knowing the universal essence under the condition of universality, so the defined (al-mahdud) is the universal, not the particular. This shows that self-evidence is not a complete refutation of the need for definition, but the author is using it to argue that the logicians' definitions are unnecessary or impossible.

B) Because 'known' is derived from 'knowledge', so the definition uses a term that depends on the definiendum.
C) Because it includes the phrase 'as it is', which is redundant.
D) Because it does not include a genus and differentia.

Key Takeaway
Circularity (dawr) invalidates a definition because it provides no independent explanation—it moves in a logical circle.

Verbal vs Real Definition (Hadd Lafzi vs Hadd Haqiqi)
Author criticizes defining 'ilm as ma'rifah as a verbal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a verbal definition (hadd lafzi) and a real definition (hadd haqiqi) and identify when a definition is merely verbal.

Core Idea
A verbal definition only swaps synonyms—it does not reveal the essence.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya mentions that defining 'ilm as ma'rifah is a 'verbal definition' (hadd lafzi). This critique points out that the definition does not add any real knowledge; it simply replaces one term with another that means the same thing. This lesson will teach you to recognize verbal definitions and understand why they are insufficient for rigorous reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined and the words used to define it. For example, in 'al-ʿilm huwa al-maʿrifah', the definiendum is ʿilm and the definiens is maʿrifah.
2
Check if the definiens is a synonym or near-synonym
Ask: Are the definiendum and definiens interchangeable in most contexts? If yes, the definition is likely verbal. In Arabic, ʿilm and maʿrifah are often used synonymously in everyday language.
A real definition should add new information, not just repeat the meaning.
3
Determine if the definition provides genus and differentia
A real definition (hadd haqiqi) typically specifies the genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) of the term. If the definition only gives a synonym, it lacks these components and is merely verbal.
For example, a real definition of 'insan' would be 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal), not just 'bashar'.
4
Judge whether the definition is informative
If learning the definiens does not give you a deeper understanding of the definiendum beyond its common usage, it is a verbal definition. A real definition should clarify the essence.
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of 'al-ʿilm huwa al-maʿrifah' highlights its lack of informative value.

Self-Evidence and the Particular-Universal Distinction
Author presents and refutes the argument that 'ilm, khabar, amr are self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the argument that 'ilm, khabar, and amr are self-evident (badihi) and evaluate the counterargument that knowing a particular instance does not give the universal definition.

Core Idea
Knowing one case does not define the universal—the mind must abstract the essence.

The passage presents a debate: some argue that concepts like 'ilm, khabar, and amr are self-evident because everyone knows their own knowledge, commands, etc. Ibn Taymiyya then rebuts this by distinguishing between knowing a particular instance and knowing the universal essence required for definition. This lesson will explore both sides and the logical distinction involved, which is crucial for understanding the limits of self-evidence in definition theory.

Guided Path
1
Understand the self-evidence claim
The claim is that because everyone directly experiences their own knowledge (e.g., knowing they are hungry), the concept of 'ilm is universally known and therefore needs no definition. The same is said for khabar and amr.
This is based on the idea that knowledge of compound concepts is preceded by knowledge of simpler ones.
2
Identify the counterargument
Ibn Taymiyya points out that what is sought by definition is the 'universal essence that includes and excludes' (al-ḥaqīqah al-ʿāmmah al-jāmiʿah al-māniʿah). Knowledge of a particular instance (e.g., 'I know I am hungry') does not automatically give you the universal concept of knowledge under the condition of universality.
The universal exists only in the mind, not in external particulars.
3
Understand the refutation: particular vs universal
Knowing a particular 'ilm (e.g., my knowledge of hunger) gives knowledge of the essence absolutely (without condition), but not the universal qua universal (bi sharṭ kulliyatihi). The definition requires the universal under the condition of universality, which is an abstraction not directly given by particular experience.
Thus, the self-evidence of particulars does not suffice for a definition.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider the concept of 'wajib' (obligatory). Everyone knows that praying five times a day is wajib (a particular). But defining 'wajib' universally (as 'that which is demanded with firmness, etc.') requires abstraction beyond particular instances.
This shows why even seemingly obvious concepts may need careful definition.

أبحاث في حد «العلم» و «الخبر». مثال ذلك: إنهم يتنازعون في حد العلم والأمر والخبر ونحو ذلك الأمور التي شاع الكلام عليها في العلوم المشهورة. فمن النظار من يحدها كما يقولون في حدّ العلم: معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به؛ أو يقولون: اعتقاد المعلوم - على رأي المعتزلة الذين لا يقولون إن الله علماً؛ أو يقولون: العلم ما أوجب لمن قام به أن يكون عالماً؛ ونحو ذلك. ويقال في حدّ الخبر: هو ما احتمل الصدق والكذب؛ أو ما ساغ أن يقال لصاحبه في اللغة صدقت أو كذبت؛ ونحو ذلك. ويعترض على هذه الحدود بالاعتراضات المشهورة. مثل أن يقال: المعلوم مشتق من العلم، ومعرفة المشتق متوقفة على معرفة العلم والخبر المشتق منه. فلو عُرّف المشتق بالمشتق منه لزم الدور. أو يقال: العلم هو المعرفة؛ وهذا حد لفظي. أو يقال: قولك على ما هو به زيادة. ويقال: إدخال الصدق والكذب أو التصديق والتكذيب في حد الخبر لا يصلح، لأنهما نوعا الخبر وتعريفهما إنما يمكن بالخبر. فلو عُرّف الخبر بهما لزم الدور. وأمثال ذلك من الاعتراضات المشهورة على الحدود المشهورة. ومنهم من يقول: هذه الأشياء لا يمكن تحديدها، أو لا يحتاج إلى تحديدها، بل غَنِيّة عن الحد. كما يقال في العلم: إن غير العلم لا يعرف إلا بالعلم. فلو عُرف العلم بغيره لزم الدور. أو يقال: تصور العلم لا يحصل إلا بعلم، وذلك العلم المعين موقوف على العلم. فلو توقف تصور العلم على غيره لزم الدور. أو يقال في تعريف العلم والخبر والأمر: إن كل أحد يعلم جوعه وشبعه، ويعلم أنه عالم بذاك، والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بالمفرد. فإذن كل أحد يعلم العلم، فيكون تصوره بديهياً. ويقال في الأمر والخبر: كل أحد يحس أن يأمر وأن يخبر، ويعلم أن هذا أمر وهذا خبر، والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بالمفرد. فيقال له: العلم بالخبر المعين والعلم المعين والأمر المعين لا يستلزم العلم بالمطلوب بالحد لأن المطلوب بالحد تعريف الحقيقة العامة الجامعة المانعة، وتصور المعين إنما يستلزم تصور الحقيقة مطلقاً، لا بشرط العموم والمطابقة. فإن الحقيقة لا توجد عامة في الأعيان إذ الكليات - بشرط كونها كليات - إنما توجد في الذهن، والعلم بالمعين لا يستلزم العلم بالكلي - بشرط كونه كليا. فإذن ما علمه بالمعين ليس هو المحدود.
Investigations concerning the definition of knowledge, assertion, and command. For example: they dispute over the definition of knowledge, command, assertion, and similar matters that have been widely discussed in the well-known sciences. Some theoreticians define them as they say in defining knowledge: 'Knowledge is the cognizance of the known as it is'; or they say: 'Belief in the known' — according to the view of the Mu'tazila who do not affirm that God has knowledge; or they say: 'Knowledge is that which makes the one who possesses it knowledgeable'; and so on. And regarding the definition of assertion, it is said: 'It is what admits truth and falsehood'; or 'That of which it is permissible to say in language, "You spoke truth" or "You lied"'; and so on. And these definitions are objected to with well-known objections. For example: the known is derived from knowledge, and knowledge of the derived term depends on knowledge of the source term from which it is derived. So if the derived is defined by its source, circularity follows. Or it is said: 'Knowledge is cognizance'; this is a verbal definition. Or it is said: Your statement 'as it is' is redundant. And it is said: Including truth and falsehood, or verification and falsification, in the definition of assertion is not valid, because they are types of assertion and their definition is only possible by assertion. So if assertion is defined by them, circularity follows. And similar well-known objections to the well-known definitions. And some of them say: These things cannot be defined, or do not need definition, rather they are independent of definition. As is said about knowledge: Only knowledge knows what is not knowledge. So if knowledge were defined by something else, circularity would follow. Or it is said: The conception of knowledge is not obtained except by knowledge, and that particular knowledge depends on knowledge. So if the conception of knowledge depended on something else, circularity would follow. Or it is said regarding the definition of knowledge, assertion, and command: Everyone knows their hunger and fullness, and knows that they are knowledgeable about that, and knowledge of the compound is preceded by knowledge of the simple. Therefore everyone knows knowledge, so its conception is self-evident. And it is said about command and assertion: Everyone senses that they command and assert, and knows that this is a command and this is an assertion, and knowledge of the compound is preceded by knowledge of the simple. Then it is said to him: Knowledge of a particular assertion, particular knowledge, and particular command does not entail knowledge of what is sought by definition, because what is sought by definition is the definition of the universal essence that includes and excludes, and the conception of the particular only entails the conception of the essence absolutely, not under the condition of universality and correspondence. For the essence does not exist universally in external things, since universals — under the condition of being universals — only exist in the mind, and knowledge of the particular does not entail knowledge of the universal under the condition of its being universal. So what is known through the particular is not that which is defined.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of definitions (hudud) of key logical and linguistic terms: knowledge ('ilm), assertion (khabar), and command (amr). Ibn Taymiyya presents several proposed definitions from different schools (e.g., Mu'tazila) and then lists standard objections (i'tiradat) against them. The objections highlight circularity (dawr) and verbal definition (hadd lafzi). For example, defining 'ilm as ma'rifah is a verbal synonym, not a real definition. Defining khabar by sidq/kidhb introduces the types of khabar, leading to circularity. Then he presents a stronger argument: these concepts are self-evident (badihi) because people directly know their own knowledge, hunger, etc., and knowledge of compound concepts presupposes knowledge of simple ones. The final part refutes a potential counterargument: knowing a particular instance does not entail knowing the universal essence under the condition of universality, so the defined (al-mahdud) is the universal, not the particular. This shows that self-evidence is not a complete refutation of the need for definition, but the author is using it to argue that the logicians' definitions are unnecessary or impossible.

جزئي
ذهن

C) Because 'ilm is too complex to be self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident familiarity with particulars does not suffice for a universal definition—the mind must abstract the common essence, which is the object of definition.

Refuting Circularity in Defining 'Ilm and Khabar
Ibn Taymiyya refutes objections to defining 'ilm and khabar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the circularity and self-evidence objections against defining 'ilm and khabar by applying Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between particular and universal.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a particular instance does not give knowledge of the universal definition.

Ibn Taymiyya recounts several objections raised by logicians against the definability of 'ilm (knowledge) and khabar (statement). Some argue that defining these terms leads to circularity; others claim they are self-evident and need no definition. Ibn Taymiyya systematically dismantles these objections by clarifying that definition seeks the universal, comprehensive, exclusive reality, whereas our ordinary knowledge is of particular instances. This lesson walks through his refutation, equipping you to defend the possibility of definition in foundational Islamic concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objectors' arguments
Read the first part of the passage where objections are listed: circularity from defining types by genus and vice versa, the claim that non-knowledge is only known through knowledge, the argument that conception of knowledge depends on a particular knowledge, and the idea that everyone already knows what knowledge is, making it self-evident.
Note the structure: each objection targets either circularity (dawr) or self-evidence (badahah).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's central distinction
Find where Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between knowledge of a particular (juz'i) instance and knowledge of the universal (kulli) definition. He says definition seeks al-haqiqa al-amma al-jami'a al-mani'a (the universal comprehensive exclusive reality), which is not obtained from a particular. The particular gives only the absolute (mutlaq) reality, not the universal as such.
This distinction is the key to refuting both circularity and self-evidence objections.
3
Apply the distinction to refute the objections
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya uses this distinction: the circularity objection fails because the term 'ilm in one premise means known things, and in the other means the definition of 'ilm itself—different meanings. The self-evidence objection fails because knowing a particular command or statement does not yield the universal definition.
Ibn Taymiyya also refutes the counter-argument that the absolute is part of the particular, showing that the universal is more extensive and cannot be literally a part.
4
Assess the strength of the refutation
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's response fully addresses each objection. Check for internal consistency: does his own argument avoid circularity? Note that he defines absolute or general knowledge by a particular knowledge, which is not circular because the terms are used in different senses.
The refutation is logically sound and preserves the possibility of defining foundational concepts.

المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : اعتراضات على حدود «العلم» و«الخبر» لا يصلح، لأنهما نوعا «الخبر» وتعريفهما إنما يمكن بـ (الخبر). فلو عُرّف «الخبر» بهما لزم الدور. وأمثال ذلك من الاعتراضات المشهورة على الحدود المشهورة. ومنهم من يقول : هذه الأشياء لا يمكن تحديدها، أو لا يحتاج إلى تحديدها، بل غَنِيّة عن الحد . كما يقال في العلم : إن غير العلم لا يعرف إلا بـ «العلم». فلو عُرف «العلم» بغيره لزم الدور. أو يقال : تصور العلم لا يحصل إلا بـ «علم»، وذلك «العلم المعين» موقوف على العلم». فلو توقف تصور العلم» على غيره لزم الدور. أو يقال في تعريف العلم» و «الخبر» و «الأمر»: إن كل أحد يعلم جُوعَه وشِبَعه ، ويعلم أنه عالم بذاك ، و «العلم» بالمركب مسبوق بـ «العلم» بالمفرد. فإذن كلُّ أحد يعلم العلم»، فيكون تصوره بديهياً. ويقال في «الأمر» و «الخبر» : كل أحد يحس أن يأمر» وأن يخبر»، ويعلم أن هذا أمر» وهذا «خبر»، و «العلم بالمركب مسبوق بـ «العلم» بالمفرد. فيقال له : العلم بـ «الخبر المعين» و«العلم المعين» و «الأمر المعين لا يستلزم العلم بالمطلوب بالحد لأن المطلوب بالحد تعريف الحقيقة العامة الجامعة المانعة، وتصور «المعين» إنما يستلزم تصور الحقيقة مطلقاً، لا بشرط العموم والمطابقة . فإن الحقيقة لا توجد عامة في الأعيان إذ الكليات - بشرط كونها كليات ـ إنما توجد في الذهن، والعلم بـ «المعين لا يستلزم العلم بالكلي - بشرط كونه كليا . فإذن ما علمه بـ «المعيَّن ليس هو المحدود. يوضح ذلك بأن تَصَوَّرَ خبر معين» و «علم معين تصور لأمر جزئي، والمطلوب بالحد هو المعنى الكلي الجامع المانع . قولهم : [فإن قيل : ] يلزم من وجود الجزئي وجود الكلي، أو قيل: المطلق جزء المعين. فإن أريد بذلك أنه يوجد جزء ما معيناً وأن ما هو مطلق» في الذهن إذا وجد في الخارج كان معيناً»، فهذا حق، ولكن لا يفيدهم. وأما إن أريد بذلك أن «المطلق» الذي لا يمنع تصوّره من وقوع الشركة فيه هو نفسه جزء من هذا «المعين» مع كونه كلياً، فهذا مكابرة ظاهرة، ونفس تصوره كاف في العلم بفساده . فإن «الجزئي» من معين خاص. و «الكلي» أعم وأكثر منه . فكيف يكون الكثير بعض القليل؟ وإذا كان المطلوب بالحد هو الكلي الجامع المانع الذي يطابق جميع أفراد المحدود - فلا يخرج عنه شيء ولا يدخل فيه ما ليس منه، فمعلوم أن تصور «المعين» لا يستلزم مثل هذا . وأيضاً فهذا منقوض بسائر المحدودات ـ «الإنسان وغيره مما يتصور جنس مفرداته كل أحد . ويقال : قوله إن غير العلم لا يُعرّف إلا بالعلم، فلو عُرّف العلم بغيره لزم الدور. إن أراد به : المعلوم لا يعرّف إلا بعلم فهذا حق ، لكن «العلم» لايعرف بـ «غير العلم، بل بـ (علم) فيعرف العلم المطلق) أو (العام) بـ ( علم معين. فلفظ «العلم في أحد المقدمتين ليس معناه معنى العلم» في المقدمة الأخرى، فلا يلزم الدور. إذ قول القائل : العلم لا يعرّف إلا بالعلم معناه أن كل معلوم لا يعرف إلا بالعلم .
The second station - the ninth aspect: Objections to the definitions of 'knowledge' and 'statement'. It is not valid because they are types of 'statement', and their definition is only possible through 'statement'. So if 'statement' were defined by them, circularity would result. And similar examples of well-known objections to well-known definitions. And among them are those who say: These things cannot be defined, or do not need to be defined, but are independent of definition. As is said about knowledge: That which is not knowledge is only known through 'knowledge'. So if 'knowledge' were defined by other than it, circularity would result. Or it is said: The conception of knowledge is not obtained except through 'knowledge', and that 'particular knowledge' depends on 'knowledge'. So if the conception of 'knowledge' depended on other than it, circularity would result. Or it is said in defining 'knowledge', 'statement', and 'command': Everyone knows his hunger and satiety, and knows that he is knowledgeable of that, and knowledge of the composite is preceded by knowledge of the simple. Therefore everyone knows 'knowledge', so its conception is self-evident. And it is said about 'command' and 'statement': Everyone senses that he commands and that he informs, and knows that this is a command and this is a statement, and knowledge of the composite is preceded by knowledge of the simple. So it is said to him: Knowledge of the particular statement, the particular knowledge, and the particular command does not entail knowledge of what is sought by definition, because what is sought by definition is the definition of the universal comprehensive exclusive reality. Conception of the particular only entails conception of the absolute reality, not qualified by universality and correspondence. For reality does not exist as universal in external entities, since universals - qualified as universals - only exist in the mind, and knowledge of the particular does not entail knowledge of the universal - qualified as universal. So what he knows by the particular is not the defined. He clarifies that by saying: The conception of a particular statement and particular knowledge is a conception of a particular thing, while what is sought by definition is the universal comprehensive exclusive meaning. Their statement [if it is said]: The existence of the particular entails the existence of the universal, or it is said: The absolute is part of the particular. If what is meant by that is that a particular part exists and that what is absolute in the mind, when it exists externally, becomes particular - this is true, but it does not benefit them. But if what is meant by that is that the 'absolute' whose conception does not prevent participation is itself a part of this 'particular' while being universal, then this is an obvious falsehood, and its very conception is sufficient for knowing its corruption. For the particular is a specific particular, and the universal is more general and more extensive than it. So how can the many be part of the little? And since what is sought by definition is the universal comprehensive exclusive that corresponds to all individuals of the defined - so that nothing exits from it nor something not from it enters - it is known that the conception of the particular does not entail such a thing. And also, this is refuted by all other definienda - 'human' and others whose individual genus everyone conceives. And it is said: His statement that non-knowledge is only known through knowledge - so if knowledge were defined by other than it, circularity would result. If he means by it: The known is only known through knowledge - this is true, but 'knowledge' is not defined by 'non-knowledge', but by 'knowledge', so absolute or general knowledge is defined by a particular knowledge. So the word 'knowledge' in one premise does not have the same meaning as 'knowledge' in the other premise, so circularity does not follow. For the statement of the speaker: Knowledge is only defined by knowledge means that every known thing is only known through knowledge.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of common objections raised by logicians against the definability of concepts like knowledge (ilm) and statement (khabar). He first lists the objections, then systematically refutes them.

The objections include: (1) circularity if types are defined by their genus and vice versa; (2) the claim that these concepts are indefinable or need no definition; (3) the argument that non-knowledge can only be known through knowledge, so defining knowledge by non-knowledge is circular; (4) that conception of knowledge depends on a particular knowledge, leading to circularity; (5) that everyone intuitively knows what knowledge, statement, and command are, making them self-evident. 

Ibn Taymiyya's core response is that knowledge of a particular instance (like a particular statement) does not amount to knowledge of the universal definition. Definition seeks the universal, comprehensive, exclusive reality (al-haqiqa al-amma al-jami'a al-mani'a). Conception of a particular yields only the absolute reality, not the universal qualified as such. Universals exist only in the mind, not externally. 

He then addresses a counter-argument that the existence of a particular entails the existence of the universal, or the absolute is part of the particular. He distinguishes: if they mean the absolute in the mind becomes particular externally, that is true but irrelevant. But if they claim that the universal itself (which does not prevent participation) is a part of the particular while remaining universal, that is absurd because the universal is more general and extensive than the particular. 

Finally, he refutes the circularity objection about defining knowledge by non-knowledge. The phrase "knowledge is only known through knowledge" can mean known things require knowledge (true), but defining knowledge itself does not require another genus; rather, the absolute knowledge is defined by a particular knowledge. The term 'knowledge' in the two premises has different meanings, so no circularity occurs. This passage demonstrates Ibn Taymiyya's method of dismantling logical objections by clarifying the distinction between particular and universal concepts.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: The Definition Must Be Universal
Author refutes the claim that the particular implies the universal in definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that the particular implies the universal in definition.

Core Idea
A definition must be universal; a particular cannot serve as a definition.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' assumption that a particular concept can somehow imply or be part of a universal definition. He carefully distinguishes between two senses of the claim, showing that one is trivial and the other contradictory. This lesson sharpens your ability to spot such logical missteps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians claim that the existence of a particular implies the existence of the universal, or that the absolute (universal) is part of the specific (particular). Note the exact phrasing in the Arabic.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
Ibn Taymiyya separates two meanings: (a) that an absolute concept in the mind, when instantiated externally, becomes particular – this is true but irrelevant to definition; (b) that the universal concept itself is part of the particular – this is the problematic claim.
3
Analyze the refutation of the second interpretation
He argues that the universal is more general (aʿamm) and includes more individuals, so it cannot be a part of the particular, which is less general. This is a whole-part argument: how can the many be part of the little?
4
Apply the criteria of definition
Recall that a proper definition (hadd) must be universal: it must include all individuals of the defined and exclude everything else. A particular concept cannot meet this requirement, so it cannot serve as a definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوضح ذلك بأن تَصَوَّرَ خبر معين» و «علم معين تصور لأمر جزئي، والمطلوب بالحد هو المعنى الكلي الجامع المانع . قولهم : [فإن قيل : ]يلزم من وجود الجزئي وجود الكلي، أو قيل: المطلق جزء جزء المعين المعين. فإن أريد بذلك أنه يوجد جزء ما معيناً وأن ما هو مطلق» في الذهن إذا وجد في الخارج كان معيناً»، فهذا حق، ولكن لا يفيدهم. وأما إن أريد بذلك أن «المطلق» الذي لا يمنع تصوّره من وقوع الشركة فيه هو نفسه جزء من هذا «المعين» مع كونه كلياً، فهذا مكابرة ظاهرة، ونفس تصوره كاف في العلم بفساده . فإن «الجزئي) من معين خاص. و «الكلي» أعم وأكثر منه . فكيف يكون الكثير بعض القليل؟ وإذا كان المطلوب بالحد هو الكلي الجامع المانع الذي يطابق جميع أفراد المحدود - فلا يخرج عنه شيء ولا يدخل فيه ما ليس منه، فمعلوم أن تصور «المعين» لا يستلزم مثل هذا . وأيضاً فهذا منقوض بسائر المحدودات ـ «الإنسان وغيره مما يتصور جنس مفرداته كل أحد .
He clarifies that by 'conception of a specific report' and 'specific knowledge' is a conception of a particular matter, while what is sought by definition (hadd) is the universal meaning that includes and excludes. Their statement: [If it is said:] The existence of the particular necessitates the existence of the universal, or it is said: The absolute is a part of the specific. If what is meant by that is that there exists some specific part and that what is absolute in the mind, if it exists externally, is specific, then this is true but does not benefit them. But if what is meant by that is that the 'absolute' whose conception does not prevent sharing is itself a part of this 'specific' while being universal, then this is blatant obstinacy, and its very conception is sufficient to know its corruption. For the particular is from a specific thing, and the universal is more general and more than it. So how can the many be part of the little? And since what is sought by the definition is the universal that includes and excludes, which corresponds to all individuals of the defined — nothing exiting from it and nothing entering that is not from it — it is known that the conception of the 'specific' does not entail such a thing. Also, this is refuted by the rest of the defined things — 'human' and others, every common person conceives their genus.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a critical explanation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim about definition. He begins by stating that the definition (hadd) must be a universal meaning that encompasses all individuals and excludes others, not a particular. Then he presents the logicians' possible objection that the existence of a particular implies the existence of the universal, or that the absolute (universal) is part of the particular. He refutes this by distinguishing between two interpretations: if they mean that an absolute concept in the mind, when instantiated externally, becomes particular, that is true but irrelevant; if they mean that the universal concept itself is a part of the particular, that is absurd because the universal is more general and cannot be a part of the less general. He further argues that if the definition aims at a universal, then a particular concept cannot achieve that. He concludes by noting that this error is refuted by all other defined things, like 'human', whose genus everyone can conceive.

Key Terms
حد
كلي
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the claim that the universal is part of the particular false?
A) Because the particular is more specific than the universal
B) Because the universal is more general and cannot be part of the less general
C) Because the universal exists outside the mind
D) Because the particular cannot be conceived without the universal

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that a definition must be universal, and the attempt to ground universality in particulars is either trivial or absurd.

Take a contemporary fiqh issue (e.g., cryptocurrency) and construct a universal definition of 'money' that would determine its legal status. Then check whether any particular instance can serve as the definition.
?
Identify a case in kalām where a particular example is mistakenly used as a definition for a universal concept, and explain why it fails using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.

Breaking Circularity: Conception vs Acquisition
Author presents two answers to circularity in defining knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between husul (acquisition) and tasawwur (conception) in definitions and apply this distinction to resolve apparent circularity in defining knowledge.

Core Idea
Circularity is broken when definition requires conception, not acquisition.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a key answer to the charge that defining knowledge leads to circularity. The answer hinges on a crucial distinction: the acquisition of knowledge (husul) versus the mental conception of a concept (tasawwur). Understanding this difference allows us to see how defining knowledge by something other than itself does not necessarily lead to circularity, as long as the definition aims at conception, not acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity charge
Find the statement that defining knowledge leads to circularity because non-knowledge requires knowledge.
The charge is that if we define knowledge by non-knowledge, then non-knowledge requires knowledge, which is circular.
2
Extract the two types: husul and tasawwur
Notice that the first answer distinguishes between the acquisition of knowledge (husul) and the conception of knowledge (tasawwur). The passage says the answer is by non-knowledge, not on the conception of knowledge.
Husul refers to the process of obtaining knowledge; tasawwur refers to the mental grasp of a concept.
3
Apply the distinction
The definition of knowledge requires conception of the definiendum, not acquisition. The conception of non-knowledge depends on acquisition of knowledge of other things, but not on conception of knowledge itself. Hence no circularity.
4
Test with an Islamic definition
Consider defining 'ilm (knowledge) in usul al-fiqh as 'al-yaqin al-mutabiq li al-waqi' (certainty matching reality). This definition provides a conception of knowledge, not an acquisition of that certainty. So it does not require prior knowledge of the defined term.
This shows how the distinction works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدور. ويقال : قوله إن غير العلم لا يُعرّف إلا بالعلم، فلو عُرّف العلم بغيره لزم الدور. إن أراد به : المعلوم لا يعرّف إلا بعلم فهذا حق ، لكن «العلم» لايعرف بـ «غير العلم، بل بـ (علم) فيعرف العلم المطلق) أو (العام) بـ ( علم معين. فلفظ «العلم في أحد المقدمتين ليس معناه معنى العلم)) في المقدمة الأخرى، فيلزم الدور. إذ قول القائل : العلم لا يعرّف إلا بالعلم معناه أن كل معلوم لا يعرف إلا بالعلم . المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : جوابان لهم في انقطاع الدور. وقوله «فلو عُرّف العلم بغيره لزم الدور. يقال: المعرَّفُ المحدود ليس علماً هو ب معلوم معين، بل هو العلم الكلي ، و الكلي يعلم بـ علم جزئي مقيد». ولا منافاة في ذلك، كما يخبر عن الخبر بخبر ، فيخبر عن الخبر المطلق أو العام» بـ «خبر معين خاص . الأول (1) وقول القائل في الجواب : إن توقف تصوّر غير العلم على حصول العلم» الجواب بغيره - أي بـ «غير العلم - لا على تصور العلم ـ فانقطع الدور ، هو أحد الجوابين . فإن حصول العلم بكل معلوم يتوقف على حصول العلم المعيَّن»، لا على «تصوره»، والمطلوب بالحد تصوّر العلم - لا حصول العلم - بتلك المعلومات . الآخر والجواب الآخر أن يقال : تصور غير العلم مما يتوقف على حصول علم بذلك الجواب المعلوم، لا على حصول العلم المحدود» - وهو الجامع المانع - والمطلوب بالحد هو هذا. فلو قُدّر أن حصول تصور غير العلم موقوف على العلم بذلك المعلوم» وعلى ذلك العلم لم يكن هذا مانعاً من الحاجة إلى الحد فكيف وقد يقال : مَنْ علم الشيء قد يعلم أنه عالم، ويعود ما ذكر (۲) تصور · وإيضاح هذا أن في الخارج أموراً معينة» كـ الإنسان المعيَّن» و «العلم المعيَّن» و «الخبر المعين»، وقد يتصور الإنسان أمراً مطلقاً» كـ «الإنسان المطلق» و «العلم المطلق و الخبر المطلق». وهذا المطلق قد يُراد به المطلق بشرط له إطلاق وهو الذي يقال إنه كل عقل»، وقد يراد به المطلق لا بشرط وهو الذي يسمى «الكلي الطبيعي . وهذا الكلي المطلق لا بشرط قد يتنازعون هل هو موجود في الخارج أم لا؟
Circularity. And it is said: His statement that non-knowledge is not defined except by knowledge, so if knowledge were defined by other than it, circularity would follow. If he means by that: the known is not defined except by knowledge, then this is true, but 'knowledge' is not defined by 'non-knowledge', rather by 'knowledge'. Thus, absolute or general knowledge is defined by specific knowledge. So the word 'knowledge' in one premise does not have the same meaning as 'knowledge' in the other premise, so circularity follows. For the speaker's statement: 'Knowledge is not defined except by knowledge' means that every known is not defined except by knowledge. The second station - the ninth aspect: two answers for them in breaking the circularity. And his statement: 'If knowledge were defined by other than it, circularity would follow.' It is said: The defined (al-mu'arraf) is not knowledge that is a specific known, but rather it is universal knowledge, and the universal is known by specific restricted knowledge. And there is no contradiction in that, just as one reports a report by a report: one reports about absolute or general report by a specific particular report. The first answer: And the speaker's statement in the answer: 'The dependence of the conception of non-knowledge on the acquisition of knowledge' — the answer is by other than it — i.e., by non-knowledge — not on the conception of knowledge — thus the circularity is broken. This is one of the two answers. For the acquisition of knowledge of every known depends on the acquisition of specific knowledge, not on its conception, and what is sought by the definition is the conception of knowledge — not the acquisition of knowledge — of those known things. The other answer: And the other answer is that it is said: The conception of non-knowledge depends on the acquisition of knowledge of that known thing, not on the acquisition of defined knowledge — which is the universal including and excluding — and what is sought by the definition is this. So if it were assumed that the acquisition of the conception of non-knowledge depends on the knowledge of that known thing and on that knowledge, this would not prevent the need for the definition. How? And it may be said: Whoever knows a thing may know that he is knowledgeable, and what was mentioned returns to conception. And clarification of this: In the external world there are specific matters, such as the specific 'human', specific 'knowledge', and specific 'report'. One may conceive of a thing as absolute, such as 'absolute human', 'absolute knowledge', and 'absolute report'. This absolute may be intended as absolute with the condition of absoluteness, which is said to be a whole intellect, or it may be intended as absolute without condition, which is called the natural universal. And this absolute universal without condition — do they disagree whether it exists externally or not?
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage deals with the problem of circularity in defining knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya first presents the charge that if knowledge is defined by something other than knowledge, that would lead to a circular definition. Then he offers two answers from the logicians to break the circularity. The first answer distinguishes between the acquisition (husul) of knowledge and the conception (tasawwur) of knowledge: definition requires conception, not acquisition, so defining non-knowledge by non-knowledge does not require prior knowledge of the definiendum. The second answer distinguishes between universal knowledge (the definiendum) and particular knowledge of a specific known: the definition of universal knowledge does not require prior knowledge of that universal but only particular knowledge. He then adds a clarification about absolute and particular, noting that the absolute universal (kulli tabi'i) is a matter of dispute regarding its external existence.

C) Because 'ilm is defined by itself
D) Because circularity is unavoidable

Key Takeaway
The first answer resolves the circularity charge by distinguishing between the acquisition of knowledge (husul) and the conception of knowledge (tasawwur), asserting that definition targets conception.
