Breaking Circularity: Universal Defined by Particular
Author presents two answers to circularity in defining knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how defining universal knowledge by particular knowledge avoids circularity and identify the analogy of reporting a report.

Core Idea
Universal knowledge is defined via particular instances without requiring prior knowledge of the universal itself.

The second answer to the circularity problem involves the distinction between universal and particular knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya argues that what is being defined is universal knowledge (al-'ilm al-kulli), and this universal is known through particular, restricted knowledge. Just as one can report about an absolute report by a specific report, one can define universal knowledge by a particular instance. This avoids circularity because the definiendum is not the same as the definiens.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
In the passage, the definiendum is 'universal knowledge' (al-'ilm al-kulli), not a specific known. The passage says: 'the defined is not knowledge that is a specific known, but rather it is universal knowledge'.
2
Find the definiens
The definition uses 'particular knowledge' (al-'ilm al-juz'i) to define the universal. The passage states: 'the universal is known by specific restricted knowledge'.
The definiens is a particular instance, not the same universal.
3
Understand the analogy
The author compares this to reporting about a report: one can report about the absolute report (al-khabar al-mutlaq) by a specific report (khabar mu'ayyan). This shows that the universal can be defined by a particular without circularity.
Just as the absolute report is known through a specific report, universal knowledge is known through particular knowledge.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Define 'fiqh' (jurisprudence) as 'knowledge of practical shariah rulings from detailed evidences'. This is a universal definition. It is known through particular instances of rulings, not by having prior knowledge of the universal 'fiqh' itself.
The definition does not require prior conception of the universal 'fiqh'.

Breaking Circularity in Defining Knowledge
Two answers to break circularity in defining 'ilm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two answers to the circularity objection in defining 'ilm and explain how each breaks the circle.

Core Idea
Circularity in defining knowledge is avoided by distinguishing absolute from specific knowledge.

One common objection to defining 'ilm is that any definition will be circular because understanding the definiens already requires knowledge. This passage from the critique of the logicians presents two sophisticated answers that break the circle. You will learn how to distinguish different senses of 'knowledge' to avoid the logical pitfall of dawr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Find the statement of the circularity objection: if knowledge is defined by something other than itself, a circle arises because understanding that other thing presupposes knowledge. Underline the key terms.
The objection is introduced by 'فلو عُرّف العلم بغيره لزم الدور'.
2
Analyze the First Answer
The first answer distinguishes between absolute knowledge (العلم الكلي) and specific knowledge (العلم المعين). The definiendum is absolute knowledge, which is known through a specific, restricted knowledge. This breaks the circle because the absolute is not the same as the specific, and the specific knowledge does not require prior definition of absolute knowledge.
Notice the analogy with reporting about a general report through a specific report.
3
Analyze the Second Answer
The second answer distinguishes between the conception of knowledge (تصور العلم) and the obtaining of knowledge (حصول العلم). The definition aims at conception, not obtaining. The conception of something other than knowledge depends on obtaining specific knowledge, not on conception of the defined knowledge. Since the definition is about conception, the circle is broken.
The author uses 'تصور' vs 'حصول' to make the distinction.
4
Compare and Evaluate
Compare the two answers. Both break the circle but by different distinctions. The first uses ontological distinction (kulli vs mu'ayyan), the second uses epistemological distinction (conception vs obtaining). Consider whether both are equally effective or if one is more fundamental.
The author does not pick a favorite; both are presented as valid refutations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : جوابان لهم في انقطاع الدور

وقوله «فلو عُرّف العلم بغيره لزم الدور. يقال: المعرَّفُ المحدود ليس علماً هو ب معلوم معين، بل هو العلم الكلي ، و الكلي يعلم بـ علم جزئي مقيد». ولا منافاة في ذلك، كما يخبر عن الخبر بخبر ، فيخبر عن الخبر المطلق أو العام» بـ «خبر معين خاص .

وقول القائل في الجواب : إن توقف تصوّر غير العلم على حصول العلم» الجواب بغيره - أي بـ «غير العلم - لا على تصور العلم ـ فانقطع الدور ، هو أحد الجوابين . فإن حصول العلم بكل معلوم يتوقف على حصول العلم المعيَّن»، لا على «تصوره»، والمطلوب بالحد تصوّر العلم - لا حصول العلم - بتلك المعلومات .

والجواب الآخر أن يقال : تصور غير العلم مما يتوقف على حصول علم بذلك المعلوم، لا على حصول العلم المحدود» - وهو الجامع المانع - والمطلوب بالحد هو هذا. فلو قُدّر أن حصول تصور غير العلم موقوف على العلم بذلك المعلوم» وعلى ذلك العلم لم يكن هذا مانعاً من الحاجة إلى الحد فكيف وقد يقال : مَنْ علم الشيء قد يعلم أنه عالم، ويعود ما ذكر
The second station - the ninth aspect: Two answers for them in breaking the circularity.

And his statement: 'If knowledge were defined by something other than itself, circularity would follow. It is said: The defined, the limited, is not knowledge as a specific known thing, rather it is absolute knowledge, and the absolute is known by a specific, restricted knowledge.' And there is no contradiction in that, just as one reports about a report by a report, so he reports about the absolute or general report by a specific, particular report.

And the statement of the speaker in the answer: 'The dependence of the conception of something other than knowledge upon obtaining knowledge - the answer is by something other than it - i.e., by something other than knowledge - not upon the conception of knowledge - so the circularity is broken' - that is one of the two answers. For obtaining knowledge of every known thing depends on obtaining specific knowledge, not on its conception, and what is required by the definition is the conception of knowledge - not the obtaining of knowledge - by those known things.

And the other answer is to say: The conception of something other than knowledge depends on obtaining knowledge of that known thing, not on obtaining the defined knowledge - which is the comprehensive, restrictive [definition] - and what is required by the definition is this. So if it were supposed that the obtaining of the conception of something other than knowledge is dependent on knowledge of that known thing and on that knowledge, this would not prevent the need for the definition; how then could it be said: whoever knows something may know that he knows, and what was mentioned returns.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents two answers to the objection that defining 'ilm (knowledge) leads to circularity. The objection states that if knowledge is defined by something other than itself, a circle arises because understanding that other thing already requires knowledge. The first answer distinguishes between absolute knowledge (al-'ilm al-kulli) and specific knowledge (al-'ilm al-mu'ayyan). The definiendum is absolute knowledge, which is known through a specific, particular knowledge. This is analogous to reporting about a general report through a specific report—no contradiction. The second answer argues that the conception of something other than knowledge depends on obtaining specific knowledge of that thing, not on obtaining the defined knowledge (the comprehensive, restrictive definition). The definition aims at the conception of knowledge, not its obtaining. Thus, the circle is broken. The author notes that even if there were dependence, it would not necessarily invalidate the definition; yet there are further considerations.

Key Terms
al-'ilm al-kulli
al-'ilm al-mu'ayyan
al-dawr

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what kind of knowledge is the definiendum in defining 'ilm?
A) Specific knowledge of a particular thing
B) Absolute or universal knowledge
C) The obtaining of knowledge
D) The conception of non-knowledge

Key Takeaway
Circularity in defining knowledge is avoided by distinguishing absolute knowledge from specific knowledge, or conception from obtaining, allowing definition without presupposing what is defined.

The Absolute Exists Only as Individualized in the External World
Author resolves the dispute on the existence of the absolute in the external world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's decisive statement (fasl al-khitab) on the existence of the absolute and explain why it resolves the dispute between those who say the absolute is part of the specific and those who deny it.

Core Idea
The universal exists externally only as a restricted particular, not as an independent entity.

Ibn Taymiyya delves into a metaphysical dispute: does the absolute (like 'humanity') exist in the external world as a separate universal, or only as individualized within specific things? He refines the second answer by clarifying that while the mind conceives of universals, they have no independent external reality; they exist only in a restricted, particularized form. This lesson walks through his argument and resolution, which is crucial for understanding Islamic critiques of realist metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key entities: absolute and specific
First, note that the author distinguishes between external specific things (e.g., a specific man, knowledge) and mental absolutes (e.g., absolute man, absolute knowledge). The absolute can be taken with the condition of absoluteness (bishart shay') or without condition (la bishart, the natural universal).
2
Recognize the dispute on the status of the absolute
Some claim the absolute is part of the specific (e.g., humanity is a part of a particular human). Others object: the many (universal) cannot be part of the few (particular), since the universal encompasses many individuals while the particular is restricted.
3
Grasp the decisive statement (fasl al-khitab)
The author resolves: nothing external is absolutely general. When a specific particular exists, the absolute (e.g., humanity) exists in it only as a restricted, qualified property, not as an absolute universal. So the absolute is part of the specific only if it is not considered as absolutely general.
4
Apply this resolution to the conception of knowledge
Everything known is known by specific knowledge. This specific knowledge contains of the absolute knowledge, but only in a restricted way. This supports the second answer: we do not need an absolutely defined universal knowledge to know a specific thing.

والتحقيق أنه يوجد في الخارج - لكن معيّناً مشخصاً». فلا يوجد في الخارج إلا إنسان معين وفيه حيوانية معينة و ناطقية معينة، ولا يوجد فيه إلا علم معين» و «خبر معين .

ومن الناس من ينكر هذا ويقول : المطلق «الكلي هو الذي لا يمنع تصور معناه من وقوع الشركة فيه، فيجوز أن تدخل فيه أفراد كثيرة و المعين» هو جزئي يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . فكيف يكون الكثير» بعض «القليل»؟ وكيف يكون «العام» أو «المطلق» - الذي يتناول أفراداً كثيرة أو يصلح للتناول - جزءاً من «المعين الجزئي الذي لا يتناول إلا ذلك الشخص الجزئي الموجود في الخارج؟

فصل الخطاب

وفصل الخطاب : أنه ليس في الخارج إلا جزئي معين، ليس في الخارج ما هو مطلق عام مع كونه مطلقاً عاماً». وإذا وجد المعين الجزئي فَـ «الإنسان» و «الحيوان وجدت فيه إنسانية معينة مختصة مقيَّدة غير عامة ولا مطلقة». فـ «المطلق» إذا قيل هو جزء من المعين) فإنما يكون جزءاً» بشرط أن لا يكون مطلقاً عاماً». والمعنى أن ما يتصوره الذهنُ مطلقاً عاماً يوجد في الخارج، لكن لا يوجد إلا مقيداً خاصاً». وهذا كما إذا قيل : إن ما في نفسي وُجد» أو «فعل» أو «فعلتُ ما في نفسي فمعناه أن الصور الذهنيةَ وُجِدَت في الخارج، أي/ وُجد ما يطابقها . إذا عُرف هذا فكل ما يعلم إنما يعلم بـ علم معين . وهذا «العلم المعين فيه من العلم المطلق العام، ليس فيه علم مطلق عام» مع كونه مطلقاً عاماً». والإنسان إذا تصور هذا الإنسان» و «هذا العلم و هذا الخبر» لم يتصوره مطلقاً عاماً، وإنما تَصَوَّره معيناً خاصاً».

وذلك لا يستلزم أن يعلم المحدود الكلي الجامع المانع، الشامل لجميع الأفراد،
And clarification of this is that in external existence there are specific things, like a specific man, specific knowledge, and a specific report. And a person may conceive of an absolute thing, like absolute man, absolute knowledge, and absolute report. This absolute may be intended as the absolute with the condition of its absoluteness, which is said to be 'every intellect', and it may be intended as the absolute without condition, which is called the 'natural universal'. And this absolute universal without condition—they dispute whether it exists in the external world or not.

And the verified truth is that it exists in the external world—but as a specific, individualized thing. So nothing exists in the external world except a specific man, and in him there is a specific animality and a specific rationality, and there is nothing there except specific knowledge and a specific report.

Then among people are those who say that the absolute is part of the specific. And they say: the general is part of the particular. For wherever this man and this animal exist, the meaning of 'man' and the meaning of 'animal' exist, which is the absolute.

And among people are those who deny this and say: The absolute - the universal - is that whose conception does not prevent sharing in it, so it is possible for many individuals to fall under it. And the specific is a particular that prevents its conception from sharing in it. So how can the many be part of the few? And how can the general or absolute - which encompasses many individuals or is suitable for encompassing - be part of the specific particular, which encompasses only that particular individual existing in the external world?

The decisive statement

And the decisive statement: There is nothing in the external world except a specific particular. There is nothing in the external world that is absolutely general while being absolutely general. And when the specific particular exists, 'man' and 'animal' exist in it as a specific, restricted, qualified humanity and animality that are not general nor absolute. So when it is said that the absolute is part of the specific, it is part only on the condition that it is not absolutely general. The meaning is that what the mind conceives as absolutely general exists in the external world, but only as restricted and particular. This is like when it is said: 'What was in my soul came into existence' or 'I did what was in my soul'—the meaning is that the mental forms came into existence in the external world, i.e., what corresponds to them came to exist. If this is known, then everything that is known is only known by specific knowledge. And this specific knowledge contains of the absolute, general knowledge—but it does not contain absolutely general knowledge while being absolutely general. And when a person conceives of this man, this knowledge, and this report, he does not conceive of them as absolutely general, but rather he conceives of them as specifically particular.

And that does not necessitate that he knows the defined, comprehensive, restrictive universal that encompasses all individuals.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage elucidates the second answer by examining the relationship between absolute (mutlaq/general) and specific (mu'ayyan/particular) in external existence. The author states that in the external world, only specific things exist—e.g., a specific human, specific knowledge. The absolute (like absolute humanity) exists externally only as individualized within a specific thing, not as an independent universal. There is a dispute: some say the absolute is part of the specific (e.g., humanity is part of a particular human), while others object that the many cannot be part of the few. The decisive statement (fasl al-khitab) resolves that nothing external is absolutely general; rather, what the mind conceives as general exists externally in a restricted, particular form. Therefore, when we know something, we know it through specific knowledge, which contains the absolute only in a restricted manner. This supports the second answer: the conception of something other than knowledge depends on specific knowledge of that thing, not on absolute defined knowledge. The definition of absolute knowledge is not needed for that.

المعين
الكلي الطبيعي
فصل الخطاب

Ibn Taymiyya's Double Dilemma Against Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues definitions are impossible on logicians' principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's double dilemma argument against the possibility of definitions on the logicians' own principles.

Core Idea
Definitions are impossible on logicians' principles because both affirming and denying them lead to contradictions.

Ibn Taymiyya delivers a powerful critique: the logicians' own criteria for definitions (hudud) make them unattainable. He argues that knowledge is always through particulars, not universals, and then presents a dilemma: if definitions are needed, they are impossible to achieve; if they are not needed, the logicians' system collapses. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logicians' claim about definitions
The logicians claim that definitions (hudud) of universal concepts are necessary for certain knowledge. A proper definition must be comprehensive and exclusive. Identify this claim in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya refers to the 'universal comprehensive exclusive limited (mahdud)'.
This is the target of the critique.
2
Identify the primary epistemological objection
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that knowledge does not depend on universal definitions. Knowledge of anything is only through particular instances. For example, knowledge of 'inference' (nazar) comes from a specific inference known by necessity, not from a universal definition.
The specific is prior to the universal in knowledge.
3
Trace the double dilemma
On the logicians' principles: if they say things have definitions, those definitions are always open to objections; if they say things do not need definitions, they contradict their own requirement that definitions are necessary. Thus, neither position is tenable on their own grounds.
The dilemma shows the impossibility of definitions.
4
Apply the conclusion to the logicians' system
Since knowledge of things exists but definitions are impossible on their principles, the logicians' position is false. Man is left unable to either affirm or deny definitions, which is absurd.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : لا يمكن تعريف الأشياء على أصلهم   المانع من دخول غيرها فيه . فـ غير العلم إذا توقف على حصول العلم» فإنما توقف على حصول علم معين جزئي»، لا على تصوره»، كما قيل في الجواب الأول . فلو قيل : إنه موقوف أيضاً على تصوره»، لكان موقوفاً على تصور أمر جزئي معين، لا على تصور المحدود المطلق الجامع المانع فإن تصور هذا الكليَّ الجامع المانع لا يتوقف عليه وجود علم بشيء من الأشياء، ولا يتوقف على وجوده، ولا على تصوره. فلا يتوقف العلم بشيء من الأشياء على المحدود ـ وهو «العلم الجامع المانع»، ولا على الحد - وهو العلم بهذا الجامع المانع. فلا يقف لا على «تصوره»، ولا على «وجوده» . وكذلك ما ذكروه في إثبات «النظر» بـ «النظر»، يقال فيه : تعلم صحة «جنس النظر» بـ «نظر معين و «النظر المعين تعلم صحته بالضرورة. فالدليل «نظر معين» والمدلول عليه جنس النظر ، و النظر «المعين تعلم صحته بالضرورة. وأمثال ذلك التصورات والتصديقات التي يُعلم المعين) منها بالضرورة، ويُعلم من جنسها بهذا المعين منها . وليس ذلك من باب تعريف الشيء بنفسه . فإذا كان القول بـ أن هذه الأمور محدودة بالحدود الممكنة تَردُ عليه الاعتراضات لا يمكن [تعریف] القادحة على أصلهم، والقول بـ «أنها غير مفتقرة إلى الحدود تَرِدُ عليه الاعتراضات القادحة على أصلهم. لم يمكن القول على أصلهم : لا بأنها محدودة بما يمكن من الأشياء الحدود، ولا بأنها مستغنية عن الحدود وإذا لم تكن غنية عن الحد بل مفتقرة إليه، على أصلهم والحدود غير ممكنة، لزم توقف تعريفها على ما لا يمكن فيلزم امتناع تعريفها . ومعلوم أن معرفتها حاصلة، فعلم بطلان قولهم . وأيضاً فيبقى الإنسان غير متمكن : لا من إثبات أن لها حدوداً، ولا من نفي أن تكون لها حدود وما استلزم المنع من النفي والإثبات أو رفع النفي والإثبات، كان باطلاً. فإن كل ما يذكر من الحدود يَرِدُ عليه ما يقدح فيه على أصلهم، وقد ثبت عندهم
The second station - the ninth aspect: It is not possible to define things according to their principles. The preventer of the entry of others into it. For other than knowledge, if it depends on the acquisition of knowledge, then it depends only on the acquisition of a specific particular knowledge, not on its conceptualization, as was said in the first answer. If it were said: it also depends on its conceptualization, then it would depend on the conceptualization of a specific particular matter, not on the conceptualization of the universal, comprehensive, exclusive limited (mahdud). For the conceptualization of this universal comprehensive exclusive does not depend on it the existence of knowledge of anything, nor does it depend on its existence, nor on its conceptualization. So knowledge of anything does not depend on the limited (mahdud) — which is 'the comprehensive exclusive knowledge', nor on the definition (hadd) — which is knowledge of this comprehensive exclusive. So it does not stand on its conceptualization nor on its existence. Similarly what they mentioned in proving 'inference (nazar)' by 'inference': it is said therein: you learn the soundness of 'the genus of inference' by 'a specific inference', and 'the specific inference' you learn its soundness by necessity. So the evidence is 'a specific inference' and the thing indicated is the genus of inference, and 'the specific inference' you learn its soundness by necessity. And examples of that are the concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) from which the specific is known by necessity, and from which the genus is known through this specific. And that is not from the category of defining a thing by itself. So if the statement that these things are defined by possible definitions is met by objections that are critical according to their principles, and the statement that they are not in need of definitions is also met by objections that are critical according to their principles, it is not possible according to their principles to say either that they are defined by whatever possible definitions, nor that they are independent of definitions. And if they are not independent of definitions but are in need of them according to their principles, and definitions are not possible, then their definition would depend on what is impossible, so the impossibility of defining them follows. And it is known that their knowledge is obtained, so the falsehood of their statement is known. Also, man remains unable: neither to prove that they have definitions, nor to deny that they have definitions. And whatever entails the prevention of negation and affirmation or the removal of negation and affirmation is false. For every definition that is mentioned, there occurs against it what disproves it according to their principles, and it has been established with them...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya demonstrating that definitions (hudud) are impossible on the logicians' own principles. He begins by reiterating that knowledge is acquired through specific particular instances, not through universal definitions. If someone claims that knowledge also requires conceptualization of the universal comprehensive limited (mahdud), Ibn Taymiyya refutes this: the universal definition is neither necessary nor sufficient for knowledge of anything. He then draws a parallel to inference (nazar): we know the soundness of the genus of inference through a specific inference, which is known by necessity. Thus, knowledge of universals is based on particulars, not the reverse. This does not constitute defining a thing by itself. The crux of the argument is a double dilemma: on the logicians' principles, one cannot claim that things have possible definitions (because any proposed definition falls to criticism) nor that they are independent of definitions (because logicians require them). Since things are known, but definitions are impossible, the logicians' position is falsified. The argument concludes that man is left in an impossible position: cannot affirm or deny the existence of definitions, and this suspension is absurd. Therefore, the logicians' claim is false.

الحد
النظر

D) Definitions are possible but only through divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's argument shows that definitions are not only unnecessary for knowledge but also impossible to achieve on logicians' own principles, thereby undermining the entire foundation of definition-based epistemology.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Necessity of Definitions
Author argues that defining fundamental concepts leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio ad absurdum argument against the logicians' claim that all concepts require definitions.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the logicians' demand for definitions leads to a contradiction, proving definitions are not necessary for knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the very foundation of the logicians' theory of definition. He uses the examples of 'knowledge' and 'reflection' to demonstrate that their approach leads to circularity and impossibility. You will learn how he argues that if definitions were both necessary (as logicians claim) and impossible (as he shows), then knowledge would be impossible—which it is not, so the logicians' position is false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' position
The logicians claim that every concept must be defined by a proper hadd (genus and differentia) to be truly known. This is the doctrine Ibn Taymiyya targets.
This position is common in works like al-Shamsiyya.
2
Find the counterexample: 'knowledge'
Ibn Taymiyya takes the concept 'knowledge' ('ilm) as an example. According to the logicians, it must have a definition. But what would that definition be? Any definition of knowledge would itself involve knowledge, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
The same applies to 'reflection' (nazar).
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Ibn Taymiyya shows that if knowledge requires a definition, but that definition is impossible (due to circularity or regress), then we have a contradiction: definitions are necessary (by the logicians' claim) and impossible (by demonstration). Therefore, the logicians' position cannot be true.
The argument is: P1: All concepts need definitions. P2: Knowledge is a concept. C1: Knowledge needs a definition. But finding a valid definition for knowledge is impossible (circular/regress). So either definitions are unnecessary (contradicting P1) or knowledge is unknowable (false).
4
Understand the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logicians' theory of definition is self-defeating. Knowledge of fundamental concepts is immediate (daruri), not dependent on definitions. Definitions are only useful for distinguishing (tamayyuz), not for producing conception (tasawwur).
This critique is a key part of his larger attack on Greek logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : لا يمكن تعريف الأشياء على أصلهم

المانع من دخول غيرها فيه . فـ غير العلم إذا توقف على حصول العلم فإنما توقف على حصول علم معين جزئي، لا على تصوره، كما قيل في الجواب الأول .
فلو قيل : إنه موقوف أيضاً على تصوره، لكان موقوفاً على تصور أمر جزئي معين، لا على تصور المحدود المطلق الجامع المانع فإن تصور هذا الكلي الجامع المانع لا يتوقف عليه وجود علم بشيء من الأشياء، ولا يتوقف على وجوده، ولا على تصوره. فلا يتوقف العلم بشيء من الأشياء على المحدود ـ وهو العلم الجامع المانع، ولا على الحد - وهو العلم بهذا الجامع المانع. فلا يقف لا على تصوره، ولا على وجوده.
وكذلك ما ذكروه في إثبات النظر بالنظر، يقال فيه : تعلم صحة جنس النظر بنظر معين والنظر المعين تعلم صحته بالضرورة. فالدليل نظر معين والمدلول عليه جنس النظر، والنظر المعين تعلم صحته بالضرورة.
وأمثال ذلك التصورات والتصديقات التي يعلم المعين منها بالضرورة، ويعلم من جنسها بهذا المعين منها . وليس ذلك من باب تعريف الشيء بنفسه .
فإذا كان القول بأن هذه الأمور محدودة بالحدود الممكنة ترد عليه الاعتراضات القادحة على أصلهم، والقول بأنها غير مفتقرة إلى الحدود ترد عليه الاعتراضات القادحة على أصلهم. لم يمكن القول على أصلهم : لا بأنها محدودة بما يمكن من الأشياء الحدود، ولا بأنها مستغنية عن الحدود وإذا لم تكن غنية عن الحد بل مفتقرة إليه، على أصلهم والحدود غير ممكنة، لزم توقف تعريفها على ما لا يمكن فيلزم امتناع تعريفها . ومعلوم أن معرفتها حاصلة، فعلم بطلان قولهم .
وأيضاً فيبقى الإنسان غير متمكن : لا من إثبات أن لها حدوداً، ولا من نفي أن تكون لها حدود وما استلزم المنع من النفي والإثبات أو رفع النفي والإثبات، كان باطلاً. فإن كل ما يذكر من الحدود يرد عليه ما يقدح فيه على أصلهم، وقد ثبت عندهم أنه لا بد لها من حد فيلزم أنه لا بد لها من حد، وكل حد فهو باطل. فيلزم إثبات الحد ونفيه .
The second station - the ninth aspect: It is not possible to define things according to their principle.

The preventer of entry of other than it into it. So other than knowledge, if it depends on obtaining knowledge, it only depends on obtaining a specific particular knowledge, not on its conception, as was said in the first answer. If it were said: it also depends on its conception, it would depend on the conception of a particular specific thing, not on the conception of the absolute comprehensive preventer definiendum. For the conception of this universal comprehensive preventer does not depend on the existence of knowledge of anything, nor does it depend on its existence, nor on its conception. So knowledge of anything does not depend on the definiendum—which is the comprehensive preventer knowledge—nor on the definition—which is knowledge of this comprehensive preventer. So it is not dependent on its conception nor on its existence.

Similarly, what they mentioned in proving 'reflection' by 'reflection': it is said therein: you learn the validity of the genus of reflection by a particular reflection, and the particular reflection's validity is known necessarily. So the evidence is a particular reflection, and the indicated is the genus of reflection; the particular reflection's validity is known necessarily.

And similar to that are conceptions and assents where the particular is known necessarily, and the genus is known from this particular. And that is not from the category of defining a thing by itself.

So if the statement that these matters are defined by possible definitions is met with objections that are fatal according to their principle, and the statement that they are not in need of definitions is met with fatal objections according to their principle, it is not possible to maintain their principle: neither that they are defined by what is possible of definitions, nor that they are independent of definitions. If they are not independent of definition but rather in need of it, according to their principle, and definitions are impossible, then their definition depends on what is impossible, necessitating the impossibility of defining them. And it is known that knowledge of them exists, so their statement is known to be false.

Also, a person remains unable: neither to prove that they have definitions, nor to deny that they have definitions. And whatever necessitates the prevention of negation and affirmation or the lifting of negation and affirmation is false. For every definition that is mentioned is met with what invalidates it according to their principle, and it has been established with them that they must have a definition, so it necessitates that they must have a definition, and every definition is false. So it necessitates affirming the definition and negating it.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) are required for all concepts. He presents a reductio ad absurdum: if definitions are both necessary (according to the logicians) and impossible (as he argues), then knowledge itself would be impossible, which is false. He uses the examples of 'knowledge' ('ilm) and 'reflection' (nazar) to show that logicians' attempts to define these lead to circularity or infinite regress. He then shows that the logicians are caught in a contradiction: they cannot prove that things have definitions, nor can they prove that they do not, and any definition they propose is invalid on their own principles. Therefore, their entire theory of definition collapses.

Redefining Definition: Purpose is Distinction, not Conceptualization
Author explains definitions serve to distinguish, clarifies lazim vs mulazim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lazim and mulazim and apply Ibn Taymiyya's criterion for valid definitions in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya redefines definition as distinction using co-extensive properties, not conceptualization.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' standard view that definitions serve to conceptualize (taswir) the essence. Instead, he argues that the true purpose of definition is to distinguish (tamayyuz) the definiendum from other things. He then introduces a crucial distinction between lazim (a property that may be broader) and mulazim (a concomitant that matches the definiendum in scope). This distinction has direct implications for how we define terms in Islamic sciences like Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that logicians made taswir al-mahdud (conceptualization) the goal. Why does he reject this? He says that if the goal is tamayyuz, then both statements about definition being needed or not can be true.
2
Distinguish lazim from mulazim
He explains that distinction is achieved by what matches the definiendum in generality and specificity, i.e., the mulazim. A lazim (like 'animal' for 'human') is broader, while a mulazim (like 'rationality' for 'humanity') is co-extensive. Find the examples in the text.
The passage uses 'human' (insan) and 'animal' (hayawan) as lazim, and 'humanity' (insaniyya) with 'rationality' (natiqiyya) as mulazim.
3
Test definition validity
Ibn Taymiyya says definitions are only valid using mulazim, not mere lawazim or malzumat. Why must the property match both in affirmation and negation? Consider: if a property is broader, the definition fails to exclude things that share that property but are not the definiendum.
For example, defining 'human' as 'animal' would include non-humans.
4
Apply to the need for definition
The passage notes that absolute universals are known without definition, but definition becomes necessary when we need to distinguish a specific meaning from others, especially when doubt arises (e.g., whether a phrase is 'command' or 'report' in Usul al-Fiqh). How does Ibn Taymiyya's view account for the practical need for definitions?
This addresses the practical use in Islamic legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما استلزم ذاك إلا لأنهم جعلوا مقصود الحد تصوير المحدود. وإذا قيل المقصود من الحد التمييز بين المحدود وغيره كان كل من القولين حقاً، ولم يتقابل النفي والإثبات. بل الذين حدوها بحدودهم أفادت حدودهم التمييز بينها وبين غيرها. والتمييز يحصل بما يطابق المحدود في العموم والخصوص وهو الملازم له من الطرفين في النفي والإثبات.
وأما اللوازم فقد تكون أعم من المحدود، كما أن الملزومات قد تكون أخص منه. فإن الملزوم قد يكون أخص من اللازم، كما أن اللازم قد يكون أعم من الملزوم. فإن الإنسان مستلزم للحيوان والإنسان أخص منه، الحيوان لازم له وهو أعم منه. بخلاف المتلازمين، فإنهما متساويان في العموم والخصوص، كالإنسانية مع الناطقية، والصاهلية مع الفرسية، ونحو ذلك.
والحدود لا تجوز إلا بالملازم في الطرفين النفي والإثبات، لا بمجرد اللوازم كما يطلقه بعضهم، ولا بمجرد الملزومات. ثم التمييز يحصل بالمطابق الملازم، وإن كان قد لا يحتاج إلى هذا التمييز إذا حصل الاستغناء عنه بالاسم.
والذين قالوا إنها غنية عن الحدود بينوا أن مطلق الحقيقة تتصور بلا حد. وهذا مطلق الحقيقة حق. بل هذا ثابت في جميع الحقائق أن مطلقها متصور بلا حد. ولكن المقصود التمييز بين مسمى هذا الاسم العام وبين غيره، أو التمييز بين المعنى العام المتشابه في أفراده وبين غيره. وتمييز العام من غيره قد لا يكفي فيه مجرد تصوره مطلقاً، أو تصور بعض أفراده. فليس كل من تصور معنى مطلقاً تصوره عاماً مميزاً بينه وبين غيره. فكيف إذا لم يتصوره إلا خاصاً مقيداً معينا؟ إذ قد يقطع في مواضع بأنها خبر وأنها أمر وأنها علم ويشك في مواضع كما يشك في الاعتقاد المطابق الذي يحصل للمقلد.
And that does not necessitate that except because they made the purpose of the definition the conceptualization of the definiendum. If it is said that the purpose of the definition is the distinction between the definiendum and other than it, then each of the two statements is true, and negation and affirmation do not oppose each other. Rather, those who defined them with their definitions, their definitions benefited the distinction between them and other than them. And distinction is obtained by what matches the definiendum in generality and specificity—which is the concomitant (mulazim) of it from both sides in negation and affirmation.

As for mere properties (lawazim), they may be more general than the definiendum, just as the subjects (malzumat) may be more specific than it. For the subject (malzum) may be more specific than the property (lazim), just as the property may be more general than the subject. For example, 'human' entails 'animal'; 'human' is more specific, 'animal' is a property of it and is more general. Unlike two mutually necessitating things (mutalazimayn), for they are equal in generality and specificity, like 'humanity' with 'rationality', and 'equinity' with 'neighing', and so on.

And definitions are only valid by the concomitant (mulazim) in both sides of negation and affirmation, not by mere properties as some use, nor by mere subjects. Then distinction is obtained by the matching concomitant, although this distinction may not be needed if one dispenses with it by the name.

And those who said that they are independent of definitions explained that the absolute reality is conceived without definition. This about absolute reality is true. Rather, this is established for all realities: their absolute is conceived without definition. But the goal is the distinction between the referent of this general name and other than it, or the distinction between the general meaning that is similar in its individuals and other than it. And distinguishing the general from other than it may not be achieved by merely conceiving it absolutely, or conceiving some of its individuals. For not everyone who conceives a meaning absolutely has conceived it as a general distinguishing between it and other than it. Then how about if he conceives it only as a specific, restricted, particular? Indeed, one may be certain in some contexts that it is 'report' and that it is 'command' and that it is 'knowledge', and may doubt in other contexts, just as one doubts concerning the conforming belief that occurs to the blind follower.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a constructive critique by Ibn Taymiyya, redefining the purpose of definition (hadd). He begins by identifying the root cause of the logicians' error: they made taswir al-mahdud (conceptualization of the definiendum) the goal of definition. If instead the goal is tamayyuz (distinction between the definiendum and other things), then both statements (that definition is needed and that it is not needed) can be true without contradiction. He then explains that distinction is achieved by what matches the definiendum in generality and specificity—that is, the mulazim (necessary concomitant from both sides), not just any lazim (property). He illustrates with examples: 'human' entails 'animal' (a lazim), but they are not co-extensive; whereas 'humanity' and 'rationality' are mutalazimayn (mutually entailing) and equal in extension. Valid definitions must use such co-extensive properties. Finally, he addresses the claim that reality is known without definition: the absolute universal is indeed conceived without definition, but the need for definition arises when we must distinguish a specific meaning from others. Not everyone who conceives a universal has a clear distinguishing concept, especially when only particular instances are known. Thus, definition serves to clarify and demarcate, not to produce the initial concept.

تمييز
ملازم
لازم

Ibn Taymiyya's Redefinition of Definition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions are for distinction, not conception, and resolves the paradox using lazim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's resolution of the logicians' paradox of definition and apply his distinction between mulāzim and lāzim to evaluate definitions.

Core Idea
Definition's true purpose is distinction (tamyīz), not conception (taṣwīr).

The logicians claimed that universals cannot be known without definition, yet every definition leads to infinite regress. Ibn Taymiyya cuts this knot by shifting the goal of definition from giving essential knowledge to merely distinguishing the defined from other things. He further introduces the concept of mulāzim (coextensive concomitant) as the only proper tool for valid definitions. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the paradox
Find in the Arabic the statement that definitions are both necessary and impossible. Ibn Taymiyya says the logicians concluded that 'every definition is false' yet they demanded one. Write down the contradiction in your own words.
Immediate contradiction: affirmation and negation of definition.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's solution
Find where he shifts the purpose of definition from 'depiction (taṣwīr)' to 'distinction (tamyīz)'. Notice that once the purpose changes, the contradiction dissolves because a definition can succeed at distinction even if it fails to give full essence.
Key phrase: 'ولم يتقابل النفي والإثبات' (negation and affirmation do not contradict).
3
Understand the roles of lāzim and malzūm
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between lāzim (concomitant) and malzūm (entailed). A lāzim may be more general (e.g., animal for human). A malzūm may be more specific. But valid definitions require a mulāzim (coextensive concomitant) that matches the defined in both generality and specificity.
Example: humanity and rationality are mutalāzimān (correlatives) – equal in extension.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider the concept 'command' (amr) in uṣūl al-fiqh. Ibn Taymiyya notes that one may know a specific command but still confuse it with report (khabar). The definition must provide a mulāzim that distinguishes 'command' from 'report' in all cases.
The purpose is jam‘ wa man‘ (collection and prevention) – i.e., genus and differentia.
5
Evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique
Judge whether his redefinition succeeds. Does it solve the infinite regress? He argues that absolute concepts (muṭlaq) are conceivable without definition, but distinction requires definition. This preserves the necessity of definitions while avoiding the paradox.
His critique is epistemological: definitions do not need to give full essential knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللوازم والمعلومات
تتصور بلا حد
أنه لا بد لها من حدّ فيلزم أنه لا بد لها من حد، وكل حد فهو باطل. فيلزم إثبات الحد ونفيه .
وما استلزم ذاك إلا لأنهم جعلوا مقصود الحدّ تصوير المحدود». وإذا قيل المقصود من الحدّ التمييز بين المحدود وغيره كان كلّ من القولين حقاً، ولم يتقابل النفي والإثبات. [٧٤] بل الذين حَدُّوها بحدودهم أفادَتْ حدودهم «التمييز بينها وبين غيرها. والتمييز يحصل بما يطابق المحدود في العموم والخصوص ـ وهو «الملازم» له من الطرفين في النفي والإثبات .
وأما «اللوازم» فقد تكون أعمّ من المحدود، كما أن «الملزومات قد تكون أخص منه. فإن «الملزوم) قد يكون أخص من اللازم، كما أن اللازم» قد يكون أعم من الملزوم». فإن الإنسان» مستلزم لـ «الحيوان» و «الإنسان» أخص منه، «الحيوان» لازم له وهو أعم منه . بخلاف المتلازمين، فإنهما متساويان في العموم والخصوص، كـ «الإنسانية» مع «الناطقية»، و «الصاهلية» مع «الفَرَسية»، ونحو ذلك .
والحدود لا تجوز إلا بـ «الملازم) في الطرفين النفي والإثبات، لا بمجرد «اللوازم كما يطلقه بعضهم، ولا بمجرد الملزومات». ثم التمييز يحصل بـ «المطابق
الملازم ، وإن كان قد لا يحتاج إلى هذا التمييز إذا حصل الاستغناء عنه بالاسم.
والذين قالوا إنها غنية عن الحدود بينوا أن مطلق الحقيقة تُتصور بلا حد. وهذا مطلق الحقيقة حق . بل هذا ثابت في جميع الحقائق أن مطلقها متصور بلا حد. ولكن المقصود التمييز بين مسمى هذا الاسم العام وبين غيره، أو التمييز بين المعنى العام المتشابه في أفراده وبين غيره. وتمييز العام من غيره قد لا يكفي فيه مجرّد تصوّره مطلقاً، أو تصور بعض .أفراده فليس كل مَنْ تصور معنى مطلقاً تصوّرَه عاماً مميزاً بينه وبين غيره (۱) . فكيف إذا لم يتصوره إلا خاصاً مقيداً معين (۱) ؟ إذ قد يُقْطَع في مواضع بأنها «خبر»و[٧٥] أنها «أمر» وأنها (علم)) ويُشك في مواضع كما يشكّ في الاعتقاد المطابق الذي يحصل للمقلّد، وفي أمر الأدنى للأعلى، وفي الأمر الخالي عن الإرادة، ونحو ذلك.
فالمطلوب من الجمع والمنع الذي هو مقصود الحدّ لا يَحْصُل بتصور أعيان معينة، ولكن يحصل منه تصور الحقيقة في الجملة. فإن من تصورها معينة تصورها في مع كونها مقيَّدة. فيمكنه حينئذ أن يتصورها (۱) لا بشرط إذا الجملة وهو تصورها (۱) جردها عن التعريف، بمعنى أنه لا يشترط فيها التعريف، لا أنه يشترط عدمه. وهذا «المطلق» هل هو في الخارج جزء من المعين»، أو هذا «المطلق» - وهو المطلق لا بشرط - ليس هو في الخارج شيئاً غير (۲) «الأعيان الموجودة»، فيه قولان، كما تقدم بيانه وفصل الخطاب فيه.
فحينئذ، فلا يكون المتصوِّرُ لـ «الأمر «المعين و الخبر المعين» و «العلم المعين» قد تصـور الأشياء معينة»، أو تصور الحقيقة معينة» ـ لا «مطلقة» ولا عامة».
Necessary concomitants and informations are conceived without definition. It is necessary that they have a definition, so it follows that they must have a definition, and every definition is false. So the affirmation and denial of definition follow. This only follows because they made the purpose of definition the depiction of the defined. But if it is said that the purpose of definition is distinction between the defined and other things, then each of the two statements is true, and negation and affirmation do not contradict. Rather, those who defined them with their definitions, their definitions provided distinction between them and other things. Distinction is obtained by what corresponds to the defined in generality and specificity — that is, the concomitant (al-mulāzim) of it on both sides in negation and affirmation. As for concomitants (al-lawāzim), they may be more general than the defined, just as the things entailed (al-malzūmāt) may be more specific than it. For the entailed (al-malzūm) may be more specific than the concomitant, just as the concomitant may be more general than the entailed. For 'human' entails 'animal', and 'human' is more specific than it, 'animal' is a concomitant of it and is more general than it. Unlike the two correlatives (al-mutalāzimān), for they are equal in generality and specificity, like 'humanity' with 'rationality', and 'horseness' with 'equine nature', and so on. Definitions are only valid with the concomitant (al-mulāzim) in both sides of negation and affirmation, not merely with the concomitants as some hold, nor merely with the entailed. Then distinction is obtained by the corresponding concomitant, though it may not need this distinction if the name suffices. And those who said that they are free from definition explained that the absolute reality (muṭlaq al-ḥaqīqa) is conceived without definition. And this absolute reality is true. Rather, this is established in all realities that their absolute is conceived without definition. But the purpose is the distinction between the referent of this general name and others, or the distinction between the general meaning similar in its individuals and others. The distinction of the general from others may not be sufficient by merely conceiving it absolutely, or conceiving some of its individuals. Not everyone who conceives a meaning absolutely conceives it as a general meaning distinguished from others. How then if he conceives it only as a specific restricted particular? For it may be decisively known in some contexts that it is 'report', that it is 'command', that it is 'knowledge', and it may be doubtful in others, as it is doubtful in the conforming belief of the blind follower, and in the matter of the lower for the higher, and in the command devoid of will, and so on. The required collection and prevention (al-jam‘ wa al-man‘) which is the purpose of definition is not obtained by conceiving specific individuals, but rather by conceiving the reality in general. For whoever conceives it specifically conceives it as restricted. He can then conceive it (1) without condition (lā bi sharṭ) if he strips it of definition, meaning that he does not stipulate definition for it, not that he stipulates its absence. And this 'absolute' — is it in the external world a part of the specific, or this 'absolute' — which is the absolute without condition — is it in the external world nothing other than the existing individuals? There are two opinions, as has been explained and the definitive statement given. Then, the one who conceives the specific 'command' and the specific 'report' and the specific 'knowledge' has not conceived things as specific, nor conceived the reality as specific, but as neither absolute nor general.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' doctrine of definition (ḥadd). He begins by stating the logicians' position: that universals (like 'human', 'animal') cannot be known without a definition, but also that every definition leads to an infinite regress, yielding a contradiction. Ibn Taymiyya resolves this by shifting the purpose of definition from 'conception' (taṣwīr) to 'distinction' (tamyīz). If definition aims merely at distinguishing the defined from other things, then the contradiction disappears because definition can achieve distinction without needing to give complete knowledge of the essence.

He then introduces the key notions of 'lāzim' (necessary concomitant) and 'malzūm' (entailed). Distinction is achieved by something that matches the defined in generality and specificity — that is, a 'mulāzim' (correlative concomitant) that is coextensive. He gives examples: 'human' entails 'animal', but they are not correlatives because 'human' is more specific. Correlatives like 'humanity' and 'rationality' are equal in extension. Valid definitions use only such correlatives that are coextensive with the defined.

Ibn Taymiyya then addresses the claim that absolute realities are known without definition. He agrees that the absolute (muṭlaq) is conceivable without definition, but argues that the real purpose of definition is to distinguish a general concept from others. Simply conceiving the absolute does not provide that distinction. He uses examples from the sciences: one may know that something is a 'command' (amr) or 'report' (khabar) but still confuse them. The required 'collection and prevention' (jam‘ wa man‘) — i.e., the combination of genus and differentia that together define — cannot be achieved by conceiving specific instances; one must conceive the reality in general (al-ḥaqīqa fī al-jumla).

Finally, he raises the question of whether the absolute (without condition) exists in the external world as part of the specific individuals or as something additional, noting that there are two opinions, but this does not affect the main point. The conclusion is that conceiving a specific instance does not give the universal, nor the absolute, nor the general. Thus definitions are necessary for scientific distinction.

Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses only Islamic intellectual examples, like the distinction between 'command' and 'report' in fiqh, or 'knowledge' in kalām.

Key Terms

الملازم
اللازم
الملزوم
المتلازمان
المطلق

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya resolves the logicians' paradox of definition by redefining its purpose as distinction (tamyīz) rather than conception (taṣwīr) and requiring the use of coextensive concomitants (mulāzim) for valid definitions.

Absolute vs Determinate in Conception
Ibn Taymiyya argues that conceiving a determinate does not yield the absolute universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceiving a determinate (mu'ayyan) and conceiving the absolute (mutlaq) and explain why the absolute universal is not directly obtained from a particular.

Core Idea
The absolute universal is an abstraction from particulars, not directly known through them.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's refutation tackles a key epistemological issue: does knowing a specific instance give you the universal concept? He argues that when you conceive a determinate individual, you conceive it as restricted (muqayyad), not as absolute. The absolute is obtained only by stripping away restriction, and it does not exist externally as a separate entity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper definition and reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Notice the contrast between 'mu'ayyan' (determinate specific individual) and 'mutlaq la bi-shart' (absolute not conditioned). The passage discusses how each is conceived.
2
Understand the process of abstraction
When you conceive a specific individual, you conceive it with restrictions (e.g., a specific act of knowledge). To conceive the absolute, you mentally strip away those restrictions, meaning you don't require the restriction in your conception, not that you require its absence.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes this is a mental operation, not a discovery of an external entity.
3
Grasp the metaphysical point
Ibn Taymiyya notes two opinions on whether the absolute exists externally as part of the determinate or is nothing other than the existing individuals. He references his earlier discussion, leaving open the resolution, but stresses the absolute is not a separate external thing.
4
Apply the conclusion
Conclude that conceiving a determinate (e.g., a specific matter, report, or knowledge) does not give the absolute or universal essence; it only gives a restricted instance. The absolute is an abstraction, not directly known from the particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه التاسع : مطلق الحقيقة تتصور بلاحد وفي أمر الأدنى للأعلى، وفي الأمر الخالي عن الإرادة، ونحو ذلك. فالمطلوب من الجمع والمنع الذي هو مقصود الحدّ لا يَحْصُل بتصور أعيان معينة، ولكن يحصل منه تصور الحقيقة في الجملة. فإن من تصورها معينة تصورها في مع كونها مقيَّدة. فيمكنه حينئذ أن يتصورها لا بشرط إذا جردها عن التعريف، بمعنى أنه لا يشترط فيها التعريف، لا أنه يشترط عدمه. وهذا «المطلق» هل هو في الخارج جزء من المعين»، أو هذا «المطلق» - وهو المطلق لا بشرط - ليس هو في الخارج شيئاً غير «الأعيان الموجودة»، فيه قولان، كما تقدم بيانه وفصل الخطاب فيه. فحينئذ، فلا يكون المتصوِّرُ لـ «الأمر المعين و الخبر المعين» و «العلم المعين» قد تصور الأشياء معينة»، أو تصور الحقيقة معينة» ـ لا «مطلقة» ولا عامة».
The second station - the ninth aspect: The absolute of reality is conceived without limit, and in the matter of the lower for the higher, and in the matter void of will, and the like. So what is sought from inclusion and exclusion which is the purpose of definition is not obtained by conceiving specific individuals, but from it is obtained a conception of the reality in general. For whoever conceives it as specific conceives it as being restricted. Then he can conceive it as absolute (la bi-shart) when he strips it of restriction, meaning that he does not require restriction in it, not that he requires its absence. And this 'absolute' – is it in the external world part of the determinate, or this 'absolute' – which is the absolute not conditioned – is it not in the external world anything other than the existing individuals? There are two sayings, as its explanation and decisive discourse have preceded. So then, the one who conceives 'the determinate matter' and 'the determinate report' and 'the determinate knowledge' has not conceived things as determinate, nor conceived the reality as determinate – not as absolute and not as universal.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of the absolute (al-muṭlaq) in relation to conception. Ibn Taymiyya explains that the purpose of definition ('al-jam' wa al-man') cannot be achieved by conceiving specific individuals alone; rather, one forms a general conception of the reality. When someone conceives a specific individual, they conceive it as restricted (muqayyad). They can then abstract from that restriction to conceive the absolute (lā bi-sharṭ), meaning they do not stipulate the restriction, not that they stipulate its absence. The key question is whether this absolute exists externally as part of the determinate thing or is nothing other than the existing individuals. Ibn Taymiyya notes two opinions, referring to his earlier explanation. Finally, he concludes: one who conceives a determinate matter, report, or knowledge has not thereby conceived the absolute or universal essence; they have only conceived a restricted instance. This guards against the mistake of thinking that empirical acquaintance with particulars yields universal concepts directly.

Ibn Taymiyya: Particulars Are Epistemically Prior
Author argues universal knowledge is intuitive and does not require demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that particular knowledge is epistemically prior to universal knowledge and does not require demonstration.

Core Idea
Particulars are more certain than universals; knowledge begins with the senses, not syllogisms.

Ibn Taymiyya here challenges the logicians' assumption that universal propositions are known by intuition but particular knowledge requires demonstration. He turns their own admission against them, arguing that if universals are intuitive, then particulars—which are more directly grasped—are even more so, and thus do not depend on any syllogistic proof. This is a key move in his broader critique of Greek-inspired logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians concede that universal propositions are known by intuition (badiha) and necessity (darura). Ibn Taymiyya states this at the start: 'if they say: This is known by intuition and necessity...'.
2
Follow the counter-argument
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if that is true, then knowledge of specific entities and universals also occurs by intuition. He uses a hierarchy: certainty about individuals is greater than about universals.
He gives the example that a person knows he himself is sentient before knowing all humans are.
3
Analyze the epistemic hierarchy
He ranks certainty: individuals (ashkhas) from sensory things > species (anwa') > genera (ajnas). Knowledge of particulars is prior to fitra and stronger.
This reverses the Aristotelian priority of universals.
4
Derive the conclusion about demonstration
Since particulars are more certain and prior, knowledge of individuals does not depend on species or genera, nor does species knowledge depend on genera. Thus, such knowledge does not require demonstration (burhan).
This directly challenges the logicians' claim that demonstration is needed for certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: هذا يُعلم بالبديهة والضرورة كان هذا قولاً بأن هذه القضايا الكلية العلم بالبديهة معلومة بالبديهة والضرورة، وأن النفس مضطرة إلى هذا العلم. وهذا إن كان حقاً فالعلم بالأعيان المعينة وبأنواع الكليات يحصل أيضاً في النفس بالبديهة والضرورة، كما هو الواقع . فإن جزم العقلاء بـ «الشخصيات» من الحسيات أعظم من جزمهم بـ «الكليات»، جزمهم بكلية الأنواع أعظم من جزمهم بكلية الأجناس». والعلم بـ «الجزئيات» من الجزئيات أسبق إلى الفطرة، فجزم الفطرة بها أقوى ؛ ثم كلما قوي العقل اتسعت الكليات». وحينئذ فلا يجوز أن يقال: إن العلم بـ «الأشخاص» موقوف على العلم بـ «الأنواع والأجناس»، ولا أن العلم بـ «الأنواع» موقوف على العلم بـ «الأجناس». بل قد يعلم الإنسان أنه حساس متحرك بالإرادة قبل أن يعلم أن «كل إنسان كذلك»، ويعلم أن الإنسان كذلك قبل أن يعلم أن كلَّ حيوان كذلك». فلم يبق علمه بـ «أنه» أو بـ «أن غيره من الحيوان حساس متحرك بالإرادة» موقوفاً على «البرهان».
For if they say: This is known by intuition and necessity, then this is a statement that these universal propositions are known by intuition and necessity, and that the soul is compelled to this knowledge. And if this is true, then knowledge of specific entities and of types of universals also occurs in the soul by intuition and necessity, as is the reality. For the certainty of rational people regarding 'individuals' from sensory things is greater than their certainty regarding 'universals', their certainty regarding the universality of species is greater than their certainty regarding the universality of genera. And knowledge of 'particulars' from particulars is prior to the innate disposition, so the innate disposition's certainty regarding them is stronger; then, whenever the intellect becomes strong, the universals expand. So it is not permissible to say: knowledge of 'individuals' depends on knowledge of 'species and genera', nor that knowledge of 'species' depends on knowledge of 'genera'. Rather, a person may know that he is sentient and voluntarily mobile before he knows that 'every human is like that', and he knows that the human is like that before he knows that 'every animal is like that'. So his knowledge of 'himself' or of 'that other than him from animals is sentient and voluntarily mobile' does not remain dependent on 'demonstration'.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defends the view that universal knowledge is attained through intuitive and necessary knowledge (badiha and darura), not through demonstration. Ibn Taymiyya argues that if the logicians concede that universal propositions are known by intuition, then the same applies to knowledge of specific individuals and types of universals—it also occurs by intuition. He provides a hierarchy of certainty: individuals (al-shakhsiyyat) from sensory perceptions are more certain than universals; within universals, species are more certain than genera. Knowledge of particulars is more prior to the innate disposition (fitra), and as intellect strengthens, universals expand naturally. Therefore, knowledge of individuals does not depend on universals, nor species on genera. For example, a person knows that he himself is sentient and mobile before knowing that all humans are so, and knows that about humans before knowing that all animals are so. Thus, such knowledge does not require the logicians' demonstrative syllogism.

البديهة
الفطرة

Key Takeaway

Equivocal Terms as Univocal: Ibn Taymiyya's Reclassification
Defining equivocal terms as a type of univocal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the common universal in any equivocal term from Islamic theology and explain why Ibn Taymiyya classifies mushtakk as a type of univocal term.

Core Idea
Equivocal terms share a universal; the hierarchy is in the differentia.

In classical logic, terms like 'existent' applied to God and creatures are often considered purely equivocal (mushtarak) with no shared meaning. But Ibn Taymiyya challenges this: if there were no common meaning, predication would be impossible. In this passage, he redefines such terms as a species of univocal (mutawati') under the umbrella of a general univocal, thereby preserving both the commonality and the hierarchy. Understanding this reclassification is key to grasping the Islamic epistemological debates on divine attributes and the limits of demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the equivocal term under discussion
Find the term that Ibn Taymiyya uses as his primary example: 'existent' (al-mawjud). Note how it applies to both the Necessary (God) and the possible (creatures).
Other examples include 'soul', 'essence', 'reality'.
2
Extract the common universal (al-qadr al-mushtarak)
Look for the shared meaning between the two applications. For 'existent', the common universal is existence itself, which is present in both the Creator and creatures despite the vast difference in modality (necessary vs. contingent).
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that this common universal exists only in the mind (fi al-dhihn).
3
Understand the classification: mushtakk as a division of general univocal
Ibn Taymiyya states that 'equivocal terms are univocal in respect of the common denominator' (والأسماء المشككة متواطئة باعتبار القدر المشترك). This means mushtakk is a subtype of al-mutawati'a al-'amma (general univocal), alongside al-mutawati'a al-khassa (specific univocal, e.g., 'human' applied equally to individuals).
Thus mushtakk are not a separate category; they are univocal in the common core, but differ in priority or perfection.
4
Recognize the three required proofs
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that for any mushtakk term, three things must be established: (1) the common universal (proved by the logicians' demonstrative syllogism), (2) the differential (proved by Quranic qiyas al-awla), and (3) the Lord's particular characteristic (proved only by His signs, not by any syllogism).
This tripartite structure limits the scope of Aristotelian demonstration and highlights the need for Quranic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه السادس : لا بد في الأسماء المشككة من معنى كلي مشترك
المعتزلة الذين كانوا أسبق من أبي علي هي حقيقة في الخالق، مجاز في المخلوق. وقالت طائفة من الجَهْمية والباطنية والفلاسفة، بالعكس: هي مجاز في الخالق، حقيقة في المخلوق.
وقال جماهير الطوائف : هي حقيقة فيهما. وهذا قول طوائف النظار من المعتزلة، والأشعرية، والكَرَّامية، والفقهاء، وأهل الحديث، والصوفية. وهو قول الفلاسفة .
لكن كثيراً من هؤلاء يتناقض، فيقر في بعضها بأنها حقيقة، كاسم «الموجود»، و «النفس»، و «الذات»، و «الحقيقة»، ونحو ذلك. وينازع في بعضها لِشُبَهِ نُفَاة الجميع. والقولُ فيما نفاه نظيرُ القول فيما أثبته، ولكن هو لقصوره فرّق بين المتماثلين . ونفي الجميع يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً) .
وقد عُلم أن «الموجود» ينقسم إلى واجب وممكن وقديم وحادث، وغني وفقير، ومفعول وغير مفعول؛ وأن وجود الممكن يستلزم وجود الواجب، ووجودَ المحدث يستلزم وجود القديم، ووجود الفقير يستلزم وجود الغني، ووجود المفعول يستلزم وجود غير المفعول. وحينئذ فبين الوجودين أمر مشترك، و «الواجب» يختص بما يتميز به. فكذلك القول في الجميع .
والأسماء المشككة متواطئة باعتبار القدر المشترك. ولهذا كان المتقدمون من نظار الفلاسفة وغيرهم لا يخصون المشككة باسم ، بل لفظ «المتواطئة» يتناول ذلك كله . فالمشككة قسم من المتواطئة العامة»، وقسيم «المتواطئة الخاصة».
وإذا كان كذلك فلا بد في المشككة من إثبات قدر مشترك كلي، وهو مسمى المتواطئة العامة». وذلك لا يكون مطلقاً إلا في الذهن وهذا مدلول قياسهم البرهاني».
ولا بد من إثبات التفاضل ، وهو مدلول المشككة التي هي قسيم «المتواطئة الخاصة». وذلك هو مدلول الأقيسة البرهانية القرآنية، وهي قياس الأولى». ولا بد من إثبات خاصة الرب التي بها يتميّز عما سواه، وذلك مدلول «آياته سبحانه التي يستلزم ثبوتها ثبوت نفسه، لا يدل على هذه «قياس»، لا «برهاني» ولا «غير برهاني».
The third station - the sixth aspect: In equivocal terms, a common universal meaning is necessary.
The Mu'tazila who preceded Abu Ali [al-Jubba'i] said: [these names] are literal in the Creator, metaphorical in the creature. A sect of the Jahmiyya, Batiniyya, and philosophers said the opposite: they are metaphorical in the Creator, literal in the creature.
The majority of sects said: they are literal in both. This is the statement of the groups of theoreticians from the Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Karramiyya, jurists, hadith folk, and Sufis. And it is the statement of the philosophers.
But many of these contradict themselves, affirming in some names that they are literal, like the name 'existent', 'soul', 'essence', 'reality', and so on, and disputing in others due to the doubts of the deniers of all. The statement regarding what he denies is similar to the statement regarding what he affirms, but he—due to his insufficiency—differentiated between similar things. And denying all is impossible for something that exists.
It is known that 'existent' is divided into necessary and possible, eternal and originated, rich and poor, effected and non-effected; and that the existence of the possible entails the existence of the necessary, the existence of the originated entails the existence of the eternal, the existence of the poor entails the existence of the rich, and the existence of the effected entails the existence of the non-effected. Thus between the two existences there is a common matter, and 'the necessary' is distinguished by what distinguishes it. So likewise the statement regarding all.
Equivocal terms are univocal in respect of the common denominator. For this reason the early theoreticians among the philosophers and others did not single out equivocal terms with a name, but rather the term 'univocal' encompassed all of that. So equivocal terms are a division of 'general univocal' and a counterpart of 'specific univocal'.
If that is so, then in equivocal terms there must be proof of a common universal, which is the named 'general univocal'. That does not exist absolutely except in the mind, and this is the import of their 'demonstrative syllogism'.
And there must be proof of the differential, which is the import of the equivocal terms that are the counterpart of 'specific univocal'. That is the import of the Quranic demonstrative syllogisms, which are the 'syllogism of priority'. And there must be proof of the particular characteristic of the Lord by which He is distinguished from other than Him, and that is the import of His signs, may He be exalted, whose existence entails His own existence. No 'syllogism', neither 'demonstrative' nor 'non-demonstrative', indicates this.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and epistemological argument about the nature of equivocal terms (al-asma' al-mushakkaka). Ibn Taymiyya begins by asserting that in such terms, a common universal meaning (ma'na kulli mushtarak) is necessary. He then reviews the positions of various Islamic sects on whether names like 'existent' are literal (haqiqa) or metaphorical (majaz) when applied to God and creatures. The Mu'tazila before al-Jubba'i said they are literal only for God; the Jahmiyya, Batiniyya, and some philosophers said the opposite; the majority of sects (including later Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, etc.) affirmed literality in both. Ibn Taymiyya notes the contradiction in many who accept some names as literal but deny others due to weak doubts. He then argues that the term 'existent' divides into necessary and possible, etc., and that between the two existences there is a common element (amr mushtarak). This common element is the basis for the claim that mushtakk terms are actually a type of mutawati' (univocal) term when considering the common denominator. He explains that early philosophers and others did not distinguish mushtakk as a separate category; rather, 'mutawati' encompassed both. Thus mushtakk is a division of 'general univocal' (al-mutawati'a al-'amma) and a counterpart of 'specific univocal' (al-mutawati'a al-khassa). Consequently, three things must be proven in mushtakk: (1) a common universal (al-qadr al-mushtarak), which is the subject of the logicians' demonstrative syllogism and exists only in the mind; (2) the differential (al-tafadul), which is the specific meaning of mushtakk and is proven by Quranic demonstrative syllogisms of the 'syllogism of priority' (qiyas al-awla); (3) the particular characteristic of the Lord (khassa al-rabb), which is proven by His signs (ayat) and not by any syllogism, demonstrative or otherwise. This establishes the limits of the logicians' method.

Key Terms
المشككة
المتواطئة
القدر المشترك
قياس الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did early philosophers and theoreticians not give a special name to mushtakk terms?
A) They considered mushtakk terms to be purely equivocal with no common meaning.
B) They believed mushtakk terms were not real terms at all.
C) They regarded mushtakk terms as included under 'univocal' (mutawati') because of the common denominator.
D) They only used the term 'mushtakk' for metaphorical language.

Key Takeaway
Equivocal terms are unified by a common universal and differ by a differential; they are a subset of univocal terms, not a separate category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defining Qiyas and Istiqra: Terminological and Logical Distinctions
Author defines qiyas and istiqra' and distinguishes complete from incomplete induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logicians' broad definition of qiyas from the jurists' restricted one, and differentiate complete induction (yielding certainty) from incomplete induction (yielding probability).

Core Idea
Qiyas is broader than analogy; istiqra' is only certain if it surveys every instance.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin opens the discussion of proof and syllogism by clarifying how the logicians use key terms. You need to grasp these definitions precisely, because later arguments about the validity of syllogism depend on whether qiyas includes analogy and whether induction can ever be complete. The terminological shift between usul al-fiqh (where qiyas means analogy) and logic (where it covers all syllogisms) is a common source of confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting definitions of qiyas
Read the first two sentences. Note that specialists in usul and kalam restrict 'qiyas' to analogy (tamthil), while the majority of rational people (including logicians) use it for both categorical syllogism and analogy. Mark the contrasting groups.
This terminological disagreement is the basis for later arguments about whether qiyas in logic is the same as qiyas in fiqh.
2
Parse the definition of istiqra'
Find the sentence: 'الاستدلال بالجزئيات على الكلي هو الاستقراء' (Reasoning from particulars to the universal is induction). Identify the genus (reasoning) and the differentia (from particulars to universal). This is the logicians' definition.
The definition is straightforward; the key is to note that induction moves from specific instances to a general rule.
3
Distinguish complete from incomplete induction
Look at the next two sentences. The author divides induction into tamm (complete) and naqis (incomplete). Complete induction covers all particulars and yields certainty (yaqin). Incomplete induction covers only most and does not yield certainty.
Memorise this distinction: tamm = certain, naqis = probable.
4
Analyse the crocodile counterexample
Read the example: someone inductively claims all animals move their lower jaw when eating, based on observing many species. The objection is that the crocodile moves its upper jaw. This shows that a single counterexample falsifies an incomplete induction.
This example illustrates why incomplete induction is not reliable for establishing universal rulings in Islamic law or theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - أقوالهم في «الدليل» و«القياس». «القياس». وكثير من أهل الأصول والكلام يخصون باسم «القياس» «التمثيل». وأما جمهور العقلاء فاسم «القياس» عندهم يتناول هذا وهذا. قالوا والاستدلال بـ «الجزئيات على «الكلي» هو «الاستقراء». فإن كان تاماً فهو : الاستقراء التام»، وهو يفيد اليقين وإن كان ناقصاً لم يفد اليقين. فالأول هو استقراء جميع «الجزئيات، والحكم عليه بما وُجد في جزئياته ؛ والثاني استقراء أكثرها وقد يكذب، كقول القائل : «الحيوان إذا أكل حَرَّك فكه الأسفل لأنا استقريناها فوجدناها هكذا»، فيقال له : «التمساح يحرك الأعلى».
The third station: Their statements about the 'proof' and 'syllogism'. 'Syllogism'. Many of the people of usul and kalam specify the name 'syllogism' to mean 'analogy'. As for the majority of rational people, the name 'syllogism' in their usage encompasses both this and that. They said: Reasoning from particulars to the universal is 'induction'. If it is complete, it is 'complete induction', and it yields certainty; if it is incomplete, it does not yield certainty. The first is the induction of all particulars and judging it based on what is found in its particulars; the second is the induction of most of them, and it may be false, like the one who says: 'Every animal when it eats moves its lower jaw, because we induced and found it thus,' and it is said to him: 'The crocodile moves the upper one.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is a definitional passage from Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians, where he first defines their key terms: qiyas and istiqra'. He begins by noting a terminological dispute: specialists in usul al-fiqh and kalam often restrict 'qiyas' to analogical reasoning (tamthil), while the broader philosophical tradition uses it for both categorical syllogism and analogy. He then defines istiqra' (induction) as reasoning from particulars to a universal. He distinguishes complete induction, which surveys all instances and yields certainty, from incomplete induction, which only surveys most instances and is thus uncertain. The example of the animal's jaw motion illustrates incomplete induction: observing that many animals move their lower jaw when eating does not prove all do, since the crocodile moves its upper jaw. This example is drawn from natural observation and teaches that inductive generalizations can be falsified by a single counterexample. In Islamic terms, this is why jurists rely on textual evidence and analogical reasoning rather than incomplete induction for general rulings. The passage sets the stage for later criticisms of syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس
الاستقراء
التمثيل
الاستقراء التام
الاستقراء الناقص

Key Takeaway
The logicians' qiyas includes both syllogism and analogy, and induction is only certain when it surveys every particular instance.
