Critique of Obscure Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya attacks logicians' definitions using the example of the sun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw of defining an obvious term by a less obvious one, and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of such definitions.

Core Idea
A definition must be clearer than the thing defined, not more obscure.

Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' definitions targets a fundamental flaw: they often define clear, everyday concepts in terms that are less clear. Using the example of the sun defined as 'a star that shines during the day', he shows how such definitions are not only useless but also reveal the ignorance of those who propose them. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for definitional practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Find the logicians' definition of the sun that Ibn Taymiyya criticizes: 'a star that shines during the day' (kawkab yatlu'u naharan). Note that he also mentions a weaker phrase from al-Kindi.
2
Analyze the basis of Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that the sun is more obvious than the terms 'star' and 'day'. Defining something more obvious by something less obvious is absurd. Ask yourself: why should someone need this definition?
3
Examine his alternative rationalizations
He considers two cases: someone who doesn't know the word (needs translation, not definition) and someone blind (cannot see day or stars, but can learn from others). Neither case requires the logicians' method.
This shows that the definition fails both for practical and theoretical purposes.
4
Recognize the fallacy type
The logicians' definition commits the fallacy of obscurum per obscurius (explaining the clear by the unclear). Ibn Taymiyya's critique targets this logical error.
This fallacy weakens many overly technical definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال الركة مثل ما ذكروه عن يعقوب بن إسحاق الكندي الفيلسوف أنه قال في بعض مناظراته : هذا من باب فقد عدم الوجود . ومثل هذه العبارات الطويلة الركيكة كثير في كلامهم، حتى في كلام أفضل متأخريهم، مع أنه أفضلهم وأحسنهم بياناً. وكذلك تكلفاتهم في حدودهم، مثل حدهم لـ «الإنسان»، و «الشمس» بأنها كوكب يطلع نهاراً . وهل مَنْ يحدُّ «الشمس» مثل هذا الحد ونحوه إلا مَنْ هو مِن أجهل الناس؟ وهل عند الناس شيء أظهر من الشمس حتى تُحدّ الشمس به؟ ومن لم يعرف «الشمس» فإما أن يجهل اللفظ ، فيترجم له، وليس هذا من الحد الذي ذكروه؛ وإما أن لا يكون رآها لعماه ، فهذا لا يرى النهار ، ولا الكواكب بطريق الأولى، مع أنه لا بد أن يسمع من الناس ما يعرف ذلك دون طريقهم .
Example of weakness: like what they mentioned about Ya'qub ibn Ishaq al-Kindi the philosopher that he said in some of his debates: 'This is from the category of absence of existence.' And many of such long, weak expressions appear in their speech, even in the speech of the best of their late ones, although he is the best of them and the best in expression. And similarly their affectations in their definitions, like their definition of 'human' and 'sun' as 'a star that shines during the day'. And is the one who defines the sun with such a definition and its like except from the most ignorant of people? And is there anything among people more apparent than the sun such that the sun is defined by it? And whoever does not know the sun, either he is ignorant of the word, so it is translated for him, and this is not from the definition they mentioned; or he has not seen it due to blindness, so he does not see the day, nor the stars, a fortiori, though he must have heard from people what makes him know that without their method.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' definitions and their convoluted language. Ibn Taymiyya begins by giving an example of al-Kindi's weak expression 'from the category of absence of existence' (a redundant phrase) and notes that such weak speech is common even among their best. Then he attacks their definitions, especially the definition of the sun as 'a star that rises during the day'. He argues that this is absurd because the sun is more obvious than any definition; defining the sun by something less obvious shows ignorance. He then analyzes the case of someone who does not know the sun: either they do not know the word (so a translation suffices, not a definition) or they are blind (so they cannot see the day either, but they can learn about the sun from others without the logician's method). The point is that the logicians' definitions are useless and unnatural.

Key Terms
حَدّ
كوكب يطلع نهاراً
فقد عدم الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sun is not a star.
B) The definition uses a less obvious term to define a more obvious thing.
C) The definition is too long.
D) The sun does not shine during the day.

Key Takeaway
A good definition must be clearer than the thing it defines; otherwise, it fails its fundamental purpose.

Refuting the Claim That Every Sought Proposition Has Only Two Parts
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that every sought proposition has only two terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that every sought proposition has at most two parts, and evaluate whether the number of parts is fixed or variable using his examples.

Core Idea
The sought proposition's parts are not fixed at two — they vary by context and expression.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational claim of Aristotelian logic: that every sought proposition (al-matlūb) consists of only two terms (subject and predicate) and therefore requires only two premises. In this passage, he dismantles this claim by showing that actual questions and answers in Islamic discourse often involve multiple terms and multiple meanings. He also turns the logicians' own 'estimation' argument against them, revealing the flexibility of linguistic expression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that the sought proposition never has more than two parts (juz'ayn), meaning two terms, so it only needs two premises. Find this claim in the Arabic: 'قولهم : ليس المطلوب أكثر من جزئين، فلا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين'.
This is the target of the refutation.
2
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's counterexamples
He gives three examples: nabidh (with three parts: prohibition, by text, by analogy), consensus (three parts), and 'man' (six parts). For each, note how many parts the sought proposition actually has according to the question's structure.
These show that the number of parts is not fixed at two.
3
Distinguish between terms and meanings
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two interpretations of 'two parts': (a) two words (terms), and (b) two meanings. He refutes both. Terms: propositions like Quranic verses have many words due to restrictions. Meanings: the seeker may ask for one, two, or many meanings.
This addresses the logicians' possible defense.
4
Understand the estimation objection and Ibn Taymiyya's counter
The logicians might argue that a single proposition can be 'estimated' as multiple propositions (e.g., 'Is man...?' becomes five separate 'Is it...?' questions). Ibn Taymiyya turns this around: if a word like 'yes' can stand for a full proposition, then a single noun can be evidence, undermining the need for two-part propositions.
This shows the inconsistency in the logicians' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم : ليس المطلوب أكثر من جزئين، فلا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين»، فيقال : إن أردتم ليس له إلا اسمان «مفردان فليس الأمر كذلك، بل قد يكون التعبير عنه بأسماء متعددة. مثل من شك في النبيذ : هل هو حرام بالنص، أم ليس حراماً، لا بنص ولا قياس؟ فإذا قال المجيب : النبيذ حرام بالنص كان المطلوب ثلاثة أجزاء . وكذلك لو سأل: هل الإجماع دليل قطعي؟»، فقال: «الإجماع دليل قطعي، كان المطلوب ثلاثة أجزاء. فإذا قال : «هل الإنسان جسم، حساس، نام، متحرك بالإرادة، ناطق أم لا؟ ، فالمطلوب هنا له ستة أجزاء . وفي الجملة فالموضوع والمحمول الذي هو مبتدأ ، وخبر، وهو جملة خبرية : قد تكون جملة مركبة من لفظين وقد تكون من ألفاظ متعددة إذا كان مضمونها مقيداً بقيود كثيرة، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّبِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْمُهَجِرِينَ وَالْأَنصَارِ وَالَّذِينَ اتَّبَعُوهُم بِإِحْسَنِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمْ وَرَضُوا عَنْهُ ﴾ [سورة التوبة : ١٠٠]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَالَّذِينَ هَاجَرُوا وَجَهَدُوا فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ أُوْلَبِكَ يَرْجُونَ رَحْمَتَ الله ﴾ [سورة البقرة : ۲۱۸]، وقوله: ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا مِنْ بَعْدُ وَهَاجَرُوا وَجَهَدُوا مَعَكُمْ فَأُوْلَيْكَ منكم ﴾ [سورة الأنفال : ٧٥]، وأمثال ذلك من القيود التي يسميها النحاةُ الصفاتِ والعَطْفَ، والأحوال، وظرف المكان، وظرف الزمان، ونحو ذلك . فإذا كانت القضية مقيدة بقيود كثيرة لم تكن مؤلفة من لفظين، بل من ألفاظ متعددة ومعان متعددة . وإن أريد أن المطلوب ليس إلا معنيان - سواء عَبَّر عنهما بلفظين أو ألفاظ متعددة قيل وليس الأمر كذلك بل قد يكون المطلوب معنى واحداً، وقد يكون معنيان، وقد يكون معاني متعددة. فإن المطلوب بحسب طلب الطالب، وهو الناظر المستدلّ، أو السائل المتعلم المناظر ، وكل منهما قد يطلب معنى واحداً، وقد يطلب معنيين، وقد يطلب معاني . والعبارة عن مطلوبه قد تكون بلفظ واحد، وقد تكون بلفظين، وقد تكون بأكثر . فإذا قال النبيذ حرام؟»، قيل له : «نعم»، كان هذا اللفظ وحده كافياً في جوابه، كما لو قيل له : هو حرام». فإن قالوا : القضية الواحدة قد تكون في تقدير قضايا كما ذكرتموه من التمثيل بـ «الإنسان فإن هذه القضية الواحدة في تقدير خمس قضايا، وهي خمس مطالب والتقدير : هل هو جسم أم لا؟ وهل هو حساس أم لا؟ وهل هو نام أم لا؟ وهل هو متحرك بالإرادة أم لا ؟ وهل هو ناطق أم لا ؟ وكذلك فيما تقدم : هل النبيذ حرام فهل تحریمه بالنص أو بالقياس؟ فيقال: إذا رضيتم بمثل هذا وهو أن تجعلوا الواحد في تقدير عدد، فالمفرد قد يكون في معنى قضية. فإذا قال: «النبيذ المسكر حرام؟»، فقال المجيب: «نعم»، فلفظ «نعم» في تقدير قوله : «هو حرام» . وإذا قال: «ما الدليل عليه؟ ، فقال : الدليل عليه تحريم كل مسكر»، أو «أن كل مسكر حرام، أو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كل مسكر حرام)، ونحو ذلك من العبارات التي جَعَل الدليل فيها اسماً مفرداً، وهو جزء واحد، لم يجعله قضية مؤلفة من اسمين - مبتدأ وخبر. فإن قوله : تحريم كل مسكر اسم مضاف .
Their statement: 'The sought proposition does not have more than two parts, so it does not need more than two premises.' It is said: If you mean it does not have more than two 'terms', then this is not the case; rather, the expression of it may be with multiple terms. Like one who doubts about nabidh: 'Is it forbidden by text, or is it not forbidden, neither by text nor analogy?' If the respondent says: 'Nabidh is forbidden by text,' the sought proposition has three parts. Likewise, if he asks: 'Is consensus a definitive proof?' and he says: 'Consensus is a definitive proof,' the sought proposition has three parts. If he says: 'Is man a body, sentient, breathing, moving by will, rational or not?' the sought proposition here has six parts. In general, the subject and predicate which are subject and predicate, and it is a declarative sentence: it may be a composite sentence of two words, and it may be of multiple words if its content is restricted by many restrictions, like Allah's saying: 'And the foremost, the first of the emigrants and the helpers and those who followed them in goodness, Allah is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him' (Quran 9:100), and His saying: 'Indeed, those who have believed and those who have emigrated and fought in the cause of Allah – those expect the mercy of Allah' (Quran 2:218), and His saying: 'And those who believed after [the initial emigration] and emigrated and fought with you – they are of you' (Quran 8:75), and similar restrictions which grammarians call attributes, conjunctions, circumstantial accusatives, adverbs of place, adverbs of time, and the like. So when the proposition is restricted by many restrictions, it is not composed of two words, but of multiple words and multiple meanings. And if it is meant that the sought proposition is not more than two meanings – whether expressed by two words or multiple words – it is said: This is not the case; rather, the sought proposition may be a single meaning, may be two meanings, and may be multiple meanings. For the sought proposition is according to the request of the seeker, who is the investigator/arguer, or the questioner/learner/disputant, and each of them may seek a single meaning, may seek two meanings, and may seek meanings. The expression of his sought proposition may be with a single word, may be with two words, and may be with more. If he says: 'Is nabidh forbidden?' and it is said to him: 'Yes,' this word alone is sufficient in his answer, just as if it is said to him: 'It is forbidden.' If they say: 'The single proposition may be in the estimation of propositions, as you exemplified with 'man' – for this single proposition is in the estimation of five propositions, and they are five sought propositions: Is it a body or not? Is it sentient or not? Is it breathing or not? Is it moving by will or not? Is it rational or not? And likewise in what preceded: Is nabidh forbidden? Is its prohibition by text or analogy?' It is said: If you accept such a thing – that you make the one in the estimation of a number – then a single word may be in the meaning of a proposition. If he says: 'Is intoxicating nabidh forbidden?' and the respondent says: 'Yes,' the word 'yes' is in the estimation of his saying: 'It is forbidden.' And if he says: 'What is the evidence for it?' and he says: 'The evidence for it is the prohibition of every intoxicant,' or 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' or the saying of the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' and the like of such expressions in which he made the evidence a single noun, which is one part, he did not make it a proposition composed of two names – subject and predicate. For his saying: 'Prohibition of every intoxicant' is a construct phrase.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that every sought proposition (al-matlūb) consists of at most two parts (i.e., two terms: subject and predicate) and therefore only requires two premises. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is false in multiple ways.

He begins by considering the logicians' statement: 'The sought proposition does not have more than two parts, so it does not need more than two premises.' He responds by saying: if by 'parts' you mean 'terms' (singular nouns), then this is not correct because the expression of a sought proposition often uses multiple terms. He gives examples: a question about nabidh (fermented drink) with three aspects (is it forbidden? by text? or by analogy?) yields a three-part response. Similarly, a question about consensus being definitive yields three parts. A question about man being body, sentient, breathing, etc., yields six parts. Thus, the sought proposition can have many parts.

He then discusses propositions (qadiyya) themselves. A proposition is a declarative sentence with subject and predicate. It may be composed of just two words, but it may also be composed of many words when the content is restricted by multiple conditions. He cites Quranic verses that include multiple restrictions (attributes, conjunctions, etc.). So a proposition can have many words and many meanings.

Next, he addresses the possibility that by 'two parts' they mean 'two meanings' (i.e., the sought proposition contains only two concepts). He refutes this too: the number of meanings in a sought proposition depends on the seeker. It can be one meaning, two, or many. The questioner may seek a single concept (e.g., 'Is nabidh forbidden?' — answer: 'Yes' suffices, a single word in the answer). Or he may seek multiple meanings (e.g., 'Is man body, sentient, etc.?'). So the number of parts is not fixed at two.

Then Ibn Taymiyya anticipates an objection: the logicians may say that a single proposition can be estimated as multiple propositions, as in the 'man' example where one proposition about man being body, sentient, etc., is estimated as five separate propositions. He turns this argument against them: if they accept that a single word or expression can stand for multiple propositions, then a single word like 'yes' can be estimated as a full proposition ('It is forbidden'). Also, the evidence for a claim can be expressed as a single noun phrase (e.g., 'the prohibition of every intoxicant') which is not a full proposition but a single part. This undermines their insistence that everything must be reduced to two-part propositions.

The passage thus demonstrates that the logicians' claim is simplistic and fails to account for the richness of actual discourse and inquiry.

Key Terms
المطلوب
القضية
جزئين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's example, how many parts does the sought proposition have when someone asks: 'Is man body, sentient, breathing, moving by will, rational or not?'
A) Two parts
B) Three parts
C) Five parts
D) Six parts

Key Takeaway
The number of parts in a sought proposition is not fixed at two; it varies with the complexity of the question and the expression used.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the question: 'Is intoxicating nabidh forbidden by text or by analogy?' List all the parts of the sought proposition according to Ibn Taymiyya's analysis (i.e., the number of distinct elements that the questioner and responder are actually dealing with).
?
Consider a hypothetical debate: A student asks, 'Is the Quran created or uncreated?' Identify how many parts the sought proposition has, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya would reject any attempt to force it into exactly two parts.

The Variable Middle Term in Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya defines the middle term as variable evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya’s definition of the middle term (al-wast) as a variable intermediary and differentiate it from the fixed Aristotelian middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term is not fixed; it varies with each person, context, and source of knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya redefines the logical concept of the middle term (al-wast). While Aristotelian logic treats the middle term as a fixed term in a syllogism, Ibn Taymiyya expands it to include any evidence or intermediary that connects a subject to its predicate. He argues that these intermediaries are not universal but shift depending on the knower, the context, and the mode of transmission. Understanding this variability is crucial for evaluating how knowledge—especially religious knowledge—is transmitted and acquired.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's definition of al-wast
Locate where he says 'the middle term is the evidence, and it is the intermediary in knowledge between the antecedent and the consequent.' This tells you what he means by wast.
2
Contrast with the fixed Aristotelian middle term
In Aristotelian logic, the middle term is a specific term in a categorical syllogism that links the major and minor terms. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this fixed role: he insists the middle term varies.
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is epistemological, not merely technical.
3
Examine the examples he provides
He gives the crescent sighting, transmission of Quran and Sunnah, and sensory/intellectual knowledge. For each, the intermediaries change for different recipients.
The examples show that the same truth can reach people through different chains of intermediaries.
4
Understand his critique of the inherent middle term
He refutes the view that the middle term is an inherent attribute of the thing itself. Instead, it is mental and relative.
This distinction between mental and external middle terms is key.
5
Recognize the implications for inference
He concludes that inference can be from one premise or many; specifying exactly two is arbitrary. This undermines the syllogistic dominance.
This is a direct attack on the Aristotelian insistence on two-premise syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الأوساط تتنوع بتنوع الناس. فليس ما كان وسطاً مستلزماً للحكم في حق هذا هو الذي يجب أن يكون وسطاً في حق الآخر، بل قد يحصل له وسط آخر. فـ «الوسط» هو الدليل، وهو الواسطة في العلم بين الملزوم واللازم، وهما المحكوم والمحكوم عليه فإن الحكم لازم للمحكوم عليه ما دام حكماً له. والأواسط - التي هي الأدلة - مما يتنوّع ويتعدّد بحسب ما يفتحه الله للناس من الهداية، كما إذا كان «الوسط» خبر صادق فقد يكون الخبر لهذا غير الخبر لهذا. وإذا رأى الناسُ الهلال وثبت عند دار السلطان وتفرّق الناس فأشاعوا ذلك في البلد، فكل قوم يحصل لهم العلم غير المخبرين الذين أخبروا غيرهم. والقرآن والسنة الذي يبلغه الناس عن الرسول بلغ كلَّ قوم بوسائط غير وسائط غيرهم، لا سيما في القرن الثاني والثالث. فهؤلاء لهم مقرئون ومعلمون، وهؤلاء لهم مقرئون ومعلمون. وهؤلاء كلهم وسائط ـ وهم الأواسط ـ بينهم وبين معرفة ما قاله الرسول وفعله، الذين دلوهم على ذلك بإخبارهم وتعليمهم. وكذلك المعلومات التي تُنال بالعقل أو الحس إذا نبه عليها مُنبّه، وأرشد إليها مرشد، فذلك أيضاً مما يختلف ويتنوّع ؛ ونفسُ الوسائط العقلية تتنوع وتختلف. وأما من جعل «الوسط» في اللوازم هو وسطاً في نفس ثبوتها للموصوف، فهذا باطل من وجوه، كما قد بسط في موضعه. وبتقدير صحته، فالوسط الذهني أعم من الخارجي، كما أن الدليل أعم من العلة فكل علة يمكن الاستدلال بها على المعلول، وليس كل دليل يكون علة في نفس الأمر. وكذلك ما كان متوسطاً في نفس الأمر أمكن جَعْلُه متوسطاً في الذهن، فيكون دليلاً، ولا ينعكس، لأن الدليل هو ما كان مستلزماً للمدلول. فالعلة المستلزمة للمعلول يمكن الاستدلال بها والوسط الذي يلزم الملزوم، ويلزمه اللازم البعيد، هو مستلزم لذلك اللازم، فيمكن الاستدلال به. فتبين أنه على كل تقدير يمكن الاستدلال على المطلوب بمقدمة واحدة إذا لم يحتج إلى غيرها، وقد لا يمكن إلا بمقدمات فيحتاج إلى معرفتهن. وأن تخصيص الحاجة بمقدمتين دون ما زاد وما نقص تحكُّم محض.
The middle terms vary according to the variation of people. For what was a middle term entailing the judgment with respect to this person is not what must be a middle term with respect to another, rather another middle term may occur for him. So 'the middle term' is the evidence, and it is the intermediary in knowledge between the antecedent and the consequent, which are the subject and predicate, for the judgment is necessary for the subject as long as it is a judgment for it. And the middle terms – which are the evidences – are something that varies and multiplies according to what God opens for people of guidance, as when the 'middle term' is a true report, so the report for this one may be other than the report for that one. And when people see the crescent and it is established at the ruler's court and the people disperse and spread that in the city, then each group obtains knowledge other than the informants who informed others. And the Qur'an and Sunnah that the people convey from the Messenger reached each group by intermediaries other than the intermediaries of others, especially in the second and third centuries. These have reciters and teachers, and those have reciters and teachers. And all of these are intermediaries – and they are the middle terms – between them and the knowledge of what the Messenger said and did, who guided them to that by their informing and teaching. And likewise the pieces of knowledge attained by intellect or sense, when a warner warns about them and a guide guides to them, that also varies and differs; and the intellectual intermediaries themselves vary and differ. As for one who makes the 'middle term' in the necessary concomitants a middle term in the very existence of those concomitants for the described thing, this is false in several respects, as has been elaborated in its place. And on the assumption of its correctness, the mental middle term is more general than the external one, just as the evidence is more general than the cause, for every cause can be used as evidence for the effect, but not every evidence is a cause in reality. And likewise what is intermediate in reality can be made intermediate in the mind, becoming evidence, but the converse is not true, because the evidence is what entails the indicated thing. So the cause that entails the effect can be used as evidence, and the middle term that entails the antecedent and the distant consequent is entailing that consequent, so it can be used as evidence. So it is shown that on every estimation the required thing can be inferred from a single premise if no other is needed, and sometimes it is only possible with multiple premises, so they need to be known. And that specifying the need to two premises, neither more nor less, is pure arbitrariness.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines and expands upon the concept of the 'middle term' (al-wast) in reasoning. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that middle terms vary according to people—what works for one may not work for another. He then defines the middle term as the evidence (dalil) and the intermediary in knowledge between the antecedent (malzum) and consequent (lazim), i.e., the subject and predicate of a judgment. The middle terms (al-awasit) are diverse, varying according to God's guidance. Examples include reports: the report that serves as evidence for one person may differ for another, as in the case of crescent sighting spreading from the ruler's court to different groups. Similarly, the transmission of the Quran and Sunnah from the Prophet involved different intermediaries for different communities, especially in the early centuries. Intellectual and sensory knowledge also varies based on the guides. Ibn Taymiyya then refutes the view that the middle term is an inherent attribute: it is false to think the middle term is intrinsically in the attributes. The mental middle term is more general than the external one, just as evidence is more general than the cause. Every cause can serve as evidence, but not every evidence is a cause. What is intermediate in reality can be made a mental intermediate. Finally, he concludes that inference can be made from a single premise or multiple premises; specifying exactly two is arbitrary.

Key Terms
الوسط
الدليل
الملزوم واللازم
الأواسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The evidence and intermediary between antecedent and consequent
C) The genus in a definition
D) The cause in a demonstrative proof

Key Takeaway
The middle term is not a fixed logical constant but varies with persons, contexts, and sources of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Trace the transmission of a specific hadith (e.g., the hadith of ‘Umar about intentions) through two different chains. Identify the intermediaries (narrators) in each chain and explain how for a student in Baghdad and a student in Andalusia, the middle terms (chains) differ, yet both can lead to knowledge of the Prophet’s statement.
?

Refuting Philosophical Claims About Intellect and Angels
Author refutes philosophers' claims about intellect and angels, including al-Ghazali's distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute two philosophical misconceptions: (1) that the intellect is the first created thing, and (2) that intellects are identical to angels, using hadith interpretation and Islamic consensus.

Core Idea
Angels are created from light; intellects are not angels. The hadith on intellect does not prove it is first creation.

Philosophers have advanced claims about the intellect and angels that conflict with Islamic teachings. They misinterpret a hadith to claim the intellect is the first creation, and they identify their abstract intellects with the angels of revelation. In this lesson, we follow the author's refutation, which combines careful hadith analysis with textual evidence from Sahih Muslim to uphold the Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' first claim
The philosophers claim that the hadith 'The first thing God created was the intellect' means the intellect is the first created being, implying priority over all other creations.
This literal reading is what the author challenges.
2
Analyze the author's counter-interpretation
The author clarifies that the hadith means God addressed the intellect at the first moment of its creation, not that it was created before all others. Evidence: the phrase 'I have not created a creation more honored to Me than you' implies other creations existed before the intellect.
The author uses internal hadith evidence to restrict the meaning.
3
Examine the second claim: intellects as angels
Philosophers claim that the intellects they posit (e.g., the Active Intellect) are identical to the angels mentioned in revelation.
4
Present the author's textual refutation
The author cites the authentic hadith in Sahih Muslim: 'God created the angels from light, created the jinn from a flame of fire, and created Adam from what was described to you.' This proves angels are created from matter, so they cannot be uncreated or eternal intellects.
The hadith is the primary proof, supported by consensus.
5
Critique al-Ghazali's distinction
The author rejects al-Ghazali's separation of 'alam al-khalq (bodies) and 'alam al-amr (souls/intellects), stating that everything other than Allah is created. This distinction is not from the religion of Muslims.
The author appeals to the Islamic principle that Allah is the Creator of everything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الكلام على «العقول» و«الملائكة». هذا فلفظه : «أوّلُ ما خلق الله العقل قال له فمدلوله أنه خاطبه في أول أوقات خلقه ليس مدلوله أنه أول المخلوقات. وفي تمامه أنه قال: «ما خلقت خلقاً أكرم علي منك ، فدل أنه خلق قبله غيره. وفيه أنه مخلوق. وأبو حامد يفرق بين عالم الخلق وعالم الأمر، فيجعل الأجسام عالم الخلق، والنفوس والعقول عالم الأمر . وهذا أيضاً ليس من دين المسلمين، بل كلُّ ما سوى الله مخلوق عند المسلمين والله تعالى خالق كل شيء . وإذا ادعو أن «العقول التي اثبتوها هي الملائكة» في كلام الأنبياء، فقد ثبت خلقت الملائكة بالنص والإجماع أن الله خلق الملائكة، بل خلقهم من مادّة، كما ثبت في صحيح مسلم، عن عائشة، أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : خلق الله الملائكة من نور، وخلق الجان من مارج من نار، وخلق آدم مما وُصف لكم. فبين أن الملائكة مخلوقون من مادة موجودة قبلهم فأين هذا من قول مَنْ ينفي الخلق عنها؟ ويقول : إنها مبتدعة لا مخلوقة أو يقول : إنها قديمة أزلية لم تكن من مادة أصلاً.
The third station - speech about 'the intellects' and 'the angels'. This is its wording: 'The first thing God created was the intellect, He said to it...' its implication is that He addressed it in the first moments of its creation, not that it is the first of created things. And in its completion: He said: 'I have not created a creation more honored to Me than you,' so it indicates that He created other than it before it. And in it, that it is created. And Abu Hamid [al-Ghazali] distinguishes between the world of creation and the world of command, making bodies the world of creation, and souls and intellects the world of command. This also is not from the religion of the Muslims; rather, everything other than God is created according to the Muslims, and God Most High is the Creator of everything. And if they claim that 'the intellects which they have posited are the angels' in the speech of the prophets, then the creation of angels has been established by text and consensus that God created the angels, indeed He created them from matter, as established in Sahih Muslim, from Aisha, that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'God created the angels from light, created the jinn from a flame of fire, and created Adam from what was described to you.' So he clarified that the angels are created from matter existing before them. So where is this from the statement of one who denies creation from them? And says: They are originated without being created, or says: They are eternal, pre-eternal, not from matter at all.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' claims about the intellect ('aql) and angels (mala'ika), including a side critique of al-Ghazali's distinction between the world of creation and the world of command. The author begins by analyzing the hadith 'The first thing God created was the intellect,' clarifying that its correct interpretation is that God addressed the intellect at the first moment of its creation, not that it was the first created thing. Evidence is provided from the hadith that God said 'I have not created a creation more honored to Me than you,' which implies that other creations existed before the intellect. Additionally, the author points out that the hadith explicitly states the intellect is created. Then the author criticizes al-Ghazali's distinction between 'alam al-khalq (world of creation, bodies) and 'alam al-amr (world of command, souls and intellects), stating that this is not from the religion of Muslims; rather, everything other than God is created. Finally, the passage refutes the philosophers' identification of their intellects with the angels. Citing the hadith from Sahih Muslim that angels were created from light, and jinn from fire, and Adam from clay, the author argues that angels are created from matter, contradicting the philosophers' claim that intellects are uncreated or eternal. The refutation uses the hadith itself as a proof.

Three Common Meanings of Tarkib
Author enumerates three common meanings of composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three common meanings of 'tarkib' (composition) and explain why none apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Composition has three established meanings; rational people apply none to Allah.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' use of 'composition' for a being with multiple attributes is an innovation. To show this, he first clarifies the three meanings that rational people actually use for 'tarkib'. Understanding these will help you see why the logicians' technical definition is problematic from the perspective of language and common sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning
Find where Ibn Taymiyya defines composition as parts that were separate joining together, like oxymel or a house. Note the examples: medicines, foods, clothes, dwellings.
This is the broadest, most common sense.
2
Identify the second meaning
Find the meaning where one part is mounted on another without mixing, like a door in its frame or a nail in wood. Note the Quranic verse 82:8 used as evidence.
This meaning is more specific, involving attachment without blending.
3
Identify the third meaning
Find the meaning where parts can separate, like human limbs and humors. Note that although created from a drop, limbs can later decompose.
This meaning implies potential separability.
4
Check divine transcendence
For each meaning, Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that no rational person would say Allah is composite in that sense. Confirm this for each.
This prepares the ground for rejecting the logicians' fourth meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما فروا منه من «التركيب» قد تكلمنا عليه في غير هذا الموضع، وبينا أن لفظ «التركيب» مجمل یراد به ترکیب الجسم من أجزاء كانت متفرقة فاجتمعت، كتركب السَّكَنْجَبِين وغيره من الأدوية، بل ومن الأطعمة والأشربة، والملابس، والمساكن، من أجزائها التي كانت متفرقة فألف بينها ورُكب بعضها مع بعض حتى صارت على الحال المركبة.

وقد يراد بـ «المركب» ما لا يمتزج فيه أحد الإثنين بالآخر، كما يقال: ركب الباب في موضعه، وركب المسمار في الباب، وهذا التركيب أخص من الأول، وهو المشهور من الكلام وقد قال تعالى: ﴿ فِي أَيِّ صُورَةٍ مَا شَاءَ رَكَبَكَ ﴾ [سورة الانفطار: ۸]. ومعلوم أن عاقلاً لا يقول إن الله تعالى مركب بهذا المعنى الأول، ولا بالثاني.

وقد يقال : «المركب» على ما يمكن مفارقة بعض أجزائه لبعض، كأخلاط الإنسان وأعضائه فإنها، وإن لم يُعقل أنها كانت مفترقة فاجتمعت بل خلقه الله من نطفة، ثم من علقة، ثم من مضغة. ولكن يمكن تفريق بعض أعضائه عن بعض، ويُعقل أيضاً أنه إذا فارق بعض أجزائه عن بعض مات واستحال، فصار بعضه تراباً وبعضه هواء، فتفرقت أعضاؤه وأخلاطه، وكذلك سائر الحيوان والنبات. ومعلوم أن عاقلاً لا يقول إن الله مركب بهذا الاعتبار.

وأما تسمية الواحد الموصوف بصفاته مركباً كتسمية الحي العالم القادر، لا يسمى الموصوف بالحياة والعلم والقدرة مركباً، فهذا اصطلاح لهم؛ لا يعرف في شيء من الشرائع ولا اللغات ولا عقول جماهير العقلاء جعل هذا تركيباً ولا تسميته بصفاته مركباً.

فإذا قالوا: نحن نسميه تركيباً لأن فيه إثبات معان متعددة لذات واحدة، ونحن نسمى ذلك «تركيباً»، ونقيم الدليل العقلي على امتناعه قيل: إذا كان الأمر كذلك فالنظر في المعاني المعقولة لا في الألفاظ السمعية، ونحن لا نوافقكم على جعل الإنسان مركباً من الحيوانية والناطقية، ولا أن في الوجود شيئاً مركباً من أجزاء عقلية. بل «المركب» من الأجزاء العقلية إنما يكون في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان. وكل ما في الوجود من «المركبات» فإنما هو «مركب» من أجزاء حسّية، موجودة في الخارج. والناس قد تنازعوا في «الجسم»، هل هو «مركب» من أجزاء حسّية، والصحيح أن الجسم لا تركيب فيه.
And what they fled from regarding 'composition' we have discussed elsewhere, and we clarified that the word 'composition' is ambiguous, used to mean the composition of a body from parts that were separate and then gathered, like the composition of oxymel and other medicines, and also foods, drinks, clothes, and dwellings, from their parts that were separate then brought together and mounted some with others until they become in a composite state.

And 'the composite' may be intended to mean that in which one of the two does not mix with the other, as is said: 'he mounted the door in its place, and mounted the nail in the door,' and this composition is more specific than the first, and it is the common usage, and Allah Almighty said: 'in whatever form He willed, He composed you' [Quran 82:8]. And it is known that a rational person does not say that Allah is composite in this first sense nor in the second.

And 'the composite' may be said of that from which some of its parts can separate, like the humors and limbs of a human; for although it is not intelligible that they were separate and then gathered, rather Allah created him from a drop, then from a clot, then from a lump, yet it is possible for some of his limbs to separate from others, and it is also intelligible that if some of his parts depart from others, he dies and transforms, so some of him becomes dust and some air, so his limbs and humors separate, and likewise the rest of animals and plants. And it is known that a rational person does not say that Allah is composite in this respect.

As for naming the one described by His attributes 'composite,' like naming the living, knowing, powerful—the one described by life, knowledge, and power is not called 'composite'—this is a convention of theirs; it is not known in any religious law, language, or the minds of the majority of rational people to make this 'composition' or to name Him 'composite' due to His attributes.

And if they say: We call it 'composition' because it involves affirming multiple meanings for one essence, and we call that 'composition,' and we establish a rational proof of its impossibility, it is said: If that is the case, then consideration is of intelligible meanings, not of textual words, and we do not agree with you in making the human 'composite' from animality and rationality, nor that there is anything in existence 'composite' from intellectual parts. Rather, 'the composite' from intellectual parts is only in the mind, not in external entities. And everything in existence that is 'composite' is only 'composite' from sensible parts that exist externally. And people have disputed about 'the body': is it 'composite' from sensible parts? And the correct view is that the body has no composition.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the term 'tarkib' (composition) and a critique of the logicians' technical usage. Ibn Taymiyya first notes that the word is ambiguous (mujmal) and lists three established meanings: (1) composition of parts that were separate and then joined, like medicine; (2) composition without mixing, like mounting a door or a nail; (3) composition where parts can separate, like human limbs. He shows that rational people do not apply any of these to Allah. He then addresses the logicians' fourth meaning: calling a single entity described by multiple attributes 'composite.' He brands this as an innovation, unknown in religious law, language, or sound reason. Finally, he responds to the logicians' justification that they call it composition because of multiple intelligible attributes. Ibn Taymiyya counters that such composition is only mental, not external, and that real composition in existence involves sensible parts. He ends with a note on the debate about whether the body (jism) is composite, stating that the correct view is that the body has no composition (in the sense of intellectual parts).

The Logicians' Innovation: Attribute-Based Composition
Author refutes the logicians' technical meaning of composition as an innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' fourth meaning of 'tarkib' and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The logicians' composition from attributes is only mental, not real.

After clarifying the three common meanings, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the logicians' technical usage: calling a single entity described by multiple attributes 'composite'. He brands this an innovation unknown in revelation, language, or sound reason. In this lesson, you will dissect his critique and see how he responds to the logicians' justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians call a subject with multiple attributes 'composite' because it involves 'affirming multiple meanings for one essence'.
This is the fourth meaning, not accepted by common people.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's rebuttal
Locate his response: 'consideration is of intelligible meanings, not of textual words' and that composition from intellectual parts is only in the mind, not externally.
He shifts from linguistic convention to epistemological reality.
3
Distinguish mental vs. external composition
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that everything composite in existence is from sensible (physical) parts, not from intellectual parts like genus and species.
This distinction is key to his critique.
4
Assess the critique's force
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya successfully shows that the logicians equivocate between two senses of 'composition'. Does his own reasoning avoid the same equivocation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما فروا منه من «التركيب» قد تكلمنا عليه في غير هذا الموضع، وبينا أن لفظ «التركيب» مجمل یراد به ترکیب الجسم من أجزاء كانت متفرقة فاجتمعت، كتركب السَّكَنْجَبِين وغيره من الأدوية، بل ومن الأطعمة والأشربة، والملابس، والمساكن، من أجزائها التي كانت متفرقة فألف بينها ورُكب بعضها مع بعض حتى صارت على الحال المركبة.

وقد يراد بـ «المركب» ما لا يمتزج فيه أحد الإثنين بالآخر، كما يقال: ركب الباب في موضعه، وركب المسمار في الباب، وهذا التركيب أخص من الأول، وهو المشهور من الكلام وقد قال تعالى: ﴿ فِي أَيِّ صُورَةٍ مَا شَاءَ رَكَبَكَ ﴾ [سورة الانفطار: ۸]. ومعلوم أن عاقلاً لا يقول إن الله تعالى مركب بهذا المعنى الأول، ولا بالثاني.

وقد يقال : «المركب» على ما يمكن مفارقة بعض أجزائه لبعض، كأخلاط الإنسان وأعضائه فإنها، وإن لم يُعقل أنها كانت مفترقة فاجتمعت بل خلقه الله من نطفة، ثم من علقة، ثم من مضغة. ولكن يمكن تفريق بعض أعضائه عن بعض، ويُعقل أيضاً أنه إذا فارق بعض أجزائه عن بعض مات واستحال، فصار بعضه تراباً وبعضه هواء، فتفرقت أعضاؤه وأخلاطه، وكذلك سائر الحيوان والنبات. ومعلوم أن عاقلاً لا يقول إن الله مركب بهذا الاعتبار.

وأما تسمية الواحد الموصوف بصفاته مركباً كتسمية الحي العالم القادر، لا يسمى الموصوف بالحياة والعلم والقدرة مركباً، فهذا اصطلاح لهم؛ لا يعرف في شيء من الشرائع ولا اللغات ولا عقول جماهير العقلاء جعل هذا تركيباً ولا تسميته بصفاته مركباً.

فإذا قالوا: نحن نسميه تركيباً لأن فيه إثبات معان متعددة لذات واحدة، ونحن نسمى ذلك «تركيباً»، ونقيم الدليل العقلي على امتناعه قيل: إذا كان الأمر كذلك فالنظر في المعاني المعقولة لا في الألفاظ السمعية، ونحن لا نوافقكم على جعل الإنسان مركباً من الحيوانية والناطقية، ولا أن في الوجود شيئاً مركباً من أجزاء عقلية. بل «المركب» من الأجزاء العقلية إنما يكون في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان. وكل ما في الوجود من «المركبات» فإنما هو «مركب» من أجزاء حسّية، موجودة في الخارج. والناس قد تنازعوا في «الجسم»، هل هو «مركب» من أجزاء حسّية، والصحيح أن الجسم لا تركيب فيه.
And what they fled from regarding 'composition' we have discussed elsewhere, and we clarified that the word 'composition' is ambiguous, used to mean the composition of a body from parts that were separate and then gathered, like the composition of oxymel and other medicines, and also foods, drinks, clothes, and dwellings, from their parts that were separate then brought together and mounted some with others until they become in a composite state.

And 'the composite' may be intended to mean that in which one of the two does not mix with the other, as is said: 'he mounted the door in its place, and mounted the nail in the door,' and this composition is more specific than the first, and it is the common usage, and Allah Almighty said: 'in whatever form He willed, He composed you' [Quran 82:8]. And it is known that a rational person does not say that Allah is composite in this first sense nor in the second.

And 'the composite' may be said of that from which some of its parts can separate, like the humors and limbs of a human; for although it is not intelligible that they were separate and then gathered, rather Allah created him from a drop, then from a clot, then from a lump, yet it is possible for some of his limbs to separate from others, and it is also intelligible that if some of his parts depart from others, he dies and transforms, so some of him becomes dust and some air, so his limbs and humors separate, and likewise the rest of animals and plants. And it is known that a rational person does not say that Allah is composite in this respect.

As for naming the one described by His attributes 'composite,' like naming the living, knowing, powerful—the one described by life, knowledge, and power is not called 'composite'—this is a convention of theirs; it is not known in any religious law, language, or the minds of the majority of rational people to make this 'composition' or to name Him 'composite' due to His attributes.

And if they say: We call it 'composition' because it involves affirming multiple meanings for one essence, and we call that 'composition,' and we establish a rational proof of its impossibility, it is said: If that is the case, then consideration is of intelligible meanings, not of textual words, and we do not agree with you in making the human 'composite' from animality and rationality, nor that there is anything in existence 'composite' from intellectual parts. Rather, 'the composite' from intellectual parts is only in the mind, not in external entities. And everything in existence that is 'composite' is only 'composite' from sensible parts that exist externally. And people have disputed about 'the body': is it 'composite' from sensible parts? And the correct view is that the body has no composition.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the term 'tarkib' (composition) and a critique of the logicians' technical usage. Ibn Taymiyya first notes that the word is ambiguous (mujmal) and lists three established meanings: (1) composition of parts that were separate and then joined, like medicine; (2) composition without mixing, like mounting a door or a nail; (3) composition where parts can separate, like human limbs. He shows that rational people do not apply any of these to Allah. He then addresses the logicians' fourth meaning: calling a single entity described by multiple attributes 'composite.' He brands this as an innovation, unknown in religious law, language, or sound reason. Finally, he responds to the logicians' justification that they call it composition because of multiple intelligible attributes. Ibn Taymiyya counters that such composition is only mental, not external, and that real composition in existence involves sensible parts. He ends with a note on the debate about whether the body (jism) is composite, stating that the correct view is that the body has no composition (in the sense of intellectual parts).

Three Meanings of 'Compound' and Their Inapplicability to Allah
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates three common meanings of 'compound' and argues none apply to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three common meanings of 'compound' (المركب) as presented by Ibn Taymiyya and explain why each is inapplicable to Allah.

Core Idea
The term 'compound' is ambiguous; none of its worldly meanings can apply to Allah.

Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' use of the term 'compound' by showing that it has multiple common-language meanings. He enumerates three distinct senses and demonstrates that each one, when properly understood, cannot be attributed to Allah. This lesson unpacks his conceptual division (taqsim) and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
Read the Arabic passage and locate the three distinct meanings of 'compound' that Ibn Taymiyya lists: (1) a state without mixing, (2) two things joined without mixing (like a nail in a door), (3) parts that can separate (like human limbs and humors).
The first meaning is implied but not fully elaborated; focus on the second and third.
2
Extract the examples given
For the second meaning, note the example of composing a door and a nail. For the third, consider the human body's parts that can separate at death. The Quranic verse 'In whatever form He willed, He composed you' is cited as a linguistic precedent.
3
Analyze why each meaning fails for Allah
For each meaning, explain the logical or theological reason it cannot apply to Allah. The first two involve physical juxtaposition or mixing, which are impossible for a non-physical being. The third involves separability and decay, which contradict divine perfection and eternity.
Ibn Taymiyya's argument relies on common sense ('no rational person would say') as a form of immediate refutation.
4
Connect to the broader critique of logicians
Understand that Ibn Taymiyya is setting the stage for criticizing the logicians who use 'compound' as a technical term (e.g., composite of genus and differentia). By showing the term's ambiguity and inapplicability to Allah, he undermines their claims about conceptual composition.
This passage is part of a larger argument against attributing any form of composition to the divine essence.

Source Text

المقام الثالث - كون لفظ التركيب مجملاً يطلق على معان على الحال المركبة غير امتزاج وقد يراد بـ «المركب» ما لا يمتزج فيه أحد الإثنين بالآخر، كما يقال: ركب التركيب من الباب في موضعه، و ركب المسمار في الباب»، وهذا التركيب» أخص من الأول، وهو المشهور من الكلام وقد قال تعالى : ﴿وفي أَيِّ صُورَةٍ مَا شَاءَ رَكَبَكَ﴾ [سورة الانفطار: ۸] . ومعلوم أن عاقلاً لا يقول إن الله تعالى مركب» بهذا المعنى الأول، ولا بالثاني . وقد يقال : «المركب على ما يمكن مفارقة بعض أجزائه لبعض، كأخلاط الإنسان وأعضائه فإنها، وإن لم يُعقل أنها كانت مفترقة فاجتمعت بل خلقه الله من نطفة، ثم يمكن تفريق علقة، ثم من مُضْغَة. ولكن يمكن تفريقُ بعض أعضائه عن بعض، ويُعقل أيضاً أنه إذا بعض أجزائه عن بعض مات استحال، فصار بعضه تراباً وبعضُه هواء، فتفرقت أعضاؤه وأخلاطه، وكذلك سائر الحيوان والنبات. ومعلوم أن عاقلاً لا يقول إن الله مركب بهذا الاعتبار.
The third station - that the word 'composition' is ambiguous, applied to meanings upon the compound state without mixing. And 'compound' may be intended as that in which one of the two does not mix with the other, as is said: 'he composed the door in its place, and he composed the nail in the door.' This 'composition' is more specific than the first, and it is the well-known usage. And Allah Almighty said: 'In whatever form He willed, He composed you' [Surah al-Infitar: 8]. And it is known that no rational person says that Allah is 'compound' in this first sense, nor in the second. And it may be said: 'compound' is that whose parts can separate from each other, like the humors and limbs of a human. For they, even if it is not understood that they were separate and then gathered, rather Allah created him from a drop, then it is possible to separate a clot, then a lump of flesh. But it is possible to separate some of his limbs from others, and it is also understood that when some of his parts separate from others, he dies and decays, so some of him becomes dust and some air, and his limbs and humors become separated. And likewise for other animals and plants. And it is known that no rational person says that Allah is 'compound' in this consideration.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya in which he distinguishes multiple meanings of the term 'compound' (المركب) and demonstrates that none of them can be applied to Allah, thereby refuting those who might attribute composition to God. He begins by stating that the word 'composition' is ambiguous (مجملاً) and can refer to different concepts. The first meaning (implicitly mentioned as 'the compound state without mixing') is not fully detailed here, but the second meaning is that of two things placed together without one mixing into the other, like a door and its nail. Ibn Taymiyya notes that this second meaning is more specific than the first and is common in speech, citing the Quranic verse 'In whatever form He willed, He composed you' (82:8). He then asserts that no rational person would claim Allah is compound in either the first or second sense. The third meaning is that of a composite whose parts can separate from one another, such as the humors and limbs of a living being. Even though a human's parts were never separate before creation, they can separate after death. Ibn Taymiyya argues that it is equally absurd to say Allah is compound in this sense. The passage thus functions as a conceptual clarification: by showing that 'compound' has multiple meanings, Ibn Taymiyya can systematically rule out each one when applied to Allah. The underlying logical operation is a division (taqsim) of the term, followed by a refutation of each possible application. This prepares the ground for his critique of the logicians who use 'compound' in a purely conceptual or technical sense.

Key Terms
المركب - al-murakkab: compound; in this passage, it has three senses: (1) a state without mixing, (2) two things joined without mixing, (3) parts that can separate.
تركيب - tarkīb: composition; the act or state of being composite; here presented as an ambiguous term.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Claim that Co-extension and Division Yield Only Probability
Ibn Taymiyya refutes that co-extension and division only give probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which division (taqsim) yields certainty according to Ibn Taymiyya and explain why the logicians' claim is self-defeating.

Core Idea
Division can yield certainty if exhaustive; the logicians' objection backfires.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on a key claim of the logicians: that the methods of co-extension (dawaran) and division (taqsim) only produce probability, not certainty. He argues that this claim is false and that it would also apply to categorical syllogism, which the logicians accept as certain. He focuses on division, showing that if the division is exhaustive (hasir) and all alternatives except one are invalidated, certainty is achieved. The logicians themselves concede this point, so their overall objection is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states what the logicians say about dawaran and taqsim. They claim that analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) is only established by these methods and that both yield only probability (zann).
This is the target of his refutation.
2
Note Ibn Taymiyya's initial counter
Observe how Ibn Taymiyya immediately labels their claim as false (bāṭil) and warns that the same objection would apply to categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumūl), which the logicians consider certain.
This is a tu quoque (you too) argument, showing inconsistency.
3
Focus on the condition for certainty in division
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the logicians themselves concede that division (taqsim) yields certainty if it is exhaustive (ḥāṣir). Locate this condition in the Arabic.
The key term is 'ḥāṣir' (encompassing/exhaustive).
4
Analyze why exhaustive division yields certainty
If the common attribute is divided into all possible categories and all but one cause are invalidated, the remaining cause must be the true 'illa. This process eliminates alternatives, leaving certainty.
This is the core logical mechanism.
5
Recognize the rhetorical strategy
Ibn Taymiyya uses the logicians' own admission against them. They accept that exhaustive division yields certainty, so their blanket claim that taqsim only yields probability is false. The passage trails off, but the argument is clear.
The incomplete ending suggests an analogy with dawaran, which may also yield certainty under similar conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for 'division', they concede that it yields certainty if it is exhaustive. And if that is the case, then it is possible to enumerate the common attribute into divisions that are not exceeded and to invalidate the causation by all of them except one. And if that is not possible, then that common attribute cannot be made a middle term, so it does not yield certainty. And if used...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the methods of co-extension (dawaran) and division (taqsim) only yield probability. Ibn Taymiyya first states that this claim is false, and that the same criticism would apply to categorical syllogism. He then focuses on division: logicians themselves admit that division yields certainty if it is exhaustive (hasir). If the divisions are exhaustive and all but one cause are invalidated, the remaining cause is certain. If not exhaustive, it does not yield certainty, but that is a matter of the method's application, not its inherent weakness. The passage appears incomplete, but the key point is that division can be certain; co-extension is analogous.

Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that division (taqsim) only yields probability by showing that exhaustive division yields certainty, a point the logicians themselves concede.

Two Benefits of Ta'lil with 'Illa Qasira
Ibn Taymiyya explains the two benefits of using a restrictive cause in legal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two benefits of using a restrictive cause ('illa qasira) in Islamic legal reasoning and distinguish its permissibility according to different madhahib.

Core Idea
Restrictive causes preserve textual authority while revealing divine wisdom.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies a key usul al-fiqh concept: the 'illa qasira. While some jurists restrict ta'lil to transitive causes, Ibn Taymiyya defends the use of restrictive causes, showing they serve two vital purposes. Let's explore what those are and why they matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of causes
Distinguish between 'illa muta'addiya (transitive) and 'illa qasira (restrictive). The former extends the ruling to other cases; the latter confines it to the original text.
A transitive cause allows qiyas; a restrictive cause prevents it.
2
Note the scholarly consensus and dispute
Explicit restrictive causes (mansusa) are unanimously accepted. Derived (mustanbata) restrictive causes are disputed; Ibn Taymiyya sides with the majority (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) who permit them.
The Hanafi school and some others prohibit derived restrictive causes.
3
Identify the first benefit: restricting the ruling
The first benefit is confining the ruling to the scope of the text, preventing unwarranted analogical extension (ilhaq). This ensures rulings are not applied beyond their intended domain.
Example: carrion prohibition explained with a cause that excludes slaughtered meat.
4
Identify the second benefit: understanding wisdom
The second benefit is knowledge of the wisdom (hikma) and interests behind the legislation, which increases faith, aids obedience, and refutes heretical doubts about the Shari'a being arbitrary.
The Shari'a is legislated for wisdom and mercy, not mere subjugation.
5
Apply the concept to examples
Note the examples given: carrion, blood, hady/adha, and wine. In each case, a restrictive cause prevents the inclusion of what is not intended.
This illustrates how the first benefit operates in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدتان في تعليل الحكم بـ «علة قاصرة»
وهذا كما يقول الفقهاء: إن الحكم يعلَّل تارة بـ علة متعدّية» وتارة بـ «علة الخلاف في جواز التعليل
قاصرة». والتعليل بـ «القاصرة» إذا كانت منصوصةٌ جائز باتفاق الفقهاء. وإنما تنازعوا بعلل قاصرة فيما إذا كانت مستنبطة»، والأكثرون على جواز ذلك، وهو الصحيح، وهو مذهب مالك والشافعي وأحمد بن حنبل في المنصوص عنه، فإنه دائماً يعلل بـ «العلل القاصرة». وهو اختيار أبي الخَطَّاب (۳) وغيره من
أصحابه .
ولكن القاضي أبو يعلى وطائفة وافقوا أصحاب أبي حنيفة في منعهم التعليل بـ «القاصرة»؛ وهذا من كلام متأخريهم. وسبب ذلك النزاع في مسئلة تعليل الربا في الذهب والفضة وأمثالهما ، هل العلة فيه «متعدية» أو «قاصرة». وأما أبو حنيفة نفسه وصاحباه لم ينقل عنهم في ذلك شيء، والذي يليق بعقلهم وفضلهم أنهم لا يمنعون ذلك مطلقاً، كما لا يمتنع في المنصوصة).
وفي ذلك فائدتان : قصر الحكم على مورد النص ومنع الإلحاق، لئلا يظن الظان
أن الحكم يثبت فيما سوى مورد النص . كما يعلَّل تحريم الميتة بعلة تمنع دخول المذكى
فيها ؛ ويعلل تحريم الدم بعلة تمنع دخول العَرَق والرّيق وغيرهما فيها ؛ ويعلل اختصاص الهدي والأضحية بالأنعام بعلة تمنع دخول غيرها فيها ؛ ويعلل وجوب الحدّ في الخمر بعلة تمنع دخول الدم والميتة فيها ؛ وأمثال ذلك كثيرة. بل مَنْ يقول إن جميع الأحكام تثبت بالنص، يقول : إنها معللة بـ علل قاصرة، لكن إنما يعلل بـ «العلة المتعدية» نص يتناول بعض أنواع الحكم فيعلل ذلك الحكم بعلة تتعدى إلى سائر النوع الذي دل على ثبوت الحكم فيه نص آخر .
والفائدة الثانية : معرفة حكمة التشريع
وما اشتمل عليه من مصالح العباد في المعاش والمعاد. فإن ذلك مما يزيد به الإيمان والعلم، ويكون أعونَ على التصديق والطاعة، وأقطعَ لشُبه أهلِ الإلحاد والشَّناعة، وأنصرَ لقول من يقول : إن الشرع جميعه إنما شُرع
لحكمة ورحمة، لم يشرع لا لحكمة ورحمة بل لمجرد قهر التعبد ومحض المشيئة .
وإذا كانت الأوصافُ في الأصل قد تكون مختصة بذات الأصل، وقد تكون بينه وبين غيره خارجة عنه لم يلزم - إذا كان هذا الأصل ثبت الحكم فيه لخارج مشترك - أن يكون الحكم في كل أصل لخارج مشترك .
Two benefits in explaining the ruling with a restrictive cause: This is as the jurists say: The ruling is sometimes explained with a transitive cause and sometimes with a restrictive cause. Explaining with a restrictive cause if it is explicitly stated is permissible by consensus of the jurists. They only disputed regarding restrictive causes when they are derived. The majority permit that, and it is correct. It is the madhhab of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal in the explicit narration, for he always explains with restrictive causes. It is the choice of Abu al-Khattab and others of his companions. But al-Qadi Abu Ya'la and a group agreed with the followers of Abu Hanifa in their prohibition of explaining with restrictive causes; this is from the speech of their later scholars. The reason for this dispute is the issue of explaining riba in gold and silver and their likes, whether the cause is transitive or restrictive. As for Abu Hanifa himself and his two companions, nothing is transmitted from them about that, and what befits their intellect and virtue is that they do not prohibit that absolutely, just as it is not prohibited in the explicit case. And in that are two benefits: Restricting the ruling to the scope of the text and preventing extension, so that one may not suppose that the ruling applies beyond the text. For example, the prohibition of carrion is explained with a cause that prevents the inclusion of legally slaughtered meat; the prohibition of blood is explained with a cause that prevents the inclusion of sweat, saliva, and others; the restriction of hady and adha to livestock is explained with a cause that prevents the inclusion of other animals; the obligation of the hadd for wine is explained with a cause that prevents the inclusion of blood and carrion; and many similar examples. Rather, one who says that all rulings are established by scripture says that they are explained with restrictive causes. However, the transitive cause is only used when a text addresses some types of a ruling, and that ruling is explained with a cause that extends to the rest of the type for which another text indicates the ruling. The second benefit: Knowledge of the wisdom of legislation and what it contains of the interests of the servants in livelihood and the afterlife. For that increases faith and knowledge, assists in belief and obedience, cuts off the doubts of the people of heresy and villainy, and supports the statement of those who say that all of the Shari'a was legislated for wisdom and mercy, not for mere subjugation and pure will. And if the descriptions in the original can be specific to the essence of the original or shared between it and others, it does not follow—if the ruling in this original is established for a common external—that the ruling in every original is for a common external.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the topic of ta'lil (explaining rulings with causes), specifically the distinction between transitive causes ('illa muta'addiya) and restrictive causes ('illa qasira). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that there are two benefits in using a restrictive cause. He then explains the jurists' view: a ruling can be explained with either a transitive or a restrictive cause. Explaining with a restrictive cause that is explicitly stated is permissible by consensus. The dispute arises when the restrictive cause is derived by deduction (mustanbata), but the majority permit it, and this is the correct view. He notes that Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal (in the explicit narration) always use restrictive causes. Abu al-Khattab and others also choose this. However, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la and some others agree with the Hanafis who prohibit restrictive causes, but this is from later scholars. The reason for their dispute is the riba analogy in gold and silver. Ibn Taymiyya then lists the two benefits. The first benefit is restricting the ruling to the scope of the text and preventing analogical extension (ilhaq), so that one does not mistakenly apply the ruling beyond its intended context. He gives examples from fiqh: the prohibition of carrion is explained with a cause that excludes slaughtered meat; the prohibition of blood excludes sweat and saliva; the restriction of hady and adha to livestock excludes other animals; the hadd for wine excludes blood and carrion. He also notes that those who say all rulings are from scripture explain them all with restrictive causes, and the transitive cause is only used when a text covers some types and another text covers the rest. The second benefit is understanding the wisdom (hikma) of the Shari'a and its interests for people, which increases faith, helps obedience, and refutes heretical doubts. It supports the view that the Shari'a was legislated for wisdom and mercy, not for mere subjugation. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya reiterates that descriptions can be specific or common, and it does not follow that every ruling is for a common external attribute.

Key Terms
علة قاصرة ('illa qasira): A cause that is specific to the text's scope and does not extend to other cases; used to confine the ruling to the original case. [Note: No significant shift.]
علة متعدية ('illa muta'addiya): A cause that extends beyond the original case to other similar cases, allowing analogical extension. [Note: No significant shift.]
منصوصة (mansusa): An explicitly stated cause from the text (Quran or Sunnah), as opposed to a derived cause. [Note: No significant shift.]
مستنبطة (mustanbata): A cause derived by juristic reasoning (ijtihad) from the text, not explicitly stated. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the first benefit of using a restrictive cause ('illa qasira) in explaining a ruling?
A) To allow analogical extension to new cases.
B) To restrict the ruling to the scope of the text and prevent unlawful extension.
C) To understand the wisdom behind the legislation.
D) To enable the use of transitive causes.

Key Takeaway
The 'illa qasira serves to prevent unwarranted analogical extension while revealing the wisdom of the Shari'a.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Views on the Justifier for Vision of God
Ibn Taymiyya presents three views on the common justifier for vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three theological positions on al-musharik al-mussahih for ru'ya Allah as presented by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
The justifier for seeing God is debated; three distinct Sunni views exist.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a crucial theological disagreement among Sunni theologians regarding the common justifier (al-musharik al-mussahih) that makes the vision of God possible. This passage outlines three distinct positions: that of al-Ash'ari, that of Ibn Kullab, and a third position that uses a comprehensive division. Understanding these positions is key to grasping the debates in kalam about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept: al-musharik al-mussahih
Find the Arabic term in the passage. It means the common justifying factor for a theological claim, specifically the vision of God (ru'ya).
2
Extract the first position: al-Ash'ari's view
Al-Ash'ari and his followers (e.g., al-Qadi Abu Bakr, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la) hold that the justifier is mere existence (mujarrad al-wujud).
3
Extract the second position: Ibn Kullab's view
Ibn Kullab and others (e.g., Abu al-Hasan al-Zaghuni) hold that it is self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs).
4
Extract the third position: comprehensive division
Some theologians do not specify a single justifier but posit an unspecified common factor (qadr mushtarak) determined via al-taqsim al-hasir, which yields certainty (al-yaqin).
5
Compare the positions
Ibn Taymiyya presents the third view favorably, as he says it yields certainty and he argues: if such a complex issue can be established this way, how much more for clearer issues?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجعل المشترك المصحح هو مجرد الوجود، كما يسلكه الأَشْعَرِي، ومن اتبعه كالقاضي أبي بكر، وطائفة من أصحاب أحمد وغيرهم، كالقاضي أبي يعلى؛ بل يجعله «القيام بالنفس»، كما يسلكه ابن كلاب، وغيره من مثبتي الرؤية من أصحاب أحمد وغيرهم كأبي الحسن الزاغوني؛ أو لا يعيّن المصحح، بل يجعل قدراً مشتركاً كـ التقسيم الحاصر. وهو أيضاً يفيد اليقين كما قد بسط في موضعه. وإذا كانت مسئلة الرؤية والمصحح لها أمكن تقريره على هذا الوجه، فكيف فيما هو أوضح منها؟
And he does not make the common justifier (al-musharik al-mussahih) mere existence, as al-Ash'ari and those who followed him, like al-Qadi Abu Bakr and a group of Ahmad's companions and others, like al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, hold; rather he makes it 'self-subsistence' (al-qiyam bi al-nafs), as Ibn Kullab and others who affirm the vision from Ahmad's companions and others, like Abu al-Hasan al-Zaghuni, hold; or he does not specify the justifier, but makes it a common factor (qadr mushitarak) like the comprehensive division (al-taqsim al-hasir). And it also yields certainty (al-yaqin), as has been elaborated elsewhere. And if the issue of the vision and its justifier could be established in this manner, how about what is clearer than it?
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct theological positions on the 'mussahih' (that which validates or justifies) the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in the afterlife. The author, Ibn Taymiyya, is laying out the disagreement among Sunni theologians. The first position, attributed to al-Ash'ari and his followers, holds that the common justifier is mere existence (mujarrad al-wujud). The second, attributed to Ibn Kullab and others, holds that it is 'self-subsistence' (al-qiyam bi al-nafs), meaning that the entity must exist as a self-subsistent substance, not an accident. The third position, which the author seems to favor, does not specify a single justifier but instead posits a common factor (qadr mushtarak) that is identified through a comprehensive division (al-taqsim al-hasir), and this method yields certainty (yaqin). The passage ends with a rhetorical question: if there is such disagreement on this issue, how much more so on issues that are clearer? This highlights the complexity of theological reasoning and the need for careful method. The footnote references (not included in this clean passage) provide biographical details of the scholars mentioned. The commentary avoids secular examples; the example here is from kalam regarding the attributes of God.

Key Terms
المشترك المصحح
القيام بالنفس
التقسيم الحاصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Critique of the Claim that a Shared Attribute is Not Sufficient Cause
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the argument that a shared attribute cannot be the sole cause because other attributes may also be causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the claim that a shared attribute cannot be the sole cause and identify the logical flaw in the opponent's objection.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that if a ruling occurs with a shared attribute in a case where other attributes are absent, those other attributes cannot be the cause.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on a sophisticated objection from his opponents regarding causation. They argue that just because a ruling is found together with a shared attribute in a case where other attributes are missing, that does not prove the shared attribute is the independent cause—the ruling might be caused by some other unknown attribute. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this by showing that if the additional attributes from the original case were truly effective, the ruling could never appear without them. This reasoning is crucial for distinguishing genuine causes from incidental attributes in Islamic legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that the presence of the ruling with a shared attribute in a new case does not prove the shared attribute is the sole cause, because the ruling in the original case might have been caused by the other attributes present there.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
Ibn Taymiyya responds that this is an error. He points out that in the new case, the ruling is present while the additional attributes from the original are absent. This proves those additional attributes are not necessary for the ruling.
3
Trace the logical reasoning
If the additional attributes were effective in producing the ruling, then the ruling would be confined to cases where those attributes exist. Since the ruling occurs without them (in the new case), they cannot be the cause. This is a modus tollens argument: if P (additional attributes cause ruling), then Q (ruling only with attributes); not Q; therefore not P.
This reasoning is standard in usul al-fiqh for testing the 'illa.
4
Understand the demand for a decisive proof
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that the opponent might suggest another unknown cause in the new case. He responds that this possibility must be negated by a decisive proof or experiment; otherwise, it is merely a hypothetical objection that undermines all causal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم: ثبوت الحكم مع المشترك في صورة مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف جواب قولهم المقارنة له في الأصل مما لا يوجب استقلاله بالتعليل، لجواز أن يكون الحكم في تلك معللاً بعلة أخرى، فيقال: هذا غلط وذلك أنه متى ثبت الحكم مع المشترك في صورة مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف، يمتنع أن تكون الأوصاف الزائدة المقارنة له في الأصل مؤثرة في الحكم مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف. فإنها مختصة بالأصل، فلو كانت مؤثرة لم يجز أن يوجد الحكم في غير الأصل. وحينئذ فعدمها لا يؤثر. والصورة الأخرى قد ثبت فيها وجود المشترك. وأما إمكان وجود وصف آخر مستقل بعلة فهذا لا بد من نفيه إما بدليل قاطع أو
As for their statement: 'The affirmation of the ruling with the shared attribute in a case while other attributes are absent is a response to their statement that the concurrent attributes in the original do not necessitate its independence in causation, because it is possible that the ruling in that case is caused by another cause,' it is said: This is an error. For when the ruling is affirmed with the shared attribute in a case with the absence of other attributes, it is impossible for the additional attributes concurrent with it in the original to be effective in the ruling when other attributes are absent. For they are specific to the original; if they were effective, the ruling would not occur in other than the original. Hence their absence does not affect. And the other case has the shared attribute present. As for the possibility of another attribute independent as a cause, this must be negated either by a decisive proof or
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage critiques a logical principle used by opponents on the topic of causation. The opponent claims: if a ruling (hukm) is found together with a shared attribute (mushtarak) in a case where other attributes are missing, that does not prove that the shared attribute is an independent cause, because the ruling in the original case might have been caused by those other attributes. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this: once the ruling is established with the shared attribute in another case where the additional attributes of the original are absent, those additional attributes cannot be effective in the ruling, because they are specific to the original. If they were effective, the ruling would be confined to the original and not appear elsewhere. Since the ruling does appear elsewhere with only the shared attribute, the shared attribute is sufficient. The opponent might then suggest that some other unknown cause exists in that other case. Ibn Taymiyya replies that such a possibility must be negated by a proof or experiment. The argument draws on fiqh methodology: for example, in determining the 'illa of wine's prohibition, if intoxication is found in both wine and nabidh, then any additional properties of wine (e.g., colour, sweetness) cannot be the sole cause.

Key Terms
حكم
علة
مشترك
أصل
صورة

Key Takeaway
